Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
5 min
Bahr Ulum's Critique of al-Dawani on Badahah
Bahr Ulum critiques al-Dawani's appeal to badahah as context-dependent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in al-Dawani's argument that appealing to badahah avoids circularity, and articulate Bahr Ulum's key objection that self-evidence is context-dependent.

Core Idea
Self-evidence is not a universal escape hatch; it depends on perspective and discipline.

In the Islamic logical tradition, the division of conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) into self-evident (badihi) and speculative (nazari) is foundational. Al-Dawani, commenting on al-Sa'd al-Taftazani, argued that appealing to self-evidence (badahah) is a safer and more elegant way to establish this division than constructing logical proofs that inevitably reduce to claiming badahah anyway. However, the Indian scholar Bahr Ulum did not accept this uncritically. He identified a crucial oversight: self-evidence is not an absolute property but varies according to perspective (bi-ikhtilaf al-'unwan). This lesson unpacks Bahr Ulum's critique and its implications for logical methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Dawani's claim about badahah
Find the passage where al-Dawani argues that referring to self-evidence is 'safer' than burdening oneself with proofs. He claims that any proof for the division would require claiming self-evidence in its premises, thus reducing to a mere assertion of the conclusion.
Al-Dawani's argument is a form of meta-logical critique: he thinks proofs for the division are circular or redundant.
2
Recognize al-Dawani's reasoning
Al-Dawani says that even the proof based on 'if all were speculative, circularity or infinite regress would result' relies on claiming self-evidence in its premises (e.g., the impossibility of circularity). Therefore, the proof is not independent of badahah.
3
Locate Bahr Ulum's objection
Bahr Ulum states that 'self-evidence and speculativeness differ according to the perspective' (bi-ikhtilaf al-'unwan). This means what is self-evident in one context (e.g., geometry) may be speculative in another (e.g., metaphysics).
This is the core of the critique: al-Dawani assumed a fixed notion of badahah.
4
Evaluate the strength of each position
Consider whether Bahr Ulum's objection completely refutes al-Dawani or only shows a limitation. Al-Dawani's point that proofs ultimately rely on some claimed badahah is still valid, but Bahr Ulum forces us to be more careful about what counts as self-evident and to whom.
5
Apply this insight to other Islamic scholarly debates
Think of a claim in Kalam or Usul al-Fiqh that is often presented as self-evident. Could its self-evidence be dependent on the context or the audience? For example, the statement 'The world is temporally originated' might be self-evident to a mutakallim but not to a layperson.
This tool helps avoid naive appeals to self-evidence without justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل على عبقريته العلمية أيضا مواضع كثيرة من كتبه، خاصة كتابه هذا، فانظر مثلا انتقاده لكلام المحقق الدواني في معرض تقسيم كل من التصور والتصديق إلى البديهي والنظري، وذلك عند قول المصنف: (وَلَيْسَ الكُل مِنْ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا بَدِيهِيًّا ، وَإِلَّا فَأَنْتَ مُسْتَغْنِ، وَلَا نَظْرِيًّا، مُتَوَقِّفًا عَلَى النَّظْرِ، وَإلَّا لَدَارَ .... أَوْ تَسَلْسَلَ. وَلا يُعْلَمُ التَّصَوُّرُ مِنَ التَّصْدِيقِ وَبِالْعَكْسِ ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمُعَرِّفَ مَقُولٌ ، وَالتَّصَوُّرَ مُتَسَاوِي النِّسْبَةِ، فَبَعْضُ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بَدِيهِيٌّ، وَبَعْضُهُ نَظْرِيٌّ. هذا هو كلام المصنف رَحِمَهُ اللهُ، كعادة أهل هذا الفن في توضيح المطلب، وخالفها السعد في التهذيب؛ حيث قال فيه: «ويقتسمان بالضرورة الضرورة والاكتساب بالنظر فجاء المحقق الدواني لشرح كلام السعد، واستحسن صنيعه؛ حيث قال: وهذا الطريق، أعني الإحالة إلى البداهة أسلم من تكلف الاستدلال عليه، بأنه لو كان الكلُّ من كل منهما نظريا لدار أو تسلسل، أو بديهيا لما احتجنا في شيء منهما إلى الفكر ؛ فإنه مع ما فيه من التوقف على امتناع اكتساب التصديق من التصور، ثم على حدوث النفس على ما هو المشهور لا يتم إلا بدعوى البديهة في مقدمات الدليل وأطرافها، وذلك كافٍ في نفي كسبية الكل، فلا حاجة إلى الدليل عليه، ثم لا بد من دعوى البديهة في ثبوت الاحتياج إلى الفكر، وذلك بعينه دعوى البديهة في عدم بداهة الكل، فظهر أن الاستدلال يؤول بالآخرة إلى دعوى البديهة في المطلوب، فَلْيُكْتَفَ به أولا، فافهم ذلك ؛ فإنه مما لا تجده لغيرنا، وانظمه في سلك نظائره المنثورة في هذه الحواشي . فرأينا هنا كيف أن الدواني يفضّل طريقة السعد على طريقة غيره من المناطقة بما فيهم صاحب السلم نفسه، وكيف اعتبر كلامه هذا من النفائس التي لا توجد لغيره، إلا أن محقق الهند بحر العلوم لم يمر على كلام الدواني مرور مقلد يتلقف ما يسمعه، منبهرا بشخصية القائل، غير ملتفت إلى ما يمليه عليه عقله المستنير، فانظر ماذا قال في تعقيبه على تحقيق الدواني تجد كيف يصنع الفكر الرجال. يقول بحر العلوم في التعقيب قائلا، بعد أن نقل حاصل كلام الدواني: ولا يخفى عليك أن البديهة والنظرية يختلفان بإختلاف العنوان، فرب قضية
And his scientific brilliance is also indicated by many places in his books, especially this book of his. Look, for example, at his critique of the words of al-Dawani in the context of dividing both conception and assent into the self-evident and the speculative. That is at the statement of the author [al-Sullam]: 'Not all of each of them is self-evident, otherwise you would be independent; nor all speculative, depending on consideration, otherwise there would be circularity or infinite regress. Neither is conception known from assent nor vice versa, because the definer is a predicate, and conception is equally related. So part of each of them is self-evident and part is speculative.' This is the statement of the author, may God have mercy on him, as is the habit of the people of this art in clarifying the issue. Al-Sa'd contradicted it in al-Tahdhib, where he said: 'They are divided by necessity into the necessary and the acquired through consideration.' Then al-Dawani came to explain al-Sa'd's statement and approved his action, saying: 'This way, namely referring to self-evidence, is safer than burdening oneself with a proof for it, such as that if all of each of them were speculative, circularity or infinite regress would result, or if self-evident, we would need no thought concerning anything of them. For it, along with its dependence on the impossibility of acquiring assent from conception, and then on the origination of the soul according to the well-known view, is not completed except by claiming self-evidence in the premises of the proof and their terms. And that is sufficient to negate the acquiredness of the whole, so there is no need for a proof for it. Then there must be a claim of self-evidence in the establishment of the need for thought, and that is exactly a claim of self-evidence in the non-self-evidence of the whole. So it becomes apparent that the proof ultimately reduces to a claim of self-evidence in the desired conclusion, so let it be satisfied with it at first. Understand that, for it is something you will not find from others, and string it in the necklace of its scattered similars in these glosses.' So we see here how al-Dawani preferred al-Sa'd's way over the way of other logicians, including the author of al-Sullam himself, and how he considered this statement of his among the precious gems not found elsewhere. However, the Indian researcher Bahr Ulum did not pass over al-Dawani's statement as a blind follower snatching what he hears, amazed by the personality of the speaker, inattentive to what his enlightened mind dictated to him. So see what he said in his criticism of al-Dawani's investigation, you will see how men's intellect operates. Bahr Ulum says in the criticism, after quoting the gist of al-Dawani's statement: 'And it is not hidden from you that self-evidence and speculativeness differ according to the perspective, for perhaps a proposition...'
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents a critical refinement by Bahr Ulum against a claim of al-Dawani. Al-Dawani, in his commentary on al-Sa'd's al-Tahdhib, argued that appealing to self-evidence (badahah) is a safer and more elegant way to establish the division of conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) into self-evident and speculative parts. He contended that logical proofs for this division inevitably require claiming self-evidence in their premises, thus reducing to a mere assertion of the desired conclusion. Therefore, he saw no need for such proofs. Bahr Ulum, however, did not accept this uncritically. He pointed out that self-evidence and speculativeness are relative terms that vary depending on the perspective or context (bi-ikhtilaf al-'unwan). He thereby exposes a flaw: what is self-evident to one person may not be so to another, and al-Dawani's assumption of self-evidence in certain propositions is itself an unacknowledged claim. For example, the proposition 'the whole is greater than the part' is self-evident in geometry, but in metaphysical discourse it may require justification. Bahr Ulum's critique demonstrates that a scholar must examine arguments on their own merit, not rely on authority. The passage thus exemplifies the independent critical spirit required in Islamic logic and debate.

Key Terms
بديهية
نظري
تصور
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Bahr Ulum's main objection to al-Dawani's reliance on badahah?
A) Al-Dawani's argument is too complex.
B) Self-evidence and speculativeness vary according to perspective, making the appeal problematic.
C) The Quran forbids claiming self-evidence without proof.
D) Al-Dawani ignored the views of earlier scholars.

Key Takeaway
The appeal to self-evidence cannot be a universal shortcut because self-evidence itself is relative to the context and the subject matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another example from Islamic logic or Kalam where a scholar appeals to self-evidence. Write a short paragraph analyzing whether the claim of badahah is context-dependent, using Bahr Ulum's insight.
?
Construct a brief critique of al-Dawani's position from the perspective of an Islamic logician who prioritizes rigorous demonstration over appeals to self-evidence. Your critique should mention the relativity of badahah.

The Relativity of Intuitive vs Theoretical Premises
Author discusses how premises can be both intuitive and theoretical depending on perspective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a single premise can be considered both intuitive (badihiyya) and theoretical (nazariyya) depending on the mental perspective.

Core Idea
A premise's epistemological status depends on how it is considered, not on its inherent nature.

The author of the commentary on Sullam al-Ulum tackles a key epistemological question: are premises inherently intuitive or theoretical? He argues that the same premise can be both, depending on whether you look at it directly or under a certain description. This insight resolves the regress problem and clarifies the nature of demonstrative science.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories
Intuitive (badihiyya) premises are self-evident, requiring no proof. Theoretical (nazariyya) premises require reasoning to be known. The author says the same proposition can be in both categories.
2
Identify the perspective shift
When a premise is considered 'in itself' (أنفسها), it is judged intuitively. When considered 'under a description of some assents and concepts' (بعنوان بعض التصديقات والتصورات), it becomes theoretical.
3
See how this avoids infinite regress
If every theoretical premise needed proof from prior premises, there would be an infinite regress. But since any premise can be treated as intuitive under direct consideration, the regress stops.
4
Recognize the dialectical context
The author notes that such discussions are dialectical (jadal), not demonstrative. The truth sought is necessary and does not need a formal proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عبر موضوعها بعنوان تصير نظرية، وإذا عبر بعنوان آخر تصير بديهية ، فيجوز أن يكون إذا لوحظ إلى المقدمات أنفسها يحكم ببداهتها بديهة، وإذا لوحظ بعنوان بعض التصديقات والتصورات يكون بداهتها نظرية، فلا أول . نعم ، لو استدل أولا بأن هذه المقدمات وأطرافها ضرورية، فبعض التصورات أو التصديقات ضرورة لكفى وخف المؤنة، لكن تعيين الطريق ليس بواجب على المناظر. والحق أن هذا كله جدل، والمطلوب ضروري لا يحتاج إلى الاستدلال. ومما يدل على جودة هذا التعقيب أن العلامة المحقق حسن العطار استجوده، وقال بعد كلام طويل: (ونعم ما قال شارح سلم العلوم... والحق أن هذا كله جدلي، والمطلوب ضروري ، لا يحتاج إلى الاستدلال»(۱).
If its subject matter is expressed with a certain description, it becomes theoretical; and if expressed with another description, it becomes intuitive. So it is possible that when the premises themselves are considered, one judges their intuitiveness intuitively; and when considered under the description of some assents and concepts, their intuitiveness becomes theoretical. So there is no first [premise]. Yes, if one first argues that these premises and their terms are necessary, then some concepts or assents are [known] necessarily, that would suffice and the burden would be lightened, but specifying the method is not obligatory for the disputant. The truth is that all of this is dialectical, and the sought conclusion is necessary, not needing demonstration. And among what indicates the excellence of this remark is that the verifying scholar Hasan al-Attar deemed it excellent, and after a long discussion said: 'How excellent is what the commentator on Sullam al-Ulum said... and the truth is that all of this is dialectical, and the sought conclusion is necessary, not needing demonstration.'
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Sullam al-Ulum discusses the epistemological status of premises in demonstration. The author explains that the same premise can be considered either intuitive (badihiyya) or theoretical (nazariyya) depending on how it is mentally conceived. He begins by stating that if a subject matter is considered under one description, it becomes theoretical; under another, it becomes intuitive. For example, the proposition 'The whole is greater than its part' is intuitive when considered in itself, but if one thinks of it as a proposition learned from Euclid, it may become theoretical. The author then addresses the regress problem: if every premise is either intuitive or theoretical, and theoretical premises need proof from earlier premises, there would be an infinite regress. He resolves this by saying there is no first premise in that sense; rather, a person can judge a premise as intuitive when looking at it directly, but it becomes theoretical if considered under a certain description. He adds that if someone insists on strict demonstration, they can argue that the premises and their terms are necessary (daruriyya), which suffices, but it is not obligatory for a disputant to specify that method. Finally, he concludes that such discussions are dialectical (jadaliyya), and the sought conclusion is necessary and does not require a formal demonstration. He supports this by quoting al-Allama Hasan al-Attar, who praised this remark. Al-Attar agrees that the debate is dialectical, not demonstrative. Thus, the passage clarifies the flexibility of epistemological categories and the proper context for their application.

Key Terms
بديهية
نظرية
جدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can the same premise be both intuitive and theoretical?
A) Because premises are always ambiguous.
B) Because the judgment depends on how the premise is mentally considered.
C) Because intuitive premises are not real knowledge.
D) Because theoretical premises are always derived from intuition.

Key Takeaway
A premise's status as intuitive or theoretical is relative to the mental frame of reference, not an intrinsic property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the proposition 'The whole is greater than its part.' Explain why it is intuitive when considered in itself, but could be considered theoretical if one reflects on its justification. Use the author's framework.
?
2. In a debate about the foundations of fiqh, one party claims that the principle 'Harm must be removed' is intuitive. The other demands proof. Using the lesson, explain how both are valid depending on perspective.

Dialectical vs Demonstrative Reasoning in Epistemological Debates
Author discusses how premises can be both intuitive and theoretical depending on perspective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish dialectical (jadal) reasoning from demonstrative (burhan) reasoning in epistemological contexts and identify when a debate is merely dialectical.

Core Idea
Discussion about whether premises are intuitive or theoretical is dialectical; the real goal is necessary truth, not formal proof.

After explaining that premises can be both intuitive and theoretical, the author concludes that such discussions are dialectical (jadal). This is a crucial epistemological point: not every inquiry requires a full demonstrative proof. In Islamic logic, jadal is a legitimate form of reasoning that uses accepted premises, but it does not yield certainty. The author, supported by al-Attar, reminds us that the sought conclusion in many debates is necessary and evident, needing no demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Understand jadal (dialectic)
Jadal is reasoning based on premises that are accepted by the interlocutors, not necessarily certain. It contrasts with burhan (demonstration), which uses certain premises.
2
Identify the dialectical nature of the premise debate
The author says 'The truth is that all of this is dialectical'. The debate about whether premises are intuitive or theoretical is itself not a demonstrative inquiry; it is a dialectical exchange.
3
Recognize when demonstration is unnecessary
The 'sought conclusion' (المطلوب) is necessary and does not need demonstration. In Islamic sciences, some truths are self-evident; demanding proof for them is an exercise in jadal, not burhan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عبر موضوعها بعنوان تصير نظرية، وإذا عبر بعنوان آخر تصير بديهية ، فيجوز أن يكون إذا لوحظ إلى المقدمات أنفسها يحكم ببداهتها بديهة، وإذا لوحظ بعنوان بعض التصديقات والتصورات يكون بداهتها نظرية، فلا أول . نعم ، لو استدل أولا بأن هذه المقدمات وأطرافها ضرورية، فبعض التصورات أو التصديقات ضرورة لكفى وخف المؤنة، لكن تعيين الطريق ليس بواجب على المناظر. والحق أن هذا كله جدل، والمطلوب ضروري لا يحتاج إلى الاستدلال. ومما يدل على جودة هذا التعقيب أن العلامة المحقق حسن العطار استجوده، وقال بعد كلام طويل: (ونعم ما قال شارح سلم العلوم... والحق أن هذا كله جدلي، والمطلوب ضروري ، لا يحتاج إلى الاستدلال»(۱).
If its subject matter is expressed with a certain description, it becomes theoretical; and if expressed with another description, it becomes intuitive. And among what indicates the excellence of this remark is that the verifying scholar Hasan al-Attar deemed it excellent, and after a long discussion said: 'How excellent is what the commentator on Sullam al-Ulum said... and the truth is that all of this is dialectical, and the sought conclusion is necessary, not needing demonstration.'

Definition of Demonstration and Certainty
Definition of demonstration and certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define demonstration (burhan) and certainty (yaqin), and list the three conditions for certainty as stated in the passage.

Core Idea
Demonstration is the highest logical art because it yields certainty through certain premises.

The five logical arts begin with demonstration (burhan), the pinnacle of reasoning. Understanding what makes a syllogism demonstrative—and what certainty (yaqin) truly means—is essential for constructing proofs in Islamic sciences like kalam and usul al-fiqh. This lesson unpacks the author's definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia of burhan
Burhan is defined as 'the certain syllogism in premises'. The genus is 'syllogism' (qiyas) and the differentia is being 'certain in premises' (yaqini al-muqaddimat). Notice that premises can be rational or transmitted, provided they yield certainty.
2
Extract the three conditions of certainty
Certainty (yaqin) is defined as 'firm belief, conformable to reality, stable'. These three conditions: jāzim (firm, no doubt), muṭābiq (conforming to truth), and thābit (stable, not wavering). Ensure you can recall each.
3
Note the role of primary axioms
The principles of certain premises are the awwaliyyāt (primary axioms, self-evident truths). They serve as the foundation for all demonstrative reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصَّنَاعَاتُ الْخَمْسُ
الْأَوَّلُ: الْبُرْهَانُ، وَهُوَ الْقِيَاسُ الْيَقِينِيُّ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ ، عَقْلِيَّةٌ كَانَتْ أَوْ نَقْلِيَّةٌ ; فَإِنَّ النَّقْلَ قَدْ يُفِيدُ الْقَطْعَ نَعَمْ ، النَّقْلُ الصِّرْفُ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ.
وَالْيَقِينُ هُوَ الِاعْتِقَادُ الْجَازِمُ الْمُطَابِقُ الثَّابِتُ، وَأُصُولُهَا الْأَوَّلِيَّاتُ ، وَهِيَ
The five arts. The first: al-burhān (demonstration), and it is the certain syllogism in premises, whether rational or transmitted; for transmission may yield certainty. Yes, bare transmission is not like that. And certainty (al-yaqīn) is firm belief, conformable [to reality], and stable. And their principles are the primary axioms, and they are…
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage introduces the five arts or divisions of logic, beginning with demonstration (burhan). Burhan is defined as a syllogism whose premises are certain (yaqiniyyah), meaning they yield absolute certainty in the conclusion. These premises can be rational (aqlīyyah), such as the law of non-contradiction, or transmitted (naqlīyyah), such as mutawātir reports that yield certain knowledge. However, the author notes that bare transmission (without certainty) does not suffice. Then certainty (yaqin) itself is defined as a firm belief (i'tiqād jāzim) that conforms to reality (muṭābiq) and is stable (thābit), meaning it does not waver. The principles of these certain premises are the primary axioms (awwaliyyāt). This is the foundation for all demonstrative knowledge, such as in kalam where the existence of God is proven through certain premises.

Key Terms
البرهان
اليقين
الأوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three conditions for certainty (yaqīn)?
A) Firm, probable, stable.
B) Firm, conformable, stable.
C) Firm, conformable, temporary.
D) Rational, transmitted, axiomatic.

Key Takeaway
Burhan is a syllogism with certain premises, and certainty itself is a firm, reality-conforming, and stable belief grounded in primary axioms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the three conditions of yaqin (certainty) as defined in the passage. Then, for each condition, provide a short explanation of why it is necessary for demonstrative knowledge.
?
Consider the following syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore every khamr is forbidden.' Is this a burhan? Why or why not based on the passage?

Distinguishing Burhan Limmi from Burhan Inni
Distinguishes demonstration of the reason why from demonstration of the fact based on middle term causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish burhan limmi from burhan inni based on the causal role of the middle term and correctly classify a given demonstration.

Core Idea
A demonstration's middle term either causes the judgment (limmi) or not (inni).

In demonstrative reasoning, the middle term's relationship to the judgment determines the type of knowledge we gain. This passage clarifies the difference between knowing why something is true (limmi) and knowing that it is true (inni). By understanding this distinction, you will be able to evaluate the strength and nature of proofs used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In a demonstrative argument (syllogism), find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term (hadd al-awsat).
For example, in 'Every changing thing is created; the world is changing; therefore the world is created,' the middle term is 'changing'.
2
Determine if the middle term is the cause
Ask: Is the middle term the 'illa (cause) of the judgment in reality? In the example, change is the cause of creation according to kalam. If yes, the demonstration is burhan limmi.
3
Classify as inni if not cause
If the middle term is not the cause—it may be an effect or unrelated—the demonstration is burhan inni. The middle term may then be called a dalil (sign).
Example: 'Every body has a creator; the world is a body; therefore the world has a creator.' Here, 'body' is not the cause of having a creator; it is an effect.
4
Recognize the epistemological difference
Burhan limmi gives knowledge of the reason why (limmi = because). Burhan inni gives knowledge that something is the case (inni = that), often through signs or effects.
Both are valid demonstrations, but limmi is considered more perfect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْأَوْسَطُ إِنْ كَانَ عِلَّةَ لِلْحُكْمِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ فَالْبُرْهَانُ لِمِّيُّ، وَإِلَّا فَإِنِّيُّ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ مَعْلُولًا لِلْحُكْمِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ ، وَيُسَمَّى دَلِيلًا ، أَوْ لَا. وَالاسْتِدْلَالُ بِوُجُودِ الْمَعْلُولِ لِشَيْءٍ عَلَى أَنَّ لَهُ عِلَّةٌ مَّا ، كَقَوْلِنَا كُلُّ جِسْمِ...
Then the middle term: if it is the cause of the judgment in reality, then the burhan is limmi (demonstration of the reason why); otherwise it is inni (demonstration of the fact), whether it is an effect of the judgment in reality—and it is called dalil (proof)—or not. And the inference from the existence of an effect of a thing to the existence of a cause for it, such as our saying 'Every body...'
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two types of demonstration (burhan) based on the relationship of the middle term to the judgment. Burhan limmi (demonstration of the reason why) occurs when the middle term is the cause ('illa) of the judgment in reality. For example, if we say 'The world is created because it is changing,' where change is the cause of creation. Burhan inni (demonstration of the fact) occurs when the middle term is not the cause, but rather an effect or something else. In inni, the middle term may be an effect of the judgment, in which case it is called dalil (proof). The author also mentions inference from the existence of an effect to the existence of a cause in general (like inferring a creator from creation), giving the example 'Every body...' which is cut off. This distinction is important: limmi gives knowledge of the reason why something is the case, while inni gives knowledge that something is the case without necessarily knowing the reason why. Both are valid demonstrations, but limmi is considered superior.

Key Terms
برهان لمّي
برهان إني
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) effect
B) cause
C) subject
D) predicate

Key Takeaway
The causal status of the middle term in a demonstration determines whether it is limmi (reason why) or inni (fact).

Resolving the Doubt on Burhan Inni: Universal vs. Particular Knowledge
Author resolves a doubt about burhan inni by distinguishing universal from particular knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the shaykh's objection does not invalidate burhan inni by distinguishing universal from particular certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Burhan inni works for particular certainties; universal certainty needs the cause or self-evidence.

A doubt is raised: the shaykh said that certain knowledge of something that has a cause can only come from that cause. If that were taken generally, it would demolish burhan inni, which argues from effect to cause. The author saves burhan inni by showing that the shaykh's statement applies only to universal knowledge, while particular certainties can be known through other demonstrations, including burhan inni. This distinction is crucial for any proof that moves from a known effect to an unknown cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
Find the shaykh's claim in the passage: certain knowledge of something with a cause cannot be obtained except from the cause. Why would this seem to demolish burhan inni? Because burhan inni uses the effect, not the cause.
The doubt is grounded in an apparent conflict between a general epistemological principle and a specific type of argument.
2
Understand the resolution's key move
The author distinguishes 'universal knowledge' (al-'ilm al-kulli) from 'particular knowledges' (al-'ulum al-juz'iyya). Universal knowledge is permanent and requires either the cause or self-evidence. Particular knowledge can be known by necessity or by a demonstration that is not limmi—which includes burhan inni.
This distinction narrows the scope of the shaykh's claim: it applies only to universal knowledge, not all certain knowledge.
3
Apply the distinction
Burhan inni, being a demonstration from effect to cause, produces certainty only about the particular fact that the cause exists. It does not give universal knowledge of the cause's essence. Thus it remains valid for particular certainties.
The author concludes with 'fa-ta'ammal' (reflect), urging the reader to internalize this nuance.
4
Check the resolution's strength
Does the resolution successfully rebut the doubt? Yes, because it accepts the shaykh's principle for universal knowledge but denies that burhan inni aims at universal knowledge. This is a clear example of refining a doctrine by specifying its scope.
The resolution is a typical case of takhsis (specification) in logical debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الشَّيْخَ ذَهَبَ إِلَى أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ الْيَقِينِيَّ بِمَا لَهُ سَبَبٌ لَا يَحْصُلُ إِلَّا مِنْ جِهَةِ السَّبَبِ، وَمَا لَيْسَ لَهُ سَبَبٌ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيِّنًا بِنَفْسِهِ، أَوْ مَأْيُوسًا عَنْ تِبْيَانِهِ بِوَجْهٍ يَقِينِيٍّ، وَهَلْ هَذَا إِلَّا هَدْمُ قَصْرِ بُرْهَانِ الْإِنَّ؟! وَجَلَّهُ: لَعَلَّ مُرَادَهُ أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ الْكُلِّيَّ، وَهُوَ الْيَقِينُ الدَّائِمُ، إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيِّنًا مِنْ جِهَةِ السَّبَبِ أَوْ بَيِّنًا بِنَفْسِهِ، فَالْعُلُومُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ جَازَ أَنْ تَكُونَ مَعْلُومَةً بِالضَّرُورَةِ، أَوْ بِالْبُرْهَانِ غَيْرِ اللَّمِّ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
And here is a doubt: that the shaykh held that certain knowledge of that which has a cause cannot be obtained except from the cause, and that which has no cause is either self-evident or hopeless to be clarified by a certain means. And is this not a demolition of the structure of burhan inni? And its solution: perhaps his intention is that universal knowledge, which is permanent certainty, is either evident from the cause or self-evident; but particular knowledges may be known by necessity or by demonstration other than limmi. So reflect.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a doubt about whether burhan inni can produce certain knowledge. The shaykh (Ibn Sina or another authority) argued that certain knowledge of something that has a cause can only come from the cause itself, and if something has no cause, it is either self-evident or cannot be known with certainty. This seems to undermine burhan inni, which uses an effect (not the cause) to prove the cause. The author provides a resolution: the shaykh was referring to universal knowledge (al-'ilm al-kulli), which is permanent certainty. For such universal knowledge, it must be from the cause or self-evident. However, particular knowledges (al-'ulum al-juz'iyya) can be known by necessity or by demonstration other than limmi, which includes burhan inni. Thus, burhan inni remains valid for particular certainties. The author concludes with 'so reflect' (fa-ta'ammal), encouraging careful distinction.

Key Terms
شك
علم يقيني
برهان إني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Universal certain knowledge
B) Particular certain knowledge
C) Only self-evident knowledge
D) Only sensory knowledge

Key Takeaway
Burhan inni is preserved by limiting the shaykh's principle to universal knowledge; particular certainties can be reached through effect-to-cause demonstration.

Doubt on Burhan al-Limmi and Its Resolution
Author raises a doubt about exclusive causality in limmi proof and resolves it via universal vs particular knowledge distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the epistemological scope of burhan al-limmi (universal certainty) from burhan al-inni (particular certainty) and explain why burhan al-inni remains valid despite the apparent exclusivity of causal knowledge.

Core Idea
Burhan al-limmi yields universal certainty; burhan al-inni yields particular certainty—both valid but for different domains.

The shaykh's statement that certain knowledge of a caused thing only comes from its cause seems to threaten burhan al-inni (demonstration from effects). The author resolves this by clarifying that this applies only to universal permanent certainty, not to particular knowledge. This distinction preserves the validity of burhan al-inni for specific facts while upholding the superiority of limmi for universal truths. Understanding this resolution is key to applying the correct demonstration in different epistemic contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
The shaykh claimed that certain knowledge of something with a cause only comes from the cause. Why does this appear to demolish burhan al-inni? Burhan al-inni infers the cause from the effect, not from the cause itself.
2
Locate the resolution
The author says the shaykh intended universal knowledge (permanent certainty) to be from the cause or self-evident. This means the shaykh's statement was about the highest level of certainty, not about all certain knowledge.
3
Understand the distinction
Universal knowledge is invariant and permanent; particular knowledge can be certain but may change. Burhan al-inni provides certain knowledge of individual facts (e.g., 'this fire is hot') which is particular, not universal.
4
Apply the distinction
Conclude that burhan al-inni is valid for particular certainties, while burhan al-limmi yields universal truths. Both are legitimate demonstrations, but they serve different epistemic purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُؤَلَّفٌ، وَكُلُّ مُؤَلَّفٍ فَلَهُ مُؤَلِّفٌ لِمِّيٌّ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي بُرْهَانِ اللِّمِّ عِلِّيَّةُ الْأَوْسَطِ وَمَا فِي حُكْمِهِ لِقُبُولِ الْأَكْبَرِ لِلْأَصْغَرِ، لَا لِقُبُولِهِ فِي نَفْسِهِ، وَبَيْنَهُمَا بَوْنٌ بَيِّنٌ. وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الشَّيْخَ ذَهَبَ إِلَى أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ الْيَقِينِيَّ بِمَا لَهُ سَبَبٌ لَا يَحْصُلُ إِلَّا مِنْ جِهَةِ السَّبَبِ، وَمَا لَيْسَ لَهُ سَبَبٌ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيِّنًا بِنَفْسِهِ، أَوْ مَيْئُوسًا عَنْ تِبْيَانِهِ بِوَجْهٍ يَقِينِيٍّ، وَهَلْ هَذَا إِلَّا هَدْمُ قَصْرِ بُرْهَانِ الْإِنَّ؟! وَجَوَابُهُ: لَعَلَّ مُرَادَهُ أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ الْكُلِّيَّ، وَهُوَ الْيَقِينُ الدَّائِمُ، إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيِّنًا مِنْ جِهَةِ السَّبَبِ أَوْ بَيِّنًا بِنَفْسِهِ، فَالْعُلُومُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ جَازَ أَنْ تَكُونَ مَعْلُومَةً بِالضَّرُورَةِ، أَوْ بِالْبُرْهَانِ غَيْرِ اللِّمِّيِّ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
A composed thing, and every composed thing has a why-cause, and that is correct; for what is considered in a demonstration of why (burhan limmi) is the causativeness of the middle term and what is in its ruling for the acceptance of the major by the minor, not for its acceptance in itself, and between them is a clear distinction. And here is a doubt, namely that the shaykh held that certain knowledge of that which has a cause is not obtained except from the side of the cause, and that which has no cause is either self-evident or hopeless of being clarified by a certain means, and is this not a demolition of the edifice of a demonstration that? (burhan inni)?! And its answer: perhaps his intent is that universal knowledge, which is permanent certainty, is either evident from the side of the cause or self-evident, so particular knowledge may be known by necessity or by a demonstration other than limmi, so reflect.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage first affirms the standard view that in a limmi demonstration (demonstration of why), the middle term's causality is what allows the major to be affirmed of the minor, not any inherent acceptance of the major itself. Then it raises a doubt: if the shaykh claims that certain knowledge of something with a cause only comes from its cause, and that which has no cause is either self-evident or impossible to demonstrate, then wouldn't that render the burhan inni (demonstration that) impossible? The answer clarifies that the shaykh intended universal knowledge (permanent certainty) to come only from the cause or be self-evident; particular knowledge (such as fact-by-observation) can be known by necessity or by non-limmi demonstration. Thus burhan inni is preserved for particular truths.

Key Takeaway
Burhan al-limmi provides universal permanent certainty; particular certain knowledge can be obtained through burhan al-inni or necessity, so burhan al-inni remains a valid demonstrative method.

The Conditional Argument: Composition Implies Contingency
Concise argument for God's simplicity via contingency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument for God's simplicity from composition, and explain the scope of its conclusion regarding external vs. mental composition.

Core Idea
If God had parts, He would need them—need proves contingency, so God must be simple.

This lesson unpacks the second proof for God's simplicity (al-wajh al-thani). The argument is a concise conditional: if the Necessary Existent were composed of parts, He would depend on them, and dependence is a mark of contingency. Therefore, composition is incompatible with necessary existence. However, as the author notes, this proof directly only negates external composition (al-tarkib al-khariji). To also negate mental composition (al-tarkib al-dhihni), one must establish a mutual implication (talazum) between the two. This nuance is crucial for understanding the full force of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The passage states: 'if He had parts, the Necessary would be needful of them.' This is the antecedent (having parts) leading to the consequent (being needy). Recognize that the argument hinges on the link between composition and need.
2
Extract the implicit conclusion
The conclusion is not explicitly stated but is implied: 'Therefore, the Necessary Existent does not have parts.' This follows by modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, so not P. Here Q is 'contingent,' which contradicts necessary existence.
The argument uses the principle that a necessary being cannot be contingent.
3
Distinguish external vs. mental composition
The author explicitly says the proof only negates external composition (physical parts). Mental composition (e.g., logical parts like genus and differentia) is not directly refuted unless one accepts talazum—the mutual implication between the two types of composition.
This distinction is important for the broader debate on God's attributes.
4
Assess the condition of mutual implication (talazum)
If talazum holds, then negating external composition also negates mental composition. The passage leaves this as a conditional: 'unless it is based on mutual implication between them.' This allows the argument to be strengthened under certain metaphysical assumptions.
Talazum is a key concept in kalam for linking different levels of analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو كان له أجزاء لكان الواجب محتاجا إليها، فصار ممكنا، وهذا الدليل إنما ينفي التركيب الخارجي فقط، اللهم إلا أن يبنى على التلازم بينهما.
The second: if He had parts, the Necessary would be needful of them, so He would become contingent. This proof only negates external composition, unless it is based on the mutual implication between them.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage provides the second proof of God's simplicity: if God were composite, He would need His parts, and need is a property of contingent beings. Therefore, God would be contingent, contradicting His necessary existence. The author notes that this argument only refutes external composition (like physical parts) unless there is an established mutual implication between external and mental composition, in which case it would also refute mental composition. This proof is less comprehensive than the first but straightforwardly shows the incompatibility of composition with necessity.

Key Terms
محتاج
تلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He would be independent of them.
B) He would need them and thus be contingent.
C) He would be simple both mentally and externally.
D) The parts would be necessary themselves.

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses a conditional to show that composition implies need, and need implies contingency, so God cannot be composite; but it only directly refutes external composition unless talazum is invoked.

The Core Argument: Composition and Differentia
Third proof: composition negated via differentia paradox

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditional structure of the third proof and explain why a composite God leads to a contradiction involving the differentia.

Core Idea
If God were composite, His differentia would produce existence — but His quiddity is existence.

The author presents a conditional argument: if the Necessary were composite, its constitutive differentia would have to be a dividing differentia. But since God's quiddity is existence itself (as per Ash'ari doctrine), the differentia would be producing existence, which is a contradiction. Let's unpack this logical step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if' clause: 'if the Necessary were composite'. This is the hypothesis being disproven.
2
Understand the constitutive differentia's role
A constitutive differentia gives the quiddity without existence. It defines what a thing is, not that it exists.
3
Recognize God's unique quiddity
In Ash'ari theology, God's quiddity (essence) is existence itself. His 'whatness' is identical to His existence.
4
Spot the contradiction
If God were composite, His constitutive differentia would give quiddity. But since His quiddity is existence, the differentia would be giving existence, which is the job of the dividing differentia. This clashes with the defined role.
5
Conclude impossibility
Thus composition leads to an absurdity, so God must be simple and non-composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: لو كان الواجب مركبا لكان فصله المقوم مقسما؛ لأن الفصل المقوم يحصل الماهية دون الوجود؛ إذ ماهيته الوجود على ما تقرر في مقره، فيكون محصلا للوجود وهو شأن الفصل المقسم، وهذا مختص بإبطال التركيب الذهني ويرد عليه أن شأن الفصل المقسم أن يكون خارجا عما هو فصل له، ومحصلا لوجوده، ومقسما له إلى قسمين، وفصله المقوم وإن أفاد الحكمة حتى يحتاج إلى التعين والتحصل، فيمكن. وتقرير الدفع أنه لو كان مفهوم الواجب عرضا على أفراد بسيطة كذلك لكان أمرا واحدا مشتركا بين هذا الواجب وهذا، وذلك لأن العرض العام أيضا ماهية من الماهيات، فيكون هناك مبهم ومحصل، فيحتاج، فيكون ممكنا، هذا خلف، فإذا لم يكن واجبا لنفسه فكيف لغيره.
The third: if the Necessary were composite, then its constitutive differentia would be a divider; because the constitutive differentia gives the quiddity without existence, since His quiddity is existence as established in its place, so it would be producing existence, and that is the function of the dividing differentia. And this is specific to negating mental composition. And it is objected that the function of the dividing differentia is to be external to what it is a differentia for, producing its existence, and dividing it into two divisions, and its constitutive differentia even if it provides the wisdom until it needs specification and actualization, so it is contingent. And the refutation is that if the concept of the Necessary were an accident upon simple individuals in that way, then it would be one common thing between this Necessary and that, because the general accident is also a quiddity among quiddities, so there would be an ambiguous and a specified, so it would need, so it would be contingent. This is a contradiction. So if it is not necessary for itself, how can it be for another?
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage gives the third proof: if God were composite, His constitutive differentia (which defines quiddity) would have to be a divider (muqassim) that produces existence. But God's quiddity is existence itself (as per the Ash'ari doctrine), so the differentia would be producing existence, which is paradoxical. The author then addresses an objection from Ibn Kammuna: maybe the concept 'necessary' is an accidental attribute applying to multiple simple individuals, not essential. The refutation argues that if it were an accident, it would still be a common concept (a general accident is itself a quiddity), leading to a composition of ambiguous and specified, which implies need and contingency. Thus, God cannot have such a concept as an accident, or He would be contingent. The proof concludes that if something is not necessary for itself, it cannot be necessary for another.

Key Terms
فصل مقوم (faṣl muqawwim): The constitutive differentia that defines the quiddity of a species; here it is argued that for God, this differentia would paradoxically produce existence.
فصل مقسم (faṣl muqassim): A dividing differentia that separates a genus into species; its function is to give existence and divide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what role would the constitutive differentia have if God were composite?
A) It would define the quiddity without existence.
B) It would produce existence, which is the function of a dividing differentia.
C) It would be identical to the dividing differentia.
D) It would be unnecessary because God is simple.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that composition in God leads to a contradiction because the constitutive differentia would have to produce existence, conflicting with God's quiddity being existence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional argument: 'If God had an attribute that is not identical to His essence, then He would be composite.' Show how this leads to a contradiction using the same differentia structure.

Refuting Ibn Kammuna's Doubt: The Accidentality of 'Necessary'
Refutation of Ibn Kammuna's doubt via accident analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that 'necessary' is an accidental concept by showing that a general accident is itself a quiddity, leading to composition and contingency.

Core Idea
Even if 'necessary' were an accident, it would still be a quiddity, causing composition and contingency.

Ibn Kammuna doubted that the concept 'necessary' might be an accidental attribute applying to multiple simple individuals. The author refutes this by analyzing the nature of general accidents. Let's see how even this doubt fails to preserve God's simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
The objector suggests that 'necessary' might be a general accident ('arad 'amm) that applies to multiple simple entities, like a property shared by simple individuals.
2
Classify the general accident
A general accident is itself a quiddity (mahiyya) among quiddities. It has an essence that can be predicated of multiple subjects.
3
Observe the composition
If 'necessary' is an accident shared by multiple individuals, then each individual would have a composition of its own essence plus the accidental quiddity of 'necessary'.
4
Detect the need for specification
The accidental quiddity is ambiguous (mubham) and requires specification (tahsil) by the individual's essence. This need indicates dependence and contingency.
5
Conclude contradiction
Thus, if 'necessary' were an accident, the subject would be contingent (mumkin). But God is necessary by essence, so this is impossible. Hence the doubt is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Critique of the Philosophers' Emanation Chain
Refutes the philosophers' emanation theory by showing circularity and grounding in the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two causal alternatives in the philosophers' emanation theory and explain why each leads to incoherence.

Core Idea
Either a cause necessitates coexistence or leads to an infinite regress—the emanation chain collapses.

In this passage, the author dissects the philosophers' claim that volitions and desires flow from a series of imaginings in a non-stationary manner. He presents a crucial fork: a particular volition flowing from a particular imagination must be either an efficient cause (ʿillah mūjibah) or a preparatory cause (muʿiddah). Each option leads to an absurdity, thereby refuting the entire emanation model. By understanding this logical fork, you will grasp how kalām thinkers dismantled the causal metaphysics of the falāsifah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers state that volitions and desires are non-stationary, flowing from imaginings, themselves non-stationary, leading to motion without a cycle. Understand the baseline model before the critique.
2
Spot the disjunctive premise
The author introduces a disjunction: a particular volition that flows from a particular imagination is either an efficient (ʿillah mūjibah) cause for another imagination or a preparatory (muʿiddah) cause. Recognize that these are the only two options in the causal framework.
3
Analyze the first option (efficient cause)
If it is an efficient cause, it must coexist with its effect and the effect's effect, leading to the simultaneous existence of all parts of motion—an impossibility. Consider why this would violate the temporal order of motion.
4
Analyze the second option (preparatory cause)
If it is a preparatory cause, then the chain of preparatory causes must terminate in the Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd). But then none of the preparatory causes would necessarily exist, because the Necessary Being is necessarily existent and the whole chain is possible. This would violate the principle 'a thing does not exist until it is necessitated'. Understand how this leads to a failure to ground existence.
5
Conclude the critique
Both alternatives are impossible, so the philosophers' model fails. The author then rejects any attempt to connect the originated (ḥādith) to the eternal (qadīm) by bypassing motion or invoking infinite preparatory causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الكتاب
الإرادات والأشواق ، وهي غير قارة، وهي فاضت من سلسلة التخييلات، أيضا غير قارة، وهي فاضت من الحركة من غير لزوم دور ، كما بينا ، وبه اندفع الاعتراض الثاني أيضا.
وهي
ينتهي
وفيه نظر؛ لأن الدورة التي فاضت عن إرادة جزئية فائضة عن تخيل جزئي إما علة موجبة لتخييل آخر، فيجب اجتماعها معه، وكذا مع معلوله ومعلول معلوله، فيلزم اجتماع أجزاء الحركة، وإما معدة، فلا بد أن لا سلسلة المعدات إلى واجب الوجود بالذات، وإلا يلزم قدمها، فلم يجب وجود واحد منها؛ لأن الواجب ما يمتنع عليه جميع أنحاء العدم، ومن بعض عدم تلك السلسلة بأجمعها وهو ممكن فيلزم أن لا يكون موجودا ؛ لأن الشيء ما لم يجب لم يوجد. ومن ههنا سقط ما قيل في ربط الحادث بالقديم من إسقاط وساطة الحركة، والقول بأن وجود(١) كل حادث مسبوق بوجود حادث قبله معد له، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، والله أعلم. وبسط القول في «العجالة النافعة».
Preface of the book. The volitions and desires, which are non-stationary, and they flowed from the series of imaginings, also non-stationary, and they flowed from motion without requiring a cycle, as we have shown, and by it the second objection is also repelled. And it ends. And there is consideration in it: because the cycle that flowed from a particular volition flowing from a particular imagination is either an efficient cause for another imagination, so it must coexist with it, and likewise with its effect and the effect of its effect, so the coexistence of the parts of motion is necessary; or it is a preparatory cause, then there must be a chain of preparatory causes ending with the Necessary Being in essence, otherwise its priority in existence would be necessary; so none of them would necessarily exist, because the necessary is that for which all aspects of non-existence are impossible, and from some non-existence of that entire chain, while it is possible, it follows that it would not exist, because a thing does not exist until it is necessary. And from this falls what was said about connecting the originated to the eternal by dropping the intermediacy of motion, and the claim that the existence of every originated event is preceded by the existence of an event before it that prepares it, and so on ad infinitum. And God knows best. And the elaboration of the statement is in 'al-'Ajalah al-Nafi'ah'.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the philosophers' theory of emanation. The author first describes the philosophers' view: volitions and desires are non-stationary and flow from a series of imaginings, which are also non-stationary and flow from motion, but without a vicious cycle. The author then raises an objection: if a particular volition flows from a particular imagination and then becomes a cause for another imagination, there are two possibilities. First, if it is an efficient cause (ʿillah mūjibah), then it would necessitate the coexistence of that imagination along with its own effect and the effect of that effect, leading to the simultaneous presence of all parts of motion, which is impossible. Second, if it is a preparatory cause (muʿiddah), then the chain of preparatory causes must end with the Necessary Being; otherwise, the chain would be eternal. But if it ends with the Necessary Being, then none of the preparatory causes would necessarily exist, because the Necessary Being cannot have non-existence, while the chain as a whole is possible, so something possible would not exist without a necessity—this violates the principle that a thing does not exist until it is necessitated. The author concludes that this invalidates any attempt to connect the originated (al-ḥādith) to the eternal (al-qadīm) by bypassing the mediation of motion and claiming an infinite regress of preparatory causes. This is a standard critique of the philosophers' causal theory from a kalām perspective.

Key Terms
إرادة جزئية
تخيل جزئي
علة موجبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the problem if a particular volition is an efficient cause (ʿillah mūjibah) for another imagination?
A) It would require an infinite regress.
B) It would necessitate the coexistence of all parts of motion.
C) It would make the Necessary Being unnecessary.
D) It would render the volition non-stationary.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' emanation chain is refuted by a dilemma: efficient causation forces simultaneous motion parts, while preparatory causation fails to ground existence—both are impossible.

The Reductio Argument Against All Knowledge Being Theoretical
Author proves that not all knowledge can be theoretical by arguing it leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument that not all knowledge can be theoretical and explain why circularity or infinite regress would result.

Core Idea
If all knowledge required reflection, we could never start — vicious circle or infinite regress.

This passage tackles a foundational epistemological issue: can every piece of knowledge depend on prior reflection? The author argues that if it did, we would fall into an impossible circularity or an infinite regress, both of which prevent knowledge from ever being attained. This reductio underlies the distinction between necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption under attack
The author criticizes the claim that all knowledge is theoretical (nazari), meaning it depends on reflection. Find where this assumption is stated.
The phrase 'لا نَظْرِيَّا' indicates the negation of this claim.
2
Trace the consequence for reflection itself
Reflection is itself a theoretical act. If all knowledge is theoretical, then reflection would require prior reflection, leading to an infinite regress.
The text says 'فلا بد من التصديق بالفائدة والتصور بوجه ما وهو أيضًا نظري'.
3
Analyze the circularity example using A and B
The author illustrates circularity: if A depends on B and B depends on A, then A depends on itself, which is absurd. Follow the logic of self-dependence.
The text uses 'أ' and 'ب' as variables. This is a formal logical notation.
4
Consider the infinite regress argument and the caveat about the eternal soul
The author notes that if the soul is eternal, infinite regress might not be impossible because knowledge could be accumulated over infinite time. This nuance is important for the completeness of the argument.
The passage mentions Al-Dawani's point about essence (kunh) never being attained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أَزْمِنَةٍ غَيْرِ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ وَبَعْدَ ذَلِكَ زَمَانٌ مَحْدُودٌ وَمَتَى لَمْ يَحْصُلْ كُنْهٌ لَمْ يَحْصُلْ وَجْهٌ لأَنَّ كُلَّ وَجْهٍ كُنْهُ شَيْءٍ وَلا أَقَلَّ مِنْ أَنْ يَكُونَ
And not all are theoretical, dependent on reflection. And if [all were theoretical], then nothing of the sciences would be obtained, because reflection is an optional act, so there must be an assent to the benefit and a concept in some way, and it too is theoretical, so in its acquisition it also requires both, and so on. Also, if all were theoretical, it would lead to circularity, so the thing's priority over itself by two degrees would follow. The circularity if it is by one degree, then the dependence on itself would be by two degrees, and if by two, then by three, and so on, nay by infinite degrees, for circularity entails infinite regress. Its exposition: that A for example, if its dependence is on B, and B on A, then A is dependent on itself, so A is dependent and A itself is depended upon, and between them difference is required, so A is a thing and A itself is another thing, and similarly the case of B. Then we say: A itself depends on B itself, and B itself on A itself, so A itself depends on the self of A itself, and due to the requirement of difference, they must be two, and so on. And there is a clear objection in it: for the difference between the dependent and the depended upon, even if true in reality, is prevented on the supposition of assuming circularity. Moreover, if difference were conceded, circularity would be negated. And what is said that what is true in reality is true even if taken on a contradictory supposition, for suppositions do not change a real matter, so his answer will come, God willing. Or infinite regress, and it is false. The famous (argument) mentioned here for its refutation is that if knowledge of the infinite regress were obtained, it would require the comprehension of infinite things. And this depends on the temporality of the soul; for if it were eternal, it could be that infinite sciences have been obtained in infinite times, and concurrence is not required so that impossible comprehension would follow. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said: If the chain of acquisition regresses infinitely, then the essence (kunh) would never be obtained, because the attainment of the essence depends on the attainment of the aspect (wajh), and it is theoretical, so if it came from its principles, it would occur in infinite times, and after that a finite time. And when no essence is obtained, no aspect is obtained, because every aspect is an essence of something, and the least is that it be...
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial epistemological argument: not all knowledge can be theoretical. The author uses a reductio: if all knowledge required reflection, then reflection itself (a theoretical act) would require prior reflection ad infinitum, leading to either a circular dependency or an infinite regress. The circularity argument is explained in detail using the example of A and B, showing that self-dependence leads to an infinite multiplication of selves. An objection is noted: the distinction between dependent and depended upon might not hold in the circular case. The author acknowledges the objection and promises a response. Then the infinite regress argument is discussed. If acquisition chains were infinite, we could never obtain knowledge because we would need to traverse an infinite series. However, the author mentions a caveat: if the soul is eternal, infinite knowledge could be accumulated over infinite time. This complicates the refutation. The passage ends with a quote from al-Dawani emphasizing that the essence of something would never be attained. This lesson is key to understanding why foundational, self-evident knowledge is necessary.

B) Knowledge would be easier to obtain.
C) Only necessary knowledge would remain.
D) It would lead to an infinite plurality of necessary truths.

Key Takeaway
Foundational, self-evident knowledge is necessary to avoid circularity or infinite regress in the chain of knowledge acquisition.

?

The Double Argument: Proving Number is Finite
Doubling proof against infinite number

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the doubling argument against an infinite number using conditional reasoning.

Core Idea
If an infinite number existed, doubling it would lead to contradiction; therefore number must be finite.

This passage from the text presents a classic Kalam argument for the finitude of number. The author lays out three premises and uses conditional (if-then) reasoning to show that an actually infinite number leads to an absurdity. You will follow the logical steps and see how this argument is used in Islamic theology to establish the finitude of the past.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author is proving that number cannot be infinite. Look for the conclusion at the end: the finitude of number entails the finitude of the counted.
2
Extract the three premises
Locate the three numbered premises in the Arabic text: (1) The number of the double is greater than the original. (2) If one number is greater, the increase occurs after the exhaustion of the smaller (order preserved). (3) The principle (first number) cannot be increased upon.
The premises are marked by parentheses in the translation.
3
Understand the conditional reasoning
Assume an infinite number exists. Double it while preserving order. By premise 1, the double is greater. By premise 2, this implies an increase after exhaustion, but premise 3 says the first number cannot be increased. Therefore, the assumption leads to contradiction, so the infinite cannot exist.
This is a conditional proof: if P then Q, but Q is impossible, so not P.
4
Note the role of the hashya
The commentator adds that infinite things are necessarily subject to number, reinforcing that the infinite must be countable and thus finite.
This clarifies that the argument applies to ordered and unordered collections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فغير المتناهي من الجانبين لا يكون معروضا لعدد. وإذا جاز عدم عروض العدد لغير المتناهي من الجانبين فليجز أن لا يكون عارضا لغير المتناهي من جانب، ففساده غير مخفي ؛ لأن الواحد ليس مبدأ لكمية سلسلة غير متناهية . وأما كونه مبدأ بمعنى أنه أول الأعداد عند من يسميه عددا، أو بمعنى كونه عادا للجميع، أو كونه علة وجزء لها لا يضر مع أن قياس غير المتناهي من جانب عليته من الجانبين غير سديد لظهور الفرق، و(عَدَدَ التَّضْعِيف أَزْيَدُ مِنْ عَدَدِ الأصل)؛ لأن الكل أزيد من الجزء، (وَكُلُّ عَدَدَيْنِ أَحَدُهُمَا أَزْيَدُ مِنَ الْآخَرِ ، فَزِيَادَةُ الزَّائِدِ بَعْدَ انْصِرَامِ جَمِيعِ أَحَادِ الْمَزِيدِ عَلَيْهِ)، إِن فرض الزيادة بحيث لا يختل ترتيب المزيد عليه؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَبْدَأَ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ عَلَيْهِ الزَّيَادَةُ، وإلا لم يكن مبدأ، وَالْأَوْسَاطُ مُنتَظِمَةٌ مُتَوَالِيَةٌ). وإذا تقرر هذه المقدمات فَحِينَئِذٍ لو كان عدد ما غير متناه، وضعفناه من غير اختلال ترتيب الأصل، فكان زائدا على الأصل المزيد عليه الغير المتناهي بحكم المقدمة الثانية، فَلَوْ كَانَ الْمَزِيدُ عَلَيْهِ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِ لَزِمَ الزَّيَادَةُ في جَانِبِ عَدَمِ التَّنَاهِي، بحكم المقدمة الثالثة، وَهُوَ بَاطِلٌ، وإلا لزم تناهي غير المتناهي، وَتَنَاهِي الْعَدَدِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ تَنَاهِيَ الْمَعْدُودِ، بحكم المقدمة الأولى. قال في «الحاشية»: الأمور الغير المتناهية سواء كانت مرتبة أو لا، مجتمعة أو متعاقبة تكون معروضة لعدد ما بالضرورة
Thus what is infinite from both sides is not subject to number. And if it is permissible that number does not apply to what is infinite from both sides, then let it be permissible that it does not apply to what is infinite from one side. But its corruption is not hidden, because the unit is not a principle for the quantity of an infinite series. And as for it being a principle in the sense that it is the first of numbers for those who call it a number, or in the sense that it counts all, or that it is a cause and a part of them, it does no harm, although the analogy of the infinite from one side to the infinite from both sides is not sound due to the evident difference. And (the number of the double is greater than the number of the original) because the whole is greater than the part, and (every two numbers one of which is greater than the other, the increase of the greater occurs after the exhaustion of all units of the lesser), if the increase is assumed such that the order of the increased-upon is not disturbed; for the principle cannot be increased upon, otherwise it would not be a principle, and the intermediates are arranged consecutively. And when these premises are established, then if some number were infinite, and we doubled it without disturbing the order of the original, then it would be greater than the original increased-upon infinite by virtue of the second premise. So if the increased-upon were infinite, an increase would be necessary in the side of infinity, by virtue of the third premise, which is false, otherwise the infinite would become finite. And the finitude of number entails the finitude of the counted, by virtue of the first premise. The commentator said in al-Hashiya: Infinite things, whether ordered or not, simultaneous or successive, are necessarily subject to some number.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn statement about the impossibility of an actually infinite number. It begins by stating that an infinite from both sides (i.e., an infinite set with no beginning and no end) cannot be subject to number, then argues that even an infinite from one side (e.g., an infinite future or past) cannot be subject to number. It then lays down three premises: (1) the number of doubled units is greater than the original number, (2) for any two numbers where one is greater, the greater exceeds the lesser after the lesser is exhausted, (3) the principle (the first number) cannot be increased upon. Then it applies these: if there were an infinite number, and we doubled it without disturbing order, the double would be greater than the original infinite, implying an increase in the infinite, which is absurd. Hence number must be finite, and finite number implies finite counted things. The hashya clarifies that the infinite things, whether ordered or not, simultaneous or successive, are necessarily subject to number. This argument is a standard Kalam proof for the finitude of the past (for the creation of the world). For example, if the number of past events were infinite, the number of events in the last 100 years would be a part of that infinite, but part and whole cannot be compared in magnitude to imply finitude unless the infinite is actual, which the mutakallimun deny.

Key Takeaway
The doubling argument demonstrates that an actually infinite number leads to a logical contradiction, thereby proving that all numbers are finite.

However, the number of events in the last century is finite. Therefore, the number of past events is finite.' Identify which premises from the passage are used.

Understanding Burhan al-Tadhayuf: Correlation Refutes Infinity
Author presents the semantic proof that cause and effect are correlatives, disproving infinite causal chains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Burhan al-Tadhayuf argument and identify why an infinite causal chain violates the correlation between cause and effect.

Core Idea
Correlatives must match in number; an infinite chain creates an impossible imbalance.

This logical proof uses the nature of correlation (tadhayuf) to show that infinite regress in causation is impossible. By understanding how cause and effect imply each other, you gain a powerful tool used in Kalam to prove the necessity of a first creator. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the correlatives
Recognize that cause ('illa) and effect (ma'lul) are defined in terms of each other. Their relationship is one of mutual dependence.
Correlatives always come in pairs.
2
State the correlation principle
For correlatives, the number of instances of one must equal the number of instances of the other. In a finite chain, the first cause is cause-only and the last effect is effect-only, balancing the count.
This is the key logical rule.
3
Consider an infinite causal chain
Imagine a chain of causes and effects with no first cause. The last effect is an effect but not a cause, while all preceding terms are both cause and effect. Count the causes and effects.
The last effect adds an effect without a corresponding cause.
4
Detect the inequality
In an infinite chain, the number of effect-statuses exceeds the number of cause-statuses by one. This violates the correlation principle.
The proof relies on this mathematical discrepancy.
5
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, an infinite causal regress is impossible. The chain must be finite, having a first cause that is cause only.
This argument is foundational for the cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان التضايف في إبطال التسلسل. تقريره لو ذهب سلسلة العلل لا إلى نهاية لوجد معلولية بلا علية، مع كونهما متضايفين. بيان الملازمة المعلول الأخير معلول وليس بعلة، والتي قبل علة ومعلول، فعدد المعلوليات أزيد من عدد العليات، وأما لو كانت متناهية فكما أن المعلول الأخير معلول غير علة، كذلك العلة الأولى علة غير معلول، وهذا كما أنه يبطل التسلسل في المبدأ كذلك يبطل التسلسل في سلسلة المعلولات؛ إذ يلزم ههنا وجود علية من غير معلولية، ففرق الصدر الشيرازي في «الأسفار بجريان هذا الدليل في التسلسل في المبدأ دون غيره تحكم. وإن شئت زيادة الكلام فاطلب من العجالة النافعة
Proof of correlation in refuting infinite regress. Its formulation: If the chain of causes goes on without end, then there would be effect-ness without cause-ness, while they are correlatives. Explanation of the implication: the last effect is an effect and not a cause, and the one before is a cause and an effect, so the number of effect-statuses is greater than the number of cause-statuses. And if they were finite, then just as the last effect is an effect not a cause, similarly the first cause is a cause not an effect. This, just as it refutes infinite regress in the beginning, it also refutes infinite regress in the chain of effects; because here there would be cause-ness without effect-ness. So al-Sadr al-Shirazi's distinction in al-Asfar that this proof applies only to regress in the beginning but not elsewhere is arbitrary. If you want more detail, seek it from al-'Ujalah al-Nafi'ah.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the Burhan al-Tadhayuf, a well-known proof in Kalam for the finitude of causal sequences. The author explains that if a chain of causes and effects were infinite (i.e., no first cause), then there would be an effect without a cause, because the last effect is only an effect, not a cause, and all preceding ones are both cause and effect. Thus the number of effect-statuses would exceed the number of cause-statuses by one. But cause and effect are correlatives (tadhayuf), which imply equality in number. In a finite chain, this inequality is resolved by the first cause being cause only and the last effect effect only, balancing the count. Therefore, an infinite regress in both directions (no beginning and no end) is impossible. The author then criticizes al-Sadr al-Shirazi for claiming that this proof only refutes regress in the principle (i.e., no first cause) but not in the chain of effects (i.e., no last effect), arguing that the same logic applies to both. For example, in the Kalam argument for the creation of the world, if there were an infinite chain of events with no beginning, there would be no first event, but the same logic shows there must be a first cause. This proof is a cornerstone of the cosmological argument.

Key Terms
تضايف
سلسلة
علة
معلول
متضايفان
تسلسل

C) It would have a cause without an effect.
D) Both B and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
Burhan al-Tadhayuf proves that causal chains must be finite because the correlation between cause and effect requires equal numbers of each.

The Full Scope of Burhan al-Tadhayuf: Refuting Regress in Both Directions
Author extends the proof to refute regress in both directions and criticizes al-Sadr al-Shirazi's exclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Burhan al-Tadhayuf to refute infinite regress both in the direction of causes (no beginning) and in the direction of effects (no end), and critically evaluate al-Sadr al-Shirazi's restriction.

Core Idea
Correlation works both ways: the proof against infinite regress is symmetric.

While the basic Burhan al-Tadhayuf is often used to prove a first cause, the author insists it equally refutes an infinite chain of effects (no last effect). This lesson explores that symmetry and critiques a famous scholar's attempt to limit the proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the symmetry of correlation
Correlatives, like cause and effect, imply each other. If one exists, the other must exist. This symmetry means the proof applies in both directions.
If there is a last effect, it implies a corresponding cause; if there is a first cause, it implies a corresponding effect.
2
Apply the proof to regress in the beginning
This is the standard case: an infinite chain with no first cause leads to an effect without a cause. The proof shows it is impossible, so there must be a first cause.
This refutes tasalsul in al-mabda' (the beginning).
3
Apply the proof to regress in the end
The author argues that an infinite chain with no last effect leads to a cause without an effect. This also violates correlation, so the chain must have a last effect.
This refutes tasalsul in silsilat al-ma'lulat (the chain of effects).
4
Evaluate al-Sadr al-Shirazi's distinction
Al-Sadr al-Shirazi claimed the proof only works for regress in the beginning. The author calls this arbitrary (tahakkum) and insists the logic applies equally.
Consider why someone might restrict it: perhaps metaphysical commitments about causality.
5
Synthesize the full argument
The Burhan al-Tadhayuf, when correctly understood, shows that an infinite causal chain in any direction is impossible. This strengthens the case for a finite, closed causal system.
This insight is crucial for Kalam arguments about the world's finitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان التضايف في إبطال التسلسل. تقريره لو ذهب سلسلة العلل لا إلى نهاية لوجد معلولية بلا علية، مع كونهما متضايفين. بيان الملازمة المعلول الأخير معلول وليس بعلة، والتي قبل علة ومعلول، فعدد المعلوليات أزيد من عدد العليات، وأما لو كانت متناهية فكما أن المعلول الأخير معلول غير علة، كذلك العلة الأولى علة غير معلول، وهذا كما أنه يبطل التسلسل في المبدأ كذلك يبطل التسلسل في سلسلة المعلولات؛ إذ يلزم ههنا وجود علية من غير معلولية، ففرق الصدر الشيرازي في «الأسفار بجريان هذا الدليل في التسلسل في المبدأ دون غيره تحكم. وإن شئت زيادة الكلام فاطلب من العجالة النافعة
Proof of correlation in refuting infinite regress. Its formulation: If the chain of causes goes on without end, then there would be effect-ness without cause-ness, while they are correlatives. Explanation of the implication: the last effect is an effect and not a cause, and the one before is a cause and an effect, so the number of effect-statuses is greater than the number of cause-statuses. And if they were finite, then just as the last effect is an effect not a cause, similarly the first cause is a cause not an effect. This, just as it refutes infinite regress in the beginning, it also refutes infinite regress in the chain of effects; because here there would be cause-ness without effect-ness. So al-Sadr al-Shirazi's distinction in al-Asfar that this proof applies only to regress in the beginning but not elsewhere is arbitrary. If you want more detail, seek it from al-'Ujalah al-Nafi'ah.

Commentary
This passage presents the Burhan al-Tadhayuf, a well-known proof in Kalam for the finitude of causal sequences. The author explains that if a chain of causes and effects were infinite (i.e., no first cause), then there would be an effect without a cause, because the last effect is only an effect, not a cause, and all preceding ones are both cause and effect.
