Misapplying Infinite Arguments to Continuous Motion
Author critiques applying infinite arguments to celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of treating continuous motion as having actual parts and explain why the infinite argument does not apply.

Core Idea
Continuous motion has no actual parts; arguments assuming discrete parts are sophistry.

Some applied the correlation proof (based on priority and posteriority of parts) to the motion of the celestial sphere, arguing that such motion must be finite. But the author objects: motion is continuous, not composed of actual parts. If we consider only mentally abstracted parts, even finite motions would have infinitely many such parts, making the argument invalid. This lesson exposes the logical error and shows how to recognize similar misapplications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
The author is responding to those who applied an infinite argument to the celestial sphere's motion. Note that they assumed the motion has parts characterized by priority and posteriority (like cause and effect).
The celestial sphere's motion is continuous rotation.
2
Understand the nature of continuous motion
Continuous motion (haraka muttasila) has no actual parts; its parts are only mentally abstracted. In physics, this means you cannot treat it as a collection of discrete moments.
Contrast with discrete motion where parts exist actually.
3
Recognize the fallacy (safsata)
The author calls the resulting argument sophistry (safsata). The fallacy is treating what is continuous as if it were discrete, leading to a false conclusion that the motion is finite.
The same error would apply to any continuous process.
4
Apply to another example
Consider the continuous flow of time. If you treat moments as actual parts and apply infinite arguments, you might wrongly conclude time must be finite. This shows the importance of the continuous/discrete distinction.
In Kalam, time is also often considered continuous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العجب إن البعض أجرى في حركة الفلك لاتصاف أجزائه بالسابقيات والمسبوقيات، ولم يعلموا أن الحركة متصلة وليس لها أجزاء بالفعل، ولو اكتفي بوجودها الانتزاعي لجرى في الحركات المتناهية؛ لأن أجزاءها غير متناهية أيضا، ومتصفة بالتقدم والتأخر فيلزم تناهي الأجزاء، وهو سفسطة، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. ومنها: أن كل غير متناه معروض لعدد، وكل عدد إما زوج أو فرد، فإن كان زوجا فينقسم بمتساويين، والنصف الأول متناه؛ لكونه محصورا بين
Then it is surprising that some applied this to the motion of the celestial sphere because its parts are characterized by priority and posteriority, not knowing that motion is continuous and has no actual parts, and if they were content with its mental existence, it would apply to finite motions as well; because its parts are also infinite, and characterized by priority and posteriority, so the finitude of parts would be necessary, which is sophistry. This, and God knows best what is correct. And among them: that every infinite is subject to number, and every number is either even or odd. If it is even, then it is divided into two equal halves, and the first half is finite because it is bounded between...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage critiques a misapplication of the infinite arguments. Some applied the correlation proof to the motion of the celestial sphere, arguing that because its parts have priority and posteriority (like cause and effect), the motion must be finite. But the author points out that motion is continuous and not composed of actual parts; if one considers only mentally abstracted parts, even finite motions would have infinitely many such parts, making the argument invalid. Then the text presents another proof: every infinite is subject to number, and every number is either even or odd. If it were even, it could be divided into two equal halves, each of which would be finite (since bounded). This would imply the original infinite is finite, a contradiction. The argument is cut off, but likely continues to show that if it were odd, a similar contradiction arises. This parity argument is another way to prove that no actual infinite number exists. For example, if the number of past days were infinite, it would be either even or odd; but both lead to finitude, so the number cannot be infinite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the infinite argument fail when applied to the motion of the celestial sphere?
A) Because the motion is finite in duration.
B) Because motion is continuous and has no actual parts.
C) Because the celestial sphere is incorporeal.
D) Because the parts are infinite in number.

Key Takeaway
When evaluating arguments about infinite regress, always check whether the subject actually has discrete parts or is continuous; misapplication leads to sophistry.

The Parity Argument Against Actual Infinite
Author presents parity proof against actual infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the parity argument that an actual infinite number cannot be even or odd without contradiction, and thereby refute the existence of an actual infinite.

Core Idea
Every number is even or odd; an infinite would be both, a contradiction.

The author presents another proof: every infinite subject to number is either even or odd. If it were even, dividing it into two equal halves yields a finite first half, contradicting infinitude. (The odd case would similarly lead to contradiction, though the text is cut off.) This parity argument is a classic reductio proof that no actual infinite can exist, because any number must have a parity property that the infinite cannot satisfy.

Guided Path
1
State the assumption to be disproven
Assume that an actual infinite number exists (e.g., the number of past days). This number is subject to number, meaning it has a quantity.
The argument uses the assumption that the infinite is a number.
2
Apply the parity classification
Every number is either even or odd. So the assumed infinite number must be either even or odd. Consider the even case first.
The text only gives the even case; the odd case is symmetrical.
3
Analyze the even case: deduce finitude
If the number is even, it can be divided into two equal halves. The first half (from 1 to half) is bounded and therefore finite. But a finite half of an infinite whole is impossible, because the whole must be at least as large as its part.
This shows that the assumption of even leads to contradiction.
4
Conclude the reductio
Since the assumption that an actual infinite exists leads to a contradiction (it would be both infinite and have a finite part), the original assumption is false. Therefore, no actual infinite number can exist.
This is a standard argument in Kalam for the finitude of the past.

Commentary

Burhan al-Tada'uf: The Proof of Correlation Against Infinite Regress
Author presents the proof of correlation and critiques its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof of correlation as a conditional (modus tollens) argument and identify its key premise about the correlation of cause and effect.

Core Idea
The proof of correlation shows that an infinite causal chain leads to an effect without a cause, contradicting their necessary correlation.

The proof of correlation (burhan al-tada'uf) is one of the classic arguments used in Islamic kalam to refute the possibility of infinite regress (tasalsul). In this lesson, we will break down its logical structure and see how it relies on the necessary correlation between cause and effect. This argument is not just a theoretical exercise; it underpins theological proofs for a first cause, necessary being, and finitude of the universe.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The target is the possibility of an infinite regress of causes (tasalsul fi al-illah). The proof aims to show this is impossible.
Infinite regress is denied by most mutakallimun.
2
State the key principle
Cause and effect are correlatives (mutadha'ifan): each necessarily implies the other in existence. An effect cannot exist without a cause.
This is a metaphysical axiom used in the proof.
3
Formulate the conditional premise
If an infinite chain of causes existed, then the last effect would exist without a cause (since there is no first cause to account for the last effect's existence).
This follows from the definition of an infinite regress.
4
Apply modus tollens
The consequent (effect without cause) is impossible due to the correlation. Therefore the antecedent (infinite chain) must be false.
This is a valid conditional inference.
5
Verify the soundness
Check that the correlation is indeed necessary and that the conditional premise correctly captures the infinite regress scenario.
The author hints that the third premise needs order to be established first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان التضايف في إبطال شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم البرهان ، وهذا ظاهر في أن هذا البرهان غير مختص بالمرتب، ودليل المقدمة الثالثة يأبى عنه ، اللهم إلا أن يثبت الترتيب أولا ، فَتَدَبَّر)؛ فإن فيه كلاما ظاهرا ؛ فإن تضعيف غير المتناهي بحيث لا يختل ترتيبه محال، فكيف يبتنى عليه حكم التناهي، هذا ، والله أعلم. ومن الدلائل التي أقيمت على إبطال غير المتناهي برهان التضايف. تقريره لو ذهب سلسلة العلل لا إلى نهاية لوجد معلولية بلا علية، مع كونهما متضايفين. بيان الملازمة المعلول الأخير معلول وليس بعلة، والتي قبل علة ومعلول، فعدد المعلوليات أزيد من عدد العليات، وأما لو كانت متناهية فكما أن المعلول الأخير معلول غير علة ، كذلك العلة الأولى علة غير معلول، وهذا كما أنه يبطل التسلسل في المبدأ كذلك يبطل التسلسل في سلسلة المعلولات ؛ إذ يلزم ههنا وجود علية من غير معلولية، ففرق الصدر الشيرازي في «الأسفار بجريان هذا الدليل في التسلسل في المبدأ دون غيره تحكم. وإن شئت زيادة الكلام فاطلب من العجالة النافعة». ثم العجب إن البعض أجرى في حركة الفلك لاتصاف أجزائه بالسابقيات والمسبوقيات، ولم يعلموا أن الحركة متصلة وليس لها أجزاء بالفعل، ولو اكتفي بوجودها الانتزاعي لجرى في الحركات المتناهية؛ لأن أجزاءها غير متناهية أيضا، ومتصفة بالتقدم والتأخر فيلزم تناهي الأجزاء، وهو سفسطة ، هذا ، والله أعلم بالصواب.
The proof of correlation in refuting [infinity]. Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: the proof, and it is apparent that this proof is not specific to the ordered [series], and the evidence of the third premise contradicts it, unless the order is first established, so reflect; for it contains apparent speech. For the multiplication of the infinite such that its order is not disturbed is impossible, so how can the judgment of finitude be based upon it? This, and God knows best. And among the evidences established for refuting the infinite is the proof of correlation. Its formulation: if a chain of causes went on without end, then an effect would be found without a cause, while they are correlatives. Explanation of the implication: the last effect is an effect and not a cause, and the one before it is a cause and an effect, so the number of effects is greater than the number of causes. But if it were finite, then just as the last effect is an effect not a cause, likewise the first cause is a cause not an effect. And this, just as it refutes infinite regress in the beginning, likewise it refutes infinite regress in the chain of effects; for here would follow an existence of causality without being an effect. So al-Sadr al-Shirazi in 'al-Asfar' distinguished by applying this proof to infinite regress in the beginning but not elsewhere as arbitrary. And if you want more speech, ask from the useful work. Then it is surprising that some applied it to the motion of the sphere because its parts are characterized by prior and posterior, and they did not know that motion is continuous and has no actual parts, and if one suffices with its mental existence, it would apply to finite motions because their parts are also infinite and characterized by priority and posteriority, so finitude of parts would follow, and it is sophistry. This, and God knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the proof of correlation (burhan al-tada'uf) against infinite regress. The author begins by noting that the proof is not specific to causally ordered series and that there is an issue with the third premise unless order is first proven. Then the proof is formulated: if a chain of causes were infinite, there would be an effect without a cause (since the last effect is not a cause), but cause and effect are correlatives, so this is impossible. For a finite chain, the first cause is uncaused and the last effect is non-causal, balancing the numbers. The author then mentions that al-Sadr al-Shirazi criticized applying this proof only to the beginning of the chain as arbitrary. Finally, the author criticizes those who apply this proof to the motion of the celestial sphere, noting that motion is continuous without actual parts, so the proof does not apply; doing so leads to sophistry.

Key Terms
التضايف
تسلسل
علية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof of correlation, what is the impossible consequence of an infinite causal chain?
A) The first cause would have no effect.
B) The last effect would have no cause.
C) The chain would have no order.
D) Causes would be finite.

Key Takeaway
The proof of correlation refutes infinite regress by showing that an infinite causal chain would yield an effect without a cause, violating the necessary correlation between cause and effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Use the proof of correlation to argue against infinite regress in a causal chain of events. Identify the conditional premise and the denied consequent. Show that the conclusion follows.
?
Critically evaluate: Is the correlation between cause and effect truly necessary? Consider the possibility of simultaneous causation or pre-eternal causation. Does the proof still hold?

Critique of Misapplying Burhan al-Tada'uf to Motion and Restricted Chains
Author presents the proof of correlation and critiques its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the proof of correlation does not apply to continuous motion or to series lacking a directional order.

Core Idea
The proof of correlation requires actual discrete parts and a directional sequence; continuous motion and restricted chains fail these conditions.

Having understood the proof of correlation, we now examine the author's critique of those who misuse it. According to the passage, some philosophers (like al-Sadr al-Shirazi) restrict the proof only to the beginning of the causal chain, while others apply it to the motion of the celestial sphere. The author argues that such applications are invalid because they ignore the conditions required for the proof: actual parts and a clear direction. This lesson will help you avoid common fallacies in applying logical proofs to different domains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misapplications mentioned
The passage criticizes two misapplications: (a) al-Sadr al-Shirazi's restriction to the upward chain (causes) only, and (b) applying the proof to the motion of the celestial sphere.
Both are improper extensions of the proof.
2
Examine the first critique
The author says the proof works equally for effects (downward chain), so restricting it to causes is arbitrary (tahakkum). There is no logical basis for the distinction.
The proof refutes infinite regress in both directions.
3
Examine the second critique
The proof is applied to motion because its parts have priority and posteriority. But motion is continuous, not composed of actual discrete parts. Actual parts are required for the proof to apply.
If only mental existence of parts sufficed, the proof would also apply to finite motions, leading to the absurd conclusion that finite motions have infinite parts.
4
Determine the conditions for the proof
The proof requires actual discrete parts and a directional ordering (cause-effect or prior-posterior). Continuous phenomena lack actual parts.
The author calls the misapplication 'sophistry' (safsata).
5
Conclude the proper domain
The proof is valid only for genuinely discrete causal series. It should not be extended to continuous processes like motion.
This safeguards the rigor of logical proofs in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان التضايف في إبطال شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم البرهان ، وهذا ظاهر في أن هذا البرهان غير مختص بالمرتب، ودليل المقدمة الثالثة يأبى عنه ، اللهم إلا أن يثبت الترتيب أولا ، فَتَدَبَّر)؛ فإن فيه كلاما ظاهرا ؛ فإن تضعيف غير المتناهي بحيث لا يختل ترتيبه محال، فكيف يبتنى عليه حكم التناهي، هذا ، والله أعلم. ومن الدلائل التي أقيمت على إبطال غير المتناهي برهان التضايف. تقريره لو ذهب سلسلة العلل لا إلى نهاية لوجد معلولية بلا علية، مع كونهما متضايفين. بيان الملازمة المعلول الأخير معلول وليس بعلة، والتي قبل علة ومعلول، فعدد المعلوليات أزيد من عدد العليات، وأما لو كانت متناهية فكما أن المعلول الأخير معلول غير علة ، كذلك العلة الأولى علة غير معلول، وهذا كما أنه يبطل التسلسل في المبدأ كذلك يبطل التسلسل في سلسلة المعلولات ؛ إذ يلزم ههنا وجود علية من غير معلولية، ففرق الصدر الشيرازي في «الأسفار بجريان هذا الدليل في التسلسل في المبدأ دون غيره تحكم. وإن شئت زيادة الكلام فاطلب من العجالة النافعة». ثم العجب إن البعض أجرى في حركة الفلك لاتصاف أجزائه بالسابقيات والمسبوقيات، ولم يعلموا أن الحركة متصلة وليس لها أجزاء بالفعل، ولو اكتفي بوجودها الانتزاعي لجرى في الحركات المتناهية؛ لأن أجزاءها غير متناهية أيضا، ومتصفة بالتقدم والتأخر فيلزم تناهي الأجزاء، وهو سفسطة ، هذا ، والله أعلم بالصواب.

The Even-Odd Proof for Infinity
Author deploys a proof using even/odd classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the even-odd proof that an infinite magnitude is finite and identify its categorical syllogistic structure.

Core Idea
The even/odd argument attempts to prove infinity is impossible via number properties.

The author presents a demonstration that every infinite thing must be finite, using the properties of even and odd numbers. This argument is structured as a case-based categorical reasoning. Let's walk through its premises and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The author aims to prove that an infinite thing is finite. This is the target claim.
The conclusion is implicit: 'therefore the infinite is finite'.
2
Extract the first premise
Every infinite thing is subject to number (has a quantity that can be counted).
This links infinity to numerability.
3
Extract the second premise
Every number is either even or odd.
This is a disjunctive classification.
4
Analyze the cases
If even, it divides into two equal halves, each finite, so the whole is finite. If odd, adding or subtracting one makes it even, leading to the same conclusion.
The proof uses the finiteness of parts to infer finiteness of the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم إلى ضروري ونظري ومنها: أن كل غير متناه معروض لعدد، وكل عدد إما زوج أو فرد، فإن كان زوجا فينقسم بمتساويين، والنصف الأول متناه؛ لكونه محصورا بين المبدأ ومبدأ النصف للآخر ، وكذا الثاني للتساوي، وإن كان فردا فيزاد واحد أو ينقص، فيصير زوجا، وينساق الكلام واعترض الصدر الشيرازي بأن الزوجية والفردية من خواص العدد المتناهي. فإن قلت : هما سلب ومسلوب، فكيف يرتفعان من الموجود ؟ قلت ممنوع، ولو سلم فحينئذ نختار أن غير المتناهي متصف بعدم الانقسام إلى متساويين ولا يصير بزيادة واحد أو حطه منقسما بمتساويين ؛ فإن أحد قسميه ناقص من الآخر بمراتب غير متناهية، فلا تكون الناقص مساويا للزائد بزيادة ،واحد ولا الزائد مساويا للناقص بنقصان ،واحد، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Division into necessary and theoretical. And among them: that every infinite thing is subject to number, and every number is either even or odd. If it is even, it divides into two equal halves, and the first half is finite because it is bounded between the beginning and the beginning of the other half, and likewise the second due to equality. And if it is odd, then add one or subtract one, it becomes even, and the discourse follows. And al-Sadr al-Shirazi objected that evenness and oddness are properties of finite number. If you say: they are negation and negated, so how can they be absent from the existent? I say: it is denied, and if conceded, then we choose that the infinite is characterized by not dividing into two equals and does not become divisible into two equals by adding or subtracting one; for one of its parts is deficient from the other by infinite degrees, so the deficient is not equal to the excess by adding one, nor the excess equal to the deficient by subtracting one. This, and God knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that an infinite magnitude must be finite based on even/odd properties. The proof runs: every infinite thing has a number; every number is either even or odd. If even, it divides into two equal halves, each bounded and thus finite. If odd, adding or subtracting one makes it even, and the same argument applies. Al-Sadr al-Shirazi objects that even/odd are properties of finite numbers only. The author responds that even if conceded, the infinite is characterized by inability to divide into equal halves, and even with addition/subtraction, the parts are infinitely unequal, so no equality results. Thus the proof fails.

Key Terms

فرد
متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what happens if the number of an infinite thing is odd?
A) It remains odd.
B) It becomes even by adding or subtracting one.
C) It cannot be determined.
D) It is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The even-odd proof attempts to show that infinity leads to contradiction, but it relies on applying finite properties to the infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the even-odd proof as a categorical syllogism by identifying the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion. Then assess whether the conclusion necessarily follows from the premises. Write your analysis in one paragraph.

Al-Sadr al-Shirazi's Objection and Response
Objection and response regarding the even/odd proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze al-Sadr al-Shirazi's objection to the even-odd proof and the author's counter-response.

Core Idea
The objection that even/odd are finite properties undermines the proof's validity.

Al-Sadr al-Shirazi raises a critical objection: evenness and oddness are properties of finite numbers only, so the proof cannot apply to infinite magnitudes. The author offers a response, but the objection reveals a fundamental limitation of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Al-Sadr al-Shirazi claims that evenness and oddness are properties of finite numbers, so the proof's premises are inapplicable to the infinite.
This is a conceptual objection about the domain of the properties.
2
Understand the hypothetical counter
The author preempts a possible reply: 'if you say they are negation and negated, how can they be absent from the existent?'
This refers to the idea that even/odd are negative attributes (not even = odd).
3
Trace the author's response
The author denies that objection, and if it were conceded, he chooses to characterize the infinite by its inability to divide equally, even with addition or subtraction.
The author shifts the argument from even/odd to a direct property of inequality.
4
Evaluate the response
Does the author successfully rebut the objection? The response seems to admit that even/odd don't apply, but then asserts a different property (infinite inequality) without proof.
The objection highlights a weakness: the original proof assumes infinity has a number, which is not self-evident.

The 'Arshī Proof and the Fallacy of Composition
Author presents a proof and its objection regarding the fallacy of composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of composition in proofs from whole to parts and distinguish it from valid first-figure categorical syllogisms in Islamic theological arguments.

Core Idea
A whole's finitude does not automatically follow from its parts' finitude if the whole is collective.

In this passage, al-Suhrawardi presents a proof that an infinite whole composed of finite parts must be finite. He immediately notes that this proof risks the fallacy of composition unless it is a valid first-figure syllogism. We will dissect this proof, identify the fallacy, and see how later commentators debated its status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its reasoning
Locate the main claim: 'every whole composed of them is finite because of boundedness, so the whole is finite.' Al-Suhrawardi calls this an 'arshī proof. Recognize that the reasoning moves from parts to whole.
The term 'arshī suggests a proof derived from intuition, not scripture.
2
Extract the syllogistic form
Consider the defense: 'the infinite is a whole, and every whole is finite.' Write this as a categorical syllogism: Major premise: Every whole is finite. Minor premise: The infinite is a whole. Conclusion: The infinite is finite. This is Barbara (first figure).
The validity of the syllogism depends on the terms being used distributively, not collectively.
3
Recognize the fallacy of composition
The objection says it is 'measuring the collective whole by the individual parts.' The fallacy occurs when a property true of each part individually is attributed to the whole collectively. Here, each part is finite, but the whole may be infinite if it is a sum of an infinite number of finite parts.
This is a classic example of the fallacy of composition (mughalata al-kull al-majmu'i).
4
Evaluate the defense against the fallacy
Al-Suhrawardi suggests that if the proof is a valid first-figure syllogism, it avoids the fallacy. But for that, the term 'whole' must be distributive (each whole is finite) and the infinite must be a distributive whole. Is the infinite a distributive whole? Not necessarily. The defense fails if 'whole' is taken collectively.
Later commentators discuss whether the 'arshī proof is sound or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن كل جملة منها متناهية لأجل المحصورية، فالكل متناه. قال الشيخ المقتول : هو برهان عرشي ، وإلا فيرد عليه أنه قياس للكل المجموعي على الأفرادي. فإن قلت : بل استدلال بالشكل الأول، بأن غير المتناهي جملة، وكل جملة متناهية ؟ (۱) نقل هذا الدليل صاحب المواقف، ونسبه السيد الشريف إلى صاحب الإشراق. انظر شرح المواقف للسيد الشريف: ١٧٣/٤ - ١٧٦. قال عبد الحكيم في حاشية شرح المواقف (١٧٥/٤): في شرح التلويحات هذان اللفظان أعني العرشي واللو. استعملهما في عدة مواضع من هذا الكتاب، ولم يبين مراد منهما، ولعل مراده بالعرشي البحث الذي حصله بنفسه، وباللوحي وباللوحي ما أخذه من الكتاب». وأنا لم أجده في منطق التلويحات الذي اطلعت عليه ولا أعرف له كتابا باسم شرح التلويحات، ورأيت هذا البرهان في كتاب آخر له يسمى حكمة الإشراق (٦٣، ٦٤) ، ولكن ليس فيه تسميته برهانا عرشيا»، والله أعلم.
And among them: that every whole [composed] of them is finite because of boundedness, so the whole is finite. The slain master [al-Suhrawardi] said: it is an 'arshī proof, otherwise it would be objected that it is a measuring of the collective whole by the individual parts. If you say: rather it is a deduction in the first figure: that the infinite is a whole, and every whole is finite? (1) This proof was transmitted by the author of al-Mawaqif, and al-Sayyid al-Sharif attributed it to the author of al-Ishraq. See Sharh al-Mawaqif by al-Sayyid al-Sharif: 4/173-176. Abd al-Hakim said in the margin of Sharh al-Mawaqif (4/175): 'In Sharh al-Talwihat, these two words, namely 'arshī and lawhī, are used in several places of this book, and he did not clarify his meaning from them, and perhaps his meaning by 'arshī is the research he obtained by himself, and by lawhī is what he took from the book.' And I did not find it in the logic of al-Talwihat that I examined, and I do not know of a book of his called Sharh al-Talwihat, and I saw this proof in another book of his called Hikmat al-Ishraq (pp. 63-64), but it is not called 'arshī proof in it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that an infinite whole composed of finite parts must be finite because each part is bounded. Al-Suhrawardi (the slain master) calls this an 'arshī proof, but notes that otherwise it would be objected that it commits the fallacy of composition (measuring the collective whole by the individual parts). The author then considers a defense: that it is a valid first-figure syllogism: the infinite is a whole, and every whole is finite. The footnote traces the origin of this proof and the term 'arshī, citing al-Mawaqif and al-Ishraq.

Key Terms
جملة (jumla): whole or aggregate, especially a collective whole composed of parts.
برهان عرشي (burhan 'arshi): A proof attributed to al-Suhrawardi; the term 'arshī may indicate it is derived from intuition rather than scripture.
قياس للكل المجموعي على الأفرادي (qiyas li'l-kull al-majmu'i 'ala al-afradi): The fallacy of composition: inferring a property of the whole from properties of its parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the potential fallacy in the proof that a whole is finite if its parts are finite?
A) Affirming the consequent
B) Fallacy of composition (measuring the collective whole by the individual parts)
C) Begging the question
D) False analogy

Key Takeaway
The 'arshī proof illustrates that moving from parts to whole requires careful attention to whether the whole is distributive or collective, otherwise the fallacy of composition invalidates the argument.

The Strongest Proof against Infinite Regress of Causes
Author presents the strongest proof refuting infinite regress of causes based on necessity and possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the modus tollens argument against infinite regress of causes and explain why the possibility of the chain's non-existence refutes the regress.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of causes is impossible because it would require a necessity that cannot be sustained.

In this passage, the author presents what he calls the 'most complete proof' against the possibility of an infinite regress of causes (tasalsul al-ʿilal). The proof is a conditional argument that hinges on the notions of necessity (wujūb) and possibility (imkān). By understanding this logical structure, you will grasp why the mutakallimūn considered an actual infinite chain of causes impossible, a key premise in arguments for the existence of a First Cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The author begins with 'if they regress, it would necessitate that something of them exist'. This sets up a conditional: if infinite regress (antecedent), then something in the chain exists necessarily (consequent).
2
Understand the justification of the consequent
Existence without necessity is impossible, and necessity implies that all possible non-existence is impossible. So for anything to exist necessarily, its non-existence must be inconceivable.
This connects to the concept of wujūb dhātī (necessary essence).
3
Recognize the denial of the consequent
The author then claims that in the case of an infinite chain, the entire chain's non-existence is possible. This is because the Necessary Being (al-Wājib) is not a sustaining cause (ʿilla kāfila) for the chain.
Possible non-existence means necessity is absent.
4
Apply modus tollens
If the consequent (necessary existence of something in the chain) is false (since the whole chain could be non-existent), then the antecedent (infinite regress) must also be false. Thus, infinite regress is impossible.
This is a valid logical form: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الدليل الأتم على إبطال تسلسل العلل أنه لو تسلسلت لزم أن يوجد شيء منها؛ لامتناع الوجود بلا وجوب، والوجوب لا يتحقق إلا إذا امتنع جميع أنحاء العدم، وههنا ارتفاع السلسلة بأسرها ممكن؛ لعدم كون الواجب علة كافلة لها، وقد مر تقريره في أوائل الكتاب أيضا. هذا، والله أعلم.
And know that the most complete proof for the invalidity of the regress of causes is that if they regress, it would necessitate that something of them exist; because existence is impossible without necessity, and necessity is not realized unless all aspects of non-existence are impossible, and here the removal of the entire chain is possible; due to the Necessary not being a sustaining cause for it. And its exposition has passed in the beginning of the book as well. This, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents the strongest argument against infinite regress of causes. The proof is conditional: if there were an infinite regress of causes, then something among them must exist necessarily. However, existence is impossible without necessity, and necessity only obtains when all possibilities of non-existence are impossible. Yet, in an infinite chain, it is possible for the entire chain to be non-existent because the Necessary Being (al-Wājib) is not a sustaining cause (ʿilla kāfila) for it. Therefore, infinite regress is impossible. The author notes that this proof was already given earlier in the book.

Key Terms
تسلسل العلل
الوجوب
علة كافلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) An infinite chain of causes.
B) The impossibility of all aspects of non-existence.
C) The existence of a sustaining cause for the entire chain.
D) The regress of causes being finite.

Key Takeaway
The most complete proof against infinite regress of causes is a conditional argument that shows an infinite chain would require a necessary existence, but since the entire chain is possibly non-existent, necessity is absent, so regress is impossible.

Why Logic is Necessary for Perpetual Certainty
Author refutes an objection by arguing logic is necessary for perpetual certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical argument that logic is necessary for perpetual certainty and explain the role of universals.

Core Idea
Perpetual certainty of particulars requires universals, which only logic guarantees.

Some object that since we have self-evident knowledge, logic is unnecessary. The author responds by clarifying that demonstration aims at perpetual certainty (al-yaqīn al-dā’im), which demands certainty in both the path of reasoning and the terms used. He then invokes a fundamental principle from the art of demonstration: perpetual certainty about particulars comes only through universal knowledge. Therefore, logic—the universal law (qānūn) of correct reasoning—is genuinely necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being refuted
The passage begins by acknowledging the division of knowledge into necessary and theoretical. An unnamed objector claims that some knowledge is immediate, so logic may be superfluous. Notice how the author frames this as a challenge to the need for logic.
The author does not name the objector; this is a common dialectical technique.
2
Extract the author's premise about demonstration
The author states that the purpose of demonstration (burhān) is to obtain perpetual certainty. What is sought (al-matlūb) depends on perpetual certainty about the validity of its path and the conceptualisation of its terms. This links logic directly to certainty.
Burhān here is used broadly for any proof yielding certainty.
3
Recognise the principle about particulars and universals
The author asserts a key principle: perpetual certainty of particulars is only obtained from universals (al-kulliyyāt). This is a claim from the art of demonstration. Understanding this step is crucial: it means that to be certain about a particular case (e.g., a specific act is obligatory), one must appeal to a universal law.
This principle is foundational in both logic and fiqh.
4
Connect universals to logic
Since the need is for the validity of the path leading to what is sought, and that path depends on knowledge of the universal that is the law (al-qānūn), logic is that law. The author concludes that the need for logic is a real need (ḥāja ḥaqīqiyya).
The 'law' here refers to the rules of logic that safeguard reasoning.
5
Analyse the concessive closing
The author adds that if we lower our standard (tanazzulnā), we might concede the objection. But since the only safeguard (al-ʿāṣim) is this law, we attribute the need to it. This shows the argument is rigorous: logic is necessary for the highest level of certainty.
This concession reinforces the strength of the original position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألا التقسيم إلى ضروري ونظري تری أن الاستحالة الصورة (۱) الجسمية الغير المتناهية لا يوجب احتياج الهيولى إلى المتناهي منها، ولا يبعد أن يجاب بأن المقصود من البرهان تحصيل اليقين الدائم، والمطلوب هو موقوف على اليقين الدائم بصحة طريقه، وصحة طرق تصور أطرافه. وقد تقرر في فن البرهان أن العلم ـ أي اليقين الدائم - بالجزئيات لا يحصل إلا من جهة الكليات، والحاجة لم يكن إلا إلى صحة الطريق الذي هو للمطلوب، وهو موقوف على العلم بالكلي الذي هو القانون، فثبت الحاجة إلى المنطق في الجملة حاجة حقيقية. ولو تنزلنا سلمنا ما قال ،المورد لكن لما كان العاصم منحصرا في القانون نسبنا إليه ،الحاجة، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Behold, the division into necessary and theoretical: you see that the impossibility of the infinite corporeal form does not necessitate the need of the matter for the infinite of it, and it is not far that it be answered that the purpose of demonstration is to obtain perpetual certainty, and what is sought depends on perpetual certainty of the validity of its path, and the validity of the paths of conceiving its terms. It has been established in the art of demonstration that knowledge – meaning perpetual certainty – of particulars is only obtained from the perspective of universals, and the need was only for the validity of the path which is for what is sought, and it depends on knowledge of the universal which is the law, so the need for logic in summary is a real need. And if we descend we concede what the objector said, but since the safeguard is limited to the law, we attribute need to it. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical reply to an objection regarding the necessity of logic. The author begins by acknowledging the division of knowledge into necessary (self-evident) and theoretical (acquired). He then addresses the objection that if some knowledge is immediate, logic may be unnecessary. His response is that demonstration aims at perpetual certainty (al-yaqīn al-dā’im), which requires certainty in the validity of the reasoning path and the terms. In the art of demonstration, it is established that perpetual certainty of particulars comes only through universals. Therefore, the need for logic—the law (qānūn) that ensures correct reasoning—is real. He concedes that if we lower our standard, we might accept the objection, but since the only safeguard is this law, the need for it remains.

Key Terms
اليقين الدائم
البرهان
الكليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because logic is required for all knowledge, even self-evident truths.
B) Because perpetual certainty about particulars depends on universals, which are provided by logic.
C) Because the objection itself is false.
D) Because logic is the only source of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Perpetual certainty about particular cases requires universal knowledge, and logic provides the universal framework that safeguards correct reasoning.

?

Critique of Peripatetic Divine Knowledge
Critique of Peripatetic divine knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Peripatetic claim that God knows particulars universally and refute it using the causal principle that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect as particular.

Core Idea
God knows each particular as particular because He is its cause; universal knowledge alone is not knowledge of the particular.

The Peripatetic philosophers faced a dilemma: how can God, who is immaterial, know changing material particulars? They proposed that God knows particulars in a universal manner. However, religious scholars (al-milliyyun) objected that this contradicts the principle that knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect. Since God is the cause of each particular as particular, He must know it as such. This lesson unpacks the Peripatetic claim, the religious critique, and the author's refutation of attempts to rescue the Peripatetic view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Peripatetic position
Find the sentence where the Peripatetics state that God knows particulars in a universal way, not a particular way. Note their reason: they considered God's knowledge to be acquired (حصولي) and were concerned about the acquisition of changing particulars.
The key phrase is 'يعلم الجزئيات على الوجه الكلي دون الجزئي'.
2
Extract the religious objection
The religious scholars (المليون) object that universal knowledge invalidates the causal principle: if God is the cause of each particular, He must know it as a particular. Locate this objection in the passage.
They say: 'ذلك يبطل قولهم: العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول'.
3
Analyze the first rejoinder and its refutation
Some commentators argued that knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect only if the cause is known from all aspects. The author refutes this: the particular is an effect as particular, so knowledge must be particular. Find this refutation.
The author says the rejoinder is 'ساقط' (invalid) because the particular as particular is an effect.
4
Analyze the second rejoinder and its refutation
Another commentator claimed that knowledge of the cause from all aspects would entail knowledge of the effect, but they distinguish between presential and acquired knowledge of God. The author refutes that God's knowledge of His essence is presential, not limited by aspects, and thus He must know things as they are.
The author argues: either God knows His essence with all aspects (then He knows all) or not (ignorance).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال هذا تأويل ما اشتهر من الحكماء من نفي علم الواجب بالجزئيات على الوجه الجزئي فافهم وتفصيل المقام: إن المشائين لما رأوا علمه تعالى حصوليا وقد كانوا يبالون عن حصول الجزئيات المتغيرة في المجرد قالوا: إنه - الله عما يقوله الظالمون - يعلم الجزئيات على الوجه الكلي دون الجزئي، وشنع عليهم المليون بأن ذلك يبطل قولهم: العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول ؛ فإنه سبحانه جاعل لجميع الممكنات. وما قيل: إن العلم بالعلة إنما يستلزم العلم بالمعلول، إذا كان العلم بالعلة من جميع الجهات ، والله الا الله جاعل لجميع الممكنات في الجملة، لا من جميع الجهات فساقط ؛ لأن الجزئي من حيث إنه جزئي معلول له سبحانه، فيجب أن يتعلق به العلم كذلك، وكذا ما قال بعض الشراح: إن العلم بالعلة من جميع الجهات يستلزم العلم بالمعلول، فإن أرادوا بعلمه بذاته علمه الحضوري فالحضور عنده لذاته ، لا من جميع جهاته ، وإن أرادوا علمه الحصولي فظاهر أن علمه إنما هو بذاته حضوري ؛ لأنه تعالى إما عالم بذاته مع جميع جهاته، فلا بد من أن يعلم المعلومات كما هي أنفسها ، أو لا، وهو تجويز الجهل ، تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا وبأن العلم بالوجه الكلي علم بالكلي حقيقة ، ونسبته إلى جميع الجزئيات على السواء، فلا يكون علما بالجزئي. وما قال بعض الشارحين إنما يلزم ذلك لو لم يكن ذلك الوجه منحصرا
Then he said: This is the interpretation of what is well-known from the philosophers of denying that the Necessary Being knows particulars in a particular manner, so understand. And the detailed discussion: When the Peripatetics saw that His knowledge is acquired and they were concerned about the acquisition of changing particulars in the immaterial, they said: He – exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say – knows particulars in a universal manner, not particular. And the religious scholars rebuked them because that invalidates their statement: knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect; for He is the Maker of all possibles. And what was said: that knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect only if knowledge of the cause is from all aspects, and God is the Maker of all possibles in general, not from all aspects, is invalid; because the particular insofar as it is particular is an effect of Him, so it is necessary that knowledge should attach to it likewise. And likewise what some commentators said: that knowledge of the cause from all aspects entails knowledge of the effect – if they mean by His knowledge of His essence His presential knowledge, then presentiality for Him is of His essence, not from all His aspects; and if they mean His acquired knowledge, then it is obvious that His knowledge is presential by essence; because He either knows His essence with all its aspects, then He must know things as they are themselves, or not, and that is allowing ignorance – exalted is He above that greatly. And that knowledge in a universal manner is knowledge of the universal in truth, and its relation to all particulars is equal, so it is not knowledge of the particular. And what some commentators said: that it only follows if that aspect is not exclusive [to this particular]...
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage critically examines the Peripatetic (المشائين) position that God knows particulars only in a universal manner. The religious scholars (المليون) object that this contradicts the causal principle that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. Since God is the cause of each particular as a particular, He must know it as such. The author refutes two rejoinders: (1) that knowledge of the cause requires knowledge from all aspects (but God's knowledge is presential, not limited by aspects), and (2) that universal knowledge is knowledge of the universal only, not of the particular. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the author clearly sides with the religious critique, affirming that God knows particulars as particulars.

علم حصولي
علم حضوري
المليون

A) Because universal knowledge is more perfect than particular knowledge
B) Because if God is the cause of every particular, He must know it as a particular
C) Because the Peripatetics denied God's existence
D) Because particulars are too lowly for divine knowledge

Key Takeaway
The causal principle forces the conclusion that God knows each particular as particular; universal knowledge alone is not sufficient for divine omniscience.

Refuting an objection about the partner of God and composition
Author responds to an objection about a premise involving a partner of God and the modal status of composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical fallacy in an objection that conflates composition with possibility in theological contexts, and explain how the verifying commentator resolves it by distinguishing between the criterion of possibility (composition) and the particularity of parts (necessitating impossibility).

Core Idea
Composition alone does not entail possibility; particularities can necessitate impossibility, avoiding contradiction.

In this passage, the author faces an objection: if the prepared premise is false, then a partner of God would be both possible and impossible. The verifying commentator (al-Sharīḥ al-Muḥaqqiq) answers by distinguishing between the composition itself and the particularity of the components. This lesson will walk you through the objection, the logical structure of the argument, and the commentator's critical refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's premise
The objection assumes that if the prepared premise is false, then the aggregate of two partners of God would itself be a partner of God. This is because the prepared premise states something like 'every composite is possible' and its converse. Find this in the Arabic: 'لَا يُقَالُ: المقدمة الممهدة باطلة وإلا فَمَجْمُوعُ شَرِيكَيْ الْبَارِي شَرِيكُ الْبَارِي بحكم المقدمة الممهدة'.
The 'prepared premise' here is likely 'every composite is possible' and 'every possible is composite' (implied).
2
Trace the syllogistic chain leading to contradiction
The objection deduces: some partner of God is composite; every composite is possible (because in need); therefore some partner of God is possible. But theologically every partner of God is impossible. So a contradiction arises. Spot the inference: 'فَبَعْضُ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُرَكَّبٌ، وَكُلُّ مُرَكَّب مُمْكِنُ'.
The syllogism is valid in form, but the premises are challenged.
3
Analyze the commentator's distinction
The verifying commentator answers: 'مناط الإمكان نفس التركيب، لا خصوص الجزئين' – the criterion of possibility is the very composition, not the particularity of the two parts. This means that while composition gives possibility, the particularity of the parts can cause impossibility. Thus possibility and impossibility arise from different aspects, so no real contradiction.
This is a modal distinction: a composite can be possible qua composite but impossible qua its specific components.
4
Apply the distinction to the objection
The objection wrongly assumed that if a thing is composite, it must be possible in every respect. The commentator shows that the particular composition (being a partner of God) makes it impossible, so the composite is impossible from one aspect and possible from another. Therefore the premise 'every composite is possible' is not absolute but conditional on the nature of composition itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا يُقَالُ: المقدمة الممهدة باطلة وإلا فَمَجْمُوعُ شَرِيكَيْ الْبَارِي شَرِيكُ الْبَارِي بحكم المقدمة الممهدة، فَبَعْضُ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُرَكَّبٌ، وَكُلُّ مُرَكَّب مُمْكِنُ؛ لأن كل مركب محتاج، وكل محتاج ممكن، فَبَعْضُ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْكِنٌ العياذ بالله، مَعَ أَنَّ كُلَّ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْتَنع. أجاب الشارح المحقق بأن مناط الإمكان نفس التركيب، لا خصوص الجزئين، فيجوز أن يكون الخصوصية موجبة للامتناع، فالإمكان والامتناع من جهتين. قال بعض الشراح يمكن دفعه بضابطة وضعها بعض أجلة المتأخرين: أن كل ما هو لازم للطبيعة من حيث هو لازم للفرد، ثبوتا كان أو سلبا، وكل ما هو للفرد من السلوب البسيطة فهو للطبيعة؛ إذ لولاه لكان المسلوب ثابتا لها، فكان ثابتا للفرد فيلزم اجتماع السلب والمسلوب في شيء واحد، اهـ. فالإمكان لما كان لازما لسنخ التركيب يكون لخصوص الفرد أيضا ثابتا، فيلزم اجتماع الإمكان والامتناع في شيء واحد والامتناع لما كان لخصوص التركيب كان لمطلقه أيضا ثابتا فيلزم اجتماع الإمكان والامتناع، اهـ. ولا يخفى أن هذا بناء على الفاسد؛ فإن أكثر لوازم الماهية ممتنعة الثبوت للفرد ألا ترى أن الوجوب ثابت لطبيعة أحد النقيضين، دون أفراده، وكذلك أكثر السلوب للفرد مع امتناعها للطبيعة. فإن قلت من الضروري أن ما هو ثابت للفرد ثابت للطبيعة، كما سيأتي من المصنف، إن شاء الله تعالى وإن لم يكن له عكس، والامتناع للفرد فيه، فيسري إلى الطبيعة فيلزم اجتماعهما فيها؟ قلت القدر الضروري والمسلم ثبوت الثابت للأفراد للطبيعة، ولو بالعرض، فلا مضايقة في اجتماعهما لثبوت الامتناع بالعرض، والإمكان بالذات، والله أعلم.
It cannot be said: The prepared premise is false; otherwise the aggregate of the two partners of God would be a partner of God by the ruling of the prepared premise. So some partner of God is composite, and every composite is possible, because every composite is in need, and everything in need is possible. So some partner of God is possible—God forbid—while every partner of God is impossible. The verifying commentator answered: The criterion of possibility is the very composition, not the particularity of the two parts, so it is permissible that the particularity necessitates impossibility. Thus possibility and impossibility are from two aspects. Some commentators said: It can be refuted by a principle formulated by some of the later eminent scholars: Everything that is necessary for the nature insofar as it is necessary for the individual, whether positive or negative, and every simple negative that is for the individual is also for the nature; because otherwise the negated would be established for it, so it would be established for the individual, leading to the conjunction of negation and negated in one thing. So since possibility is necessary for the kind of composition, it is also established for the particular individual, leading to the conjunction of possibility and impossibility in one thing. And since impossibility is for the particular composition, it is also established for its absolute, leading to the conjunction of possibility and impossibility. It is not hidden that this is based on the corrupt; for most properties of the quiddity are impossible to be established for the individual. Do you not see that necessity is established for the nature of one of the two contradictories, but not for its individuals? Similarly, most negatives are for the individual while impossible for the nature. If you say: It is necessary that what is established for the individual is established for the nature, as will come from the author, God willing, even if it does not have a converse, and impossibility is for the individual in it, so it transmits to the nature, leading to their conjunction in it? I say: The necessary and conceded amount is the establishment of what is established for individuals to the nature, even if accidentally, so there is no harm in their conjunction because impossibility is established accidentally and possibility essentially. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of an objection concerning a logical premise and its implications for the concept of a partner of God (شريك الباري). The author presents an objection: if the prepared premise were false, then the aggregate of two partners of God would itself be a partner of God. This leads to the conclusion that some partner of God is composite, and since every composite is possible (due to being in need), some partner of God would be possible, which contradicts the impossibility of any partner of God. The verifying commentator (الشارح المحقق) answers by distinguishing the criterion of possibility: it is the composition itself, not the particular components. The particularities of the components may necessitate impossibility, so the composite could be impossible from one aspect and possible from another, thus avoiding contradiction. Another commentator offers a principle from later scholars: any attribute that is necessary for the nature is necessary for the individual, but this principle is criticized as corrupt because many attributes of the quiddity are not realized in individuals (e.g., necessity for the nature of one of two contradictories is not in its individuals). A further objection notes that what is established for the individual should be established for the nature. The answer clarifies that the established attribute is transferred to the nature only accidentally, so no real contradiction arises between possibility (essential) and impossibility (accidental). The entire discussion guards against conflating essential and accidental modalities in theological contexts.

Key Terms
المقدمة الممهدة
شريك الباري
الإمكان
الامتناع
التركيب
خصوص الجزئين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the verifying commentator's answer, what determines whether a composite is possible or impossible?
A) The fact that it is composite alone
B) The particularity of its parts
C) Both composition and the particularity of parts together
D) The need of the composite for its parts

Key Takeaway
Composition gives a presumption of possibility, but the specific nature of the components can override it with impossibility, allowing a composite to be both possible and impossible from different aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following claim: 'The world is composite, and every composite is temporally originated (hadith), so the world is temporally originated.' An objector says: 'But the world is also the creation of God, and every creation of God is eternal in knowledge, so the world is both originated and eternal.' Using the commentator's distinction, explain how the world can be originated from one aspect and eternal from another without contradiction.

The nature–individual principle and modal consistency
Author critiques a principle about nature and individual, using the partner of God example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the principle that 'what is necessary for the nature is necessary for the individual' and identify its limitations using the example of necessity for the nature of one of two contradictories not applying to its individuals.

Core Idea
What holds for the nature does not always hold for the individual; necessity of nature differs from necessity of individual.

The passage continues with a principle proposed by later scholars: every property necessary for the nature is necessary for the individual, and simple negatives for the individual are also for the nature. The author challenges this principle by pointing to counterexamples from modal logic and theology. We will dissect this critical refinement to understand the relationship between universal natures and their instances.

Guided Path
1
State the principle under discussion
The principle: 'كل ما هو لازم للطبيعة من حيث هو لازم للفرد، ثبوتا كان أو سلبا، وكل ما هو للفرد من السلوب البسيطة فهو للطبيعة' – everything necessary for the nature insofar as it is necessary for the individual (positive or negative), and every simple negative for the individual is also for the nature. Locate this in the Arabic.
This is an attempt to ensure consistency between universal and particular modal properties.
2
Identify the counterexample given
The author provides a counterexample: 'الوجوب ثابت لطبيعة أحد النقيضين، دون أفراده' – necessity is established for the nature of one of the two contradictories, but not for its individuals. For example, the nature of 'being a contradiction' (e.g., 'either A or not A') is necessary in itself, but any specific case (e.g., 'this statement is true') is not necessary.
This shows that a property can be necessary for the nature but not for the individual.
3
Analyze the objection that a property of the individual transmits to the nature
An objector argues: if impossibility is established for the individual (a partner of God), then it must also be established for the nature (the composite partner of God), leading to both possibility and impossibility for the same nature. The author counters: 'القدر الضروري والمسلم ثبوت الثابت للأفراد للطبيعة، ولو بالعرض' – what is established for the individual is transmitted to the nature only accidentally (bil-'arad), not essentially.
Thus possibility remains essential to the nature qua composite, while impossibility is accidental from the particularity.
4
Synthesize the refined principle
The final position: properties essential to the nature (like possibility for composition) are essential to the individual; properties accidental to the nature (like impossibility for a specific composite) are only accidentally in the nature. So no contradiction arises because they are different modes of attribution.

What counterexample does the author give to refute the principle that what is necessary for the nature is necessary for the individual?

Can the Possible Entail the Impossible? A Dialectical Analysis
Author discusses whether the possible can entail the impossible and replies to an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection and reply in a dialectical exchange about whether a possible can entail an impossible, and identify the role of intermediaries and temporal priority.

Core Idea
A possible can entail an impossible through intermediaries, provided there is no temporal priority.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical exchange about a crucial principle: can a contingent being (mumkin) entail an impossibility (muhal)? An objector argues that if it does, it is only accidental. The author replies that it is essential, using the concept of intermediaries and a refinement about priority and posteriority. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to evaluate causal entailments in kalam and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and objection
Locate the author's initial statement: the non-existence of the possible effect is entailed by the non-existence of the Necessary (impossible). Then find the objection: 'then the impossible is not necessary for the possible in essence, but accidentally, and there is no harm.' Understand the challenge.
The objector tries to defuse the argument by claiming the entailment is merely accidental.
2
Follow the author's reply and analysis of intermediaries
The author insists the entailment is in essence, because intermediaries mediate in subsistence. He then splits the case: some intermediaries are impossible, some possible. In both scenarios, the possible ends up entailing the impossible. Trace this reasoning step by step.
This shows how essential entailment can pass through a chain of causes.
3
Understand the refinement about priority and posteriority
Some scholars add a condition: an antecedent only becomes impossible when it entails an impossible if there is no temporal priority (taqaddum) or posteriority (ta'akhkhur) between them. The example given is the non-division of an essentially extended substance entailing its division. Analyze this condition.
This refinement prevents over-application of the principle.
4
Apply the dialectical structure to a kalam debate
Consider how the objector might respond further. The author asserts this specification is not arbitrary but required by investigation. Reflect on how dialectical rules guide such exchanges.
This step connects the logical analysis to the broader munazara tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدم الواجب ، وهي ملزومة لنفس العدم ؛ لأن وصف الجاعلية ضروري لما هو جاعل ، كما نص عليه الشيخ ، فلزم أن يكون عدم المعلول الممكن ملزوما لعدم الواجب المحال . فإن قلت: فعلى هذا لا يكون المحال لازما للمكن بالذات، بل بالعرض ، ولا ضير فيه ؟ قلت : لا ، بل بالذات إنما الوسائط وسائط في الثبوت ؛ على ما يشهد به الضرورة، وأيضا شيء من الوسائط محال بالذات، أو كلها ممكنات، وعلى كلا التقديرين استلزام الممكن المحال . أما على الأول: فلأن الممكن يستلزم الوسائط المحالة، وأما على الثاني: فلأن الواسطة الممكنة استلزمت محالا ، وبعضهم لصعوبة هذا الإيراد خصصوا، وقالوا المستلزم للمحال إنما يصير محالا ، إذا لم يكن بينهما تقدم وتأخر كاستلزام عدم انقسام الجوهر المتحيز بالذات لانقسامه، وليس هذا تخصيصا كتخصيصات العلوم الأدبية ؛ لأن الفحص يقتضي ذلك، كما لا يخفى، والله أعلم.
The non-existence of the Necessary, and it is entailed by non-existence itself; because the attribute of making is necessary for that which is a maker, as the Shaykh stated, so it follows that the non-existence of the possible effect is entailed by the non-existence of the impossible Necessary. If you say: accordingly, the impossible is not necessary for the possible in essence, but accidentally, and there is no harm in that? I say: No, rather in essence, the intermediaries are intermediaries in subsistence, as attested by necessity, and also some of the intermediaries are impossible in essence, or all of them are possible, and in both cases the possible entails the impossible. As for the first: because the possible entails the impossible intermediaries, and as for the second: because the possible intermediary entailed an impossible. And some, due to the difficulty of this objection, specified and said: that which entails the impossible only becomes impossible if there is no priority and posteriority between them, like the entailment of the non-division of the substance that is essentially extended to its division. This is not a specification like the specifications of the literary sciences, because investigation requires it, as is not hidden, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange commenting on a previous claim about the non-existence of the Necessary (God) and the possible. The author begins by stating that the non-existence of the Necessary is entailed by absolute non-existence, because the attribute of being a maker is necessary for a maker. That leads to the conclusion that the non-existence of a possible effect is entailed by the non-existence of the Necessary (which is impossible). Then an objection is raised: if that is the case, then the impossible (the non-existence of the Necessary) is not the necessary consequence of the possible in essence, but only accidentally—so there is no problem. The author replies: No, it is in essence, because the intermediaries are intermediaries in subsistence. He explains that some intermediaries are impossible and some are possible, and in both cases the possible entails the impossible. For example, if an intermediary is impossible, the possible entails it; if the intermediary is possible but leads to an impossible, again the possible entails the impossible. Some scholars refined this by specifying that the antecedent only becomes impossible if there is no temporal priority/posteriority between the antecedent and the impossible consequent, such as the entailment of the non-division of an essentially extended substance to its division. This specification is not arbitrary but required by investigation. The author concludes with 'And God knows best.' The example used (non-division of substance leading to division) is drawn from kalam discussions on atomism.

C) Only if the antecedent is also impossible
D) Only if the consequent is the non-existence of the Necessary

Key Takeaway
A possible can entail an impossible essentially if the chain of intermediaries is such that the impossible is a necessary consequence, but temporal priority can block that entailment from affecting the status of the possible.

Refuting Suhrawardi's Objection on Contingent Existence
Author responds to al-Shaykh al-Maqtul's objection about contingent existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Shaykh al-Maqtul's objection against the identity of existence and essence in the Necessary Being and evaluate the author's response using the distinction between particular and infinitive existence.

Core Idea
The identity of existence and essence is defended even for contingent beings, but only for particular existence.

In this passage, the author engages with a powerful objection from al-Shaykh al-Maqtul (Suhrawardi) against the claim that existence is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being. The objection turns the same reasoning against contingent beings, threatening to collapse the distinction. The author's response requires a careful distinction between two senses of existence: external particular existence and the mental infinitive concept. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping the Islamic philosophical argument for the Necessary Being's simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original argument
Locate the claim that existence must be identical to the essence of the Necessary Being. The philosophers argue that if existence were an additional accident, it would require a cause, leading to infinite regress. Find this reasoning in the Arabic passage.
2
State al-Shaykh al-Maqtul's objection
Al-Shaykh al-Maqtul applies the same reasoning to contingent beings: if existence were additional, it would require prior existence, so existence must also be identical in contingent beings. This is an attempt to refute by counterexample (naqd).
3
Examine the proposed solution
The solution claims that existence is identical in the external world but additional in the intellect. The author rejects this solution as invalid for the argument.
4
Understand the author's rejection
The author argues that the discussion concerns particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), which is external. If it is identical to the essence externally, it must also be identical in the intellect, because a thing does not differ between mind and external. Thus the solution's claim of being additional in the intellect is irrelevant.
5
Note the author's concession
The author concedes that the objection is valid for contingent beings, but argues that existence in them is also identical (to their essences), as previously indicated. Therefore, the objection does not undermine the original argument about the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيما ذكرناه إشارة إلى الجواب بأن الوجود عرض لازم، وهو لا يعلل . اعلم أن الوجود يطلق ويراد به المعنى المصدري البديهي التصور، ويطلق ویراد به منشأ انتزاعه ومصداق حمله، ولا بد أن يكون موجودا، وإلا فيكون له منشأ أيضا، فهو المنشأ حقيقة ، وقد يقال له الوجود الخاص.
قال الحكماء: إنه عين الواجب جل شأنه، وإلا لكان خارجا عارضا؛ لامتناع التركيب، وكل عارض محتاج إلى معروضه قطعا، فيكون ممكنا، فلا بد له من علة ، فإن كان نفس الذات يلزم تقدمها بالوجود عليه، وإن كان غيره فیلزم احتياجه تعالى إلى الغير، هذا خلف، وعلى هذا لا یرد ما أورده
المصنف .
وإن شئت قلت بعبارة أخرى الواجب ذاته كاف في انتزاع الوجود الذي هو بديهي التصور، وإلا لكان في اتصافه بالوجود محتاجا إلى الغير، وهو محال ، فيكون الوجود الخاص هو نفسه ، وهذا أيضا لا غبار عليه.

المنضم ، فيلزم كونه موجودا قبل وجوده.
والحل : أن الوجود عين في الخارج، زائد في العقل، فلا يحتاج إلى العلة ولا يخفى أن هذا الحل غير متوجه ؛ لأن الكلام في الوجود الخاص،
·
ولا بد من أن يكون موجودا في الخارج ؛ فإن كان عين الذات فلو حصلت في العقل كان أيضا عينها؛ إذ الشيء لا يختلف ذهنا وخارجا. نعم، الوجود المصدري زائد في العقل ؛ لكونه اعتباريا عقليا نعم، النقض بالممكن تام، بل الوجود فيه أيضا عين كما تقدم الإشارة إليه في أوائل الكتاب، فإن ظهر أن
And in what we have mentioned there is an indication of the answer: that existence is a necessary accident, and it is not caused. Know that existence is used and meant by it the infinitive meaning which is self-evident in conception, and it is used and meant by it the source of its derivation and the referent of its predication, and it must exist, otherwise it would have another source, so it is the source in reality, and it may be called particular existence. The philosophers said: It is identical to the Necessary Being, Exalted be He; otherwise it would be an external accident, because composition is impossible, and every accident is necessarily needful of its subject, so it would be possible, and there must be a cause for it. If it is the essence itself, then its existence would be prior to it; if it is another, then it would require His need of another. This is a vicious circle. And upon this, the objection from the author does not apply. If you wish, you can say in other words: The essence of the Necessary Being is sufficient for deriving existence, which is self-evident in conception; otherwise, in its being qualified with existence it would be needful of another, which is impossible. So particular existence is it itself. And this is also without any objection. Al-Shaykh al-Maqtul said: This argument is refuted by the existence of contingent beings; for if it were additional, it would be an accident attached to it, and the attached thing must have a prior existence, so it would require it to be existent before its existence. The solution: that existence is identical in the external, additional in the intellect, so it does not need a cause. And it is not hidden that this solution is not valid, because the discussion is about particular existence, and it must exist in the external; for if it were identical to the essence, then if the essence occurs in the intellect, it would also be identical to it; for a thing does not differ in mind and external. Indeed, the infinitive existence is additional in the intellect, because it is a mental accident. Indeed, the refutation by contingent beings is valid, but existence in them is also identical as was previously indicated in the beginning of the book. So it becomes apparent that...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement in which the author responds to an objection raised by al-Shaykh al-Maqtul (Suhrawardi) against the argument that existence is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being. The objection is that the same reasoning would apply to contingent beings: if existence were an additional accident, it would require a prior existence, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, existence must also be identical to the essence of contingent beings, which is problematic. The author dismisses the solution that existence is identical in the external but additional in the intellect, because the discussion concerns particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), which must be external. He then concedes that the objection is valid, but argues that existence in contingent beings is also identical (to their essences), as indicated earlier. This is a sophisticated theological-philosophical debate about the nature of existence. The passage demonstrates the method of dialectical refutation and the importance of distinguishing between different meanings of existence: the infinitive meaning (al-ma'na al-masdari) and particular existence.

Key Terms
الوجود الخاص (al-wujud al-khas): Particular existence; the specific existence of a thing in the external world, as opposed to the infinitive concept of existence. [Note: In later texts, al-wujud al-khas may be equated with the very essence of a thing, especially in the context of the Necessary Being.]
الوجود المصدري (al-wujud al-masdari): The infinitive existence; the mental concept of existence derived from the verb 'exists', considered self-evident and additional in the intellect. [Note: This distinction is crucial in later Islamic philosophy to differentiate between mental and external existence.]
النقض (al-naqd): Refutation or counterexample; an objection that shows a flaw in an argument by applying it to another case. [Note: In jadal (dialectics), naqd is a standard form of rebuttal.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because contingent beings do not have existence.
C) Because the objection only applies to the Necessary Being.
D) Because the solution was proposed by al-Shaykh al-Maqtul himself.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the identity of existence and essence by distinguishing between particular existence (external) and the infinitive concept (mental), showing that the objection only applies if the distinction is ignored.

Proof that God's Existence is Identical to His Essence
Author presents a reductio proof that existence is not other than the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument for the identity of existence and essence in the Necessary Being using a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Existence must be identical to God's essence, because any separation leads to impossibility.

This passage presents a sophisticated logical proof (burhan) from Islamic philosophy demonstrating that in the Necessary Being (Allah), existence is not an added attribute but identical to His essence. The argument uses a conditional syllogism with reductio ad absurdum (khulf), showing that any assumption of separation leads to contradictions. By mastering this argument, you will see how logical tools are used to defend core theological doctrines like tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if' clause: 'if existence were other than the Necessary'. This is the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional. The author assumes this for reductio.
2
Trace the consequent chain
The consequent is that the Necessary would have a universal quiddity (mahiyya kulliyya) and existence. Every universal quiddity has individuals beyond its existing instances.
3
Analyze the trichotomy of individuals
The individuals of that quiddity, considered in themselves, must be either impossible, necessary, or possible. The author examines each case.
4
Spot the contradictions in each branch
If impossible, the existing individuals would also be impossible (contradiction). If necessary, an infinite number of necessary beings would exist (absurd). If possible, the Necessary would be possible (contradicts His necessity).
5
Conclude via modus tollens
Since each branch of the consequent leads to absurdity (khulf), the consequent is false. Therefore the antecedent (existence being other than the Necessary) is false, proving identity.
This is a valid modus tollens form: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أقام حجة أخرى من عند نفسه، هي: أن الوجود لو كان غير الواجب لكان له ماهية كلية ووجود، وكل ماهية كلية لها أفراد سوى ما هي موجودة، وتلك الأفراد بالنظر إلى نفس ماهيتها إما ممتنعة، فيكون الأفراد الموجودة أيضا كذلك، وإما واجبة فتكون موجودة، فيلزم وجود غير المتناهي، وإما ممكنة، فيلزم أن يكون الموجود أيضا كذلك، وقد فرض واجبا، هذا خلف.
Then he adduced another proof from himself: that if existence were other than the Necessary, then He would have a universal quiddity and an existence, and every universal quiddity has individuals other than that which exists, and those individuals with respect to their own quiddity are either impossible, so the existing individuals would also be so; or necessary, so they would be existent, and the existence of the infinite would follow; or possible, so the existent would also be possible, but He has been assumed necessary; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (burhan) for the identity of existence and essence in the Necessary Being (Allah). The author constructs a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) with a reductio (khulf). The premise is: if existence were additional (ghayr) to the Necessary Being, then He would have a quiddity (mahiyya) distinct from existence. This quiddity would be a universal, having individuals. Those individuals, in terms of their essences, must be either impossible (mumtani'a), necessary (wajiba), or possible (mumkina). Each leads to absurdity: if impossible, then the existing individuals would also be impossible (contradiction); if necessary, then an infinite number of necessary beings would exist, which is absurd; if possible, then the Necessary Being would be possible, contradicting His necessity. Therefore, the assumption is false, proving that existence is identical to essence for the Necessary Being.

Key Terms
الوجود
الواجب
الماهية
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Existence is identical to the essence of the Necessary.
C) The Necessary has no existence.
D) Existence is a part of the quiddity of the Necessary.

Key Takeaway
The proof uses a conditional syllogism with reductio ad absurdum (khulf) to establish that existence is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being.
