The True Subject of Demonstrative Propositions: Nature vs Individuals
First objection and answer on predication to nature vs individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and accidental predication in demonstrative propositions and identify why nature is the primary subject.

Core Idea
The predicate must attach essentially to the nature for a proposition to be demonstrative.

In this passage, the author defends the view that universal demonstrative propositions have nature (tabi'a) as their true subject, not individual instances. He responds to an objection that individuals are more specific than nature, which would make the predicate accidental. Let's break down the first objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector says each individual is more specific than the nature because nature is realized only in some individuals. That would make the predicate an accidental attribute ('arad gharib) of the subject, rendering the proposition non-demonstrative.
2
Understand why accidental predication fails
For a proposition to be a mas'ala burhaniyya, the predicate must belong essentially (bil-dhat) to the subject. If it attaches accidentally (bil-'arad), certainty is lost.
Accidental here means through an intermediary; the predicate does not directly belong to the subject by virtue of its essence.
3
Analyze the author's answer
The author responds that the reality (haqiqa) of the subject can be a substrate for the predicate essentially if that reality is a species for the individuals, with individuals as intermediaries. Example: 'Fire is hot and dry' – 'fire' as a nature has 'hot and dry' essentially.
Alternatively, a property coextensive with the species, like geometric example, can serve.
4
Apply the criterion to an Islamic example
Consider 'The world is temporally originated'. 'The world' as a nature has 'temporally originated' as an essential attribute, because everything material changes. Thus this is a demonstrative proposition.
From kalam; this is a classical argument for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس أن كل واحد من الأشخاص أخص من الطبيعة؛ لتحققها في البعض فقط ، بدون الكل، فيكون المحمول عرضا غريبا للموضوع، فلا تقع القضية حينئذ مسألة برهانية. الجواب : أن حقيقة الموضوع قد يكون معروضة للمحمول بالذات، إذا كانت نوعا للأشخاص، والأشخاص بواسطتها، كقولنا النار حار يابس ، أو بواسطة أمر مساو للنوع ، كقولنا «ما ليس فيه مبدأ مميل مستقيم، لا يقبل الخرق ، فحينئذ تقع مسألة برهانية. نعم، في بعض القضايا ليس الأمر كذلك ، فيلزم عدم وقوعها مسألة برهانية ، فتأمل.
الساحمن[؟] ما أشار إليه بقوله: (وَرُبَّمَا يَتَرَأَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ)، أي الحكم على الحقيقة (لَاقْتَضَى الْإِيجَابُ وُجُودَ الْحَقِيقَةِ حَقِيقَةٌ ; فَإِنَّ) الإيجاب يستدعي وجود المثبت له ، وأن الْمُثْبَتَ لَهُ هُوَ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ حَقِيقَةٌ، مَعَ) أن التالي باطل؛ إذ (إِنَّهَا قَدْ تَكُونُ عَدَمِيَّةً)، كما في المعدولة الموضوع، (بَلْ سَلْبِيَّةٌ)، كما في سالبة الموضوع، فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْأَفْرَادَ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةً بِالوَجْهِ، لَكِنَّهَا مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهَا حَقِيقَةٌ ، أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْوَضْعِ الْعَام وَالْمَوْضُوع لَهُ الْخَاصَّ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَعْلُومَ بِالْوَجْهِ هُوَ الْمَوْضُوعُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ .
وَالْجَوَابُ ): أن اتحاد المثبت له والمحكوم عليه مطلقا ممنوع، كيف، و (إِنَّ مُفَادَ الْإِيجَابِ مُطْلَقًا هُوَ النُّبُوتُ مُطْلَقًا)، سواء كانت بالذات أو بالعرض، (وَكُلُّ حُكْمِ ثَابِتٍ لِلْأَفْرَادِ ثَابِتٌ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ)، أو بالعرض فيكون صالحة لكونها محكوما عليها بالذات، مع عدم صلوحها لكونها مثبتا لها بالذات .
(وَأَمَّا أَنَّهُ لِمَاذَا أَوَّلًا وَبِالذَّاتِ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ أَوْ لِلْفَرْدِ؟ فَمَنْهُومٌ زَائِدٌ عَلَى الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ). وقد يقال: فعلى هذا يلزم أن يكون النتيجة والمقدمات متخالفة في الثبوت، بأن يكون الثبوت في المقدمات بالذات، وفي النتيجة بالعرض، كقولنا «الإنسان أعمى» «وكل إنسان متحرك»، فينتج «بعض الأعمى متحرك»، مع أن الضرورة يحكم بالتوافق والجواب بالتزام ذلك، والحكم .
ثم الحق أن القضايا المستعملة في العلوم يحكم فيها بالثبوت بالذات، أي من غير واسطة في العروض، وإنكار ذلك مكابرة بعيدة عن الإنصاف، كيف ، والثبوت بالعرض ليس ثبوتا حقيقة ، ولا يحصل اليقين الدائم به إلا بعد حصول اليقين بالواسطة على ما تقرر عندهم وثبوت المحمول بعنوان الموضوع بالذات غير بين في كثير من المواضع، بل عدمه بين ; فإن الموضوع ربما يكون عرضا ذاتيا معدوما في الخارج، فإذن الحق أن الحكم في القضايا المتعارفة في الحكمة على الأفراد، كما عليه المتأخرون، ويمكن تطبيق عبارات الشيخ عليه أيضا. والعلم بالوجه كان للحكم ، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Fifth: That each one of the individuals is more specific than the nature; because it is realized in some only, not in all, so the predicate would be an accidental accident to the subject, so the proposition would not then be a demonstrative problem. The answer: That the reality of the subject may be a substrate for the predicate essentially, if it is a species for the individuals, and the individuals are by its mediation, like our saying 'Fire is hot and dry', or by means of something coextensive with the species, like our saying 'That which has no principle of rectilinear inclination does not accept penetration', so then it becomes a demonstrative problem. Yes, in some propositions it is not so, so it follows that they do not become demonstrative problems, so reflect. The sixth, what he pointed to by his saying: (And perhaps it appears that if it were so, affirmation would require the existence of the nature as a reality; because) affirmation requires the existence of that for which something is affirmed, and that that for which something is affirmed is the subject judged as a reality, while the consequent is false; since (it may be non-existent), as in the modified subject, (rather negative), as in the negative subject. So the truth is that the individuals, although known in one aspect, are judged as a reality. Do you not see the common positing and the specific posited? For that known in one aspect is the truly posited. And the answer: That the unification of that for which something is affirmed and the subject judged is absolutely denied. How, and (the import of affirmation absolutely is the affirmation absolutely), whether essentially or accidentally, (and every judgment established for individuals is established for the nature in sum), or accidentally, so it is fit to be a subject judged essentially, while not being fit to be that for which something is affirmed essentially. (And as for why it is primarily and essentially for the nature or for the individual? It is a concept additional to the reality, so reflect). And it may be said: Accordingly, it would be necessary that the conclusion and the premises differ in the mode of being established, such that the establishment in the premises is essential, and in the conclusion accidental, like our saying 'A blind man is a human' and 'Every human is moving', so it results 'Some blind man is moving', while necessity judges agreement, and the answer is to commit to that, and the judgment. Then the truth is that the propositions used in the sciences are judged with essential establishment, i.e., without intermediacy in attribution, and denying that is an obstinacy far from fairness. How, and accidental establishment is not real establishment, and permanent certainty is not obtained by it except after obtaining certainty of the intermediary as established with them, and the establishment of the predicate under the title of the subject essentially is not evident in many places, rather its non-existence is evident; for the subject may be an essential accident non-existent in the external. So then the truth is that judgment in the customary propositions in wisdom is upon individuals, as the later scholars hold, and the expressions of the Master can also be applied to it. And knowledge in one aspect was for judgment, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage engages in a dialectical exchange defending the use of nature (tabi'a) as the true subject of universal demonstrative propositions, against an objection that individuals are more specific. The author follows the order of objection, answer, further objection, and final resolution. He first states the objection: each individual is more specific than the nature because nature is realized in only some individuals, so the predicate would be an accidental attribute, making the proposition unfit as a demonstrative problem. The answer distinguishes: the reality (haqiqa) of the subject may be a substrate for the predicate essentially, if that reality is a species for the individuals, with individuals as intermediaries (e.g., 'Fire is hot and dry'), or via something coextensive with the species (e.g., geometric example). Then an objection based on Ibn Sina's point: if predication is on the nature, affirmative judgment would require the nature's existence as a reality, but the subject may be non-existent or negative. The answer is that the unity of 'what for' (muthbat lahu) and 'judged upon' is not absolute; the import of affirmation is simply 'thubut' (affirmation), whether essential or accidental. Every judgment for individuals holds for nature in general. Further discussion arises about the conclusion and premises differing in mode (essential vs. accidental), illustrated with 'A blind man is human, every human is moving, so some blind man is moving'—the conclusion's predicate is accidental to blind man. The author ultimately adopts the position of later scholars that judgment in sciences is on individuals, but this can be reconciled with the Shaykh's statements. The passage is dense and requires careful parsing of the technical terms 'bil-dhat' (essentially) and 'bil-'arad' (accidentally).

بالعرض
طبيعة
مسألة برهانية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why would the proposition 'Fire is hot and dry' be demonstrative?
A) Because fire is a specific individual.
B) Because 'hot and dry' are essential properties of the nature 'fire'.
C) Because the predicate is accidental.
D) Because the proposition is particular.

Key Takeaway
A demonstrative proposition requires the predicate to be essentially attributed to the nature, not accidentally to individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'Every human being is mortal.' Is 'mortal' an essential or accidental predication if the subject is taken as the nature 'human'? Explain using the criterion from this lesson.
?
Find an example from fiqh (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden') and argue whether it qualifies as a demonstrative problem according to the author's criterion.

The Existence of Nature and the Import of Affirmation
Second objection and answer on the existence requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a universal proposition can be true even if the nature has no real existence.

Core Idea
Affirmation does not require the existence of the nature; the judgment is about the concept.

A further objection is raised: if judgment is on the nature, then affirmative propositions would require the nature to exist. But the subject can be non-existent or negative. The author clarifies that affirmation ('ithbat') simply means establishing the predicate for the subject, regardless of external existence.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector says: if predication is on the nature, then an affirmative proposition like 'Nature is such-and-such' would require the nature to exist in reality. But we have propositions with non-existent or negative subjects (e.g., 'Non-existent thing is thought').
2
Recognize the counterexample
The author points out that the nature may be non-existent ('adam) or even negative (salbi). For example, 'Round square does not exist' – here the subject 'round square' is a nature but does not exist.
This shows that existence is not a requirement for predication.
3
Examine the author's answer
The answer: the import of affirmation (mafad al-ijab) is simply 'thubut' (establishment) – the predicate is asserted of the subject, whether essentially or accidentally. The unity of 'what for' and 'subject judged' is not required.
Every judgment for individuals holds for the nature in general.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In usul al-fiqh, the principle 'Riba is forbidden' applies to the nature of 'riba' even if no actual instance of riba exists at the moment of ruling. The judgment is on the concept, not its instances.
This preserves the universality of legal rulings.

Commentary
This passage engages in a dialectical exchange defending the use of nature (tabi'a) as the true subject of universal demonstrative propositions, against an objection that individuals are more specific. The author follows the order of objection, answer, further objection, and final resolution. Essential vs Accidental Predication in Demonstrative Sciences
Final resolution: sciences use essential predication on individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a proposition's predicate is essential or accidental and determine if it is suitable for demonstration.

Core Idea
Sciences require essential predication, but modern scholars judge on individuals, reconciling with the tradition.

The passage concludes with a key distinction: in the sciences, we must have essential predication (bil-dhat), meaning no intermediary in attribution. However, the later scholars hold that the subject of judgment is actually the individual, not the nature. This seems contradictory, but the author reconciles by showing that even when judging individuals, the predication can be essential if we consider the nature as the intermediary. Let's analyze this through the example of 'blind man' and the final resolution.

Guided Path
1
Examine the blind man example
The author gives: 'A blind man is a human, every human is moving, so some blind man is moving.' The predicate 'moving' is accidental to 'blind man' because it comes through 'human'. This shows that conclusion can have accidental predication even if premises are essential.
This raises the problem of uniformity: if premises are essential and conclusion accidental, is the demonstration valid?
2
Understand the author's position on sciences
The author asserts that propositions used in the sciences are judged with essential establishment (thubut bil-dhat), without intermediacy in attribution. Denying this is obstinacy.
'Essential establishment' means the predicate belongs to the subject by virtue of the subject's essence.
3
Recognize the later scholars' view
The later scholars hold that judgment in the sciences is actually on individuals, not on the nature. The author notes this can be reconciled with the earlier view (the Master = Ibn Sina) by careful application.
The key is that even when judging individuals, we can still have essential predication if the individual is considered under its nature.
4
Apply to a kalam argument
Take the kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Here 'has a cause' is essential to 'temporally originated', but the conclusion is about the world as an individual. The predication is essential because it follows from the nature of the world (being temporally originated).
This shows how essential predication can apply to individuals when mediated by their nature.

Source Text

الخامس أن كل واحد من الأشخاص أخص من الطبيعة؛ لتحققها في البعض فقط ، بدون الكل، فيكون المحمول عرضا غريبا للموضوع، فلا تقع القضية حينئذ مسألة برهانية. الجواب : أن حقيقة الموضوع قد يكون معروضة للمحمول بالذات، إذا كانت نوعا للأشخاص، والأشخاص بواسطتها، كقولنا النار حار يابس ، أو بواسطة أمر مساو للنوع ، كقولنا «ما ليس فيه مبدأ مميل مستقيم، لا يقبل الخرق ، فحينئذ تقع مسألة برهانية. نعم، في بعض القضايا ليس الأمر كذلك ، فيلزم عدم وقوعها مسألة برهانية ، فتأمل.
الساحمن[؟] ما أشار إليه بقوله: (وَرُبَّمَا يَتَرَأَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ)، أي الحكم على الحقيقة (لَاقْتَضَى الْإِيجَابُ وُجُودَ الْحَقِيقَةِ حَقِيقَةٌ ; فَإِنَّ) الإيجاب يستدعي وجود المثبت له ، وأن الْمُثْبَتَ لَهُ هُوَ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ حَقِيقَةٌ، مَعَ) أن التالي باطل؛ إذ (إِنَّهَا قَدْ تَكُونُ عَدَمِيَّةً)، كما في المعدولة الموضوع، (بَلْ سَلْبِيَّةٌ)، كما في سالبة الموضوع، فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْأَفْرَادَ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةً بِالوَجْهِ، لَكِنَّهَا مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهَا حَقِيقَةٌ ، أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْوَضْعِ الْعَام وَالْمَوْضُوع لَهُ الْخَاصَّ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَعْلُومَ بِالْوَجْهِ هُوَ الْمَوْضُوعُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ .
وَالْجَوَابُ ): أن اتحاد المثبت له والمحكوم عليه مطلقا ممنوع، كيف، و (إِنَّ مُفَادَ الْإِيجَابِ مُطْلَقًا هُوَ النُّبُوتُ مُطْلَقًا)، سواء كانت بالذات أو بالعرض، (وَكُلُّ حُكْمِ ثَابِتٍ لِلْأَفْرَادِ ثَابِتٌ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ)، أو بالعرض فيكون صالحة لكونها محكوما عليها بالذات، مع عدم صلوحها لكونها مثبتا لها بالذات .
(وَأَمَّا أَنَّهُ لِمَاذَا أَوَّلًا وَبِالذَّاتِ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ أَوْ لِلْفَرْدِ؟ فَمَنْهُومٌ زَائِدٌ عَلَى الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ). وقد يقال: فعلى هذا يلزم أن يكون النتيجة والمقدمات متخالفة في الثبوت، بأن يكون الثبوت في المقدمات بالذات، وفي النتيجة بالعرض، كقولنا «الإنسان أعمى» «وكل إنسان متحرك»، فينتج «بعض الأعمى متحرك»، مع أن الضرورة يحكم بالتوافق والجواب بالتزام ذلك، والحكم .
ثم الحق أن القضايا المستعملة في العلوم يحكم فيها بالثبوت بالذات، أي من غير واسطة في العروض، وإنكار ذلك مكابرة بعيدة عن الإنصاف، كيف ، والثبوت بالعرض ليس ثبوتا حقيقة ، ولا يحصل اليقين الدائم به إلا بعد حصول اليقين بالواسطة على ما تقرر عندهم وثبوت المحمول بعنوان الموضوع بالذات غير بين في كثير من المواضع، بل عدمه بين ; فإن الموضوع ربما يكون عرضا ذاتيا معدوما في الخارج، فإذن الحق أن الحكم في القضايا المتعارفة في الحكمة على الأفراد، كما عليه المتأخرون، ويمكن تطبيق عبارات الشيخ عليه أيضا. والعلم بالوجه كان للحكم ، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Fifth: That each one of the individuals is more specific than the nature; because it is realized in some only, not in all, so the predicate would be an accidental accident to the subject, so the proposition would not then be a demonstrative problem. The answer: That the reality of the subject may be a substrate for the predicate essentially, if it is a species for the individuals, and the individuals are by its mediation, like our saying 'Fire is hot and dry', or by means of something coextensive with the species, like our saying 'That which has no principle of rectilinear inclination does not accept penetration', so then it becomes a demonstrative problem. Yes, in some propositions it is not so, so it follows that they do not become demonstrative problems, so reflect. The sixth, what he pointed to by his saying: (And perhaps it appears that if it were so, affirmation would require the existence of the nature as a reality; because) affirmation requires the existence of that for which something is affirmed, and that that for which something is affirmed is the subject judged as a reality, while the consequent is false; since (it may be non-existent), as in the modified subject, (rather negative), as in the negative subject. So the truth is that the individuals, although known in one aspect, are judged as a reality. Do you not see the common positing and the specific posited? For that known in one aspect is the truly posited. And the answer: That the unification of that for which something is affirmed and the subject judged is absolutely denied. How, and (the import of affirmation absolutely is the affirmation absolutely), whether essentially or accidentally, (and every judgment established for individuals is established for the nature in sum), or accidentally, so it is fit to be a subject judged essentially, while not being fit to be that for which something is affirmed essentially. (And as for why it is primarily and essentially for the nature or for the individual? It is a concept additional to the reality, so reflect). And it may be said: Accordingly, it would be necessary that the conclusion and the premises differ in the mode of being established, such that the establishment in the premises is essential, and in the conclusion accidental, like our saying 'A blind man is a human' and 'Every human is moving', so it results 'Some blind man is moving', while necessity judges agreement, and the answer is to commit to that, and the judgment. Then the truth is that the propositions used in the sciences are judged with essential establishment, i.e., without intermediacy in attribution, and denying that is an obstinacy far from fairness. How, and accidental establishment is not real establishment, and permanent certainty is not obtained by it except after obtaining certainty of the intermediary as established with them, and the establishment of the predicate under the title of the subject essentially is not evident in many places, rather its non-existence is evident; for the subject may be an essential accident non-existent in the external. So then the truth is that judgment in the customary propositions in wisdom is upon individuals, as the later scholars hold, and the expressions of the Master can also be applied to it. And knowledge in one aspect was for judgment, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage engages in a dialectical exchange defending the use of nature (tabi'a) as the true subject of universal demonstrative propositions, against an objection that individuals are more specific. The author follows the order of objection, answer, further objection, and final resolution. He first states the objection: each individual is more specific than the nature because nature is realized in only some individuals, so the predicate would be an accidental attribute, making the proposition unfit as a demonstrative problem. The answer distinguishes: the reality (haqiqa) of the subject may be a substrate for the predicate essentially, if that reality is a species for the individuals, with individuals as intermediaries (e.g., 'Fire is hot and dry'), or via something coextensive with the species (e.g., geometric example). Then an objection based on Ibn Sina's point: if predication is on the nature, affirmative judgment would require the nature's existence as a reality, but the subject may be non-existent or negative. The answer is that the unity of 'what for' (muthbat lahu) and 'judged upon' is not absolute; the import of affirmation is simply 'thubut' (affirmation), whether essential or accidental. Every judgment for individuals holds for nature in general. Further discussion arises about the conclusion and premises differing in mode (essential vs. accidental), illustrated with 'A blind man is human, every human is moving, so some blind man is moving'—the conclusion's predicate is accidental to blind man. The author ultimately adopts the position of later scholars that judgment in sciences is on individuals, but this can be reconciled with the Shaykh's statements. The passage is dense and requires careful parsing of the technical terms 'bil-dhat' (essentially) and 'bil-'arad' (accidentally).

بالعرض
واسطة في العروض
التأخرون
الشيخ

Two Interpretations of Logical Entailment: Reality vs. Knowledge
Author distinguishes entailment in reality vs. knowledge, citing Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the realist, Ash'ari occasionalist, and Mu'tazili causalist interpretations of logical entailment, and identify their implications for the foundation of demonstration.

Core Idea
Is entailment a real necessity or a habitual mental sequence? Your answer affects the entire edifice of rational proof.

In this passage, the author explores a foundational question: what does it mean for a conclusion to 'follow' from premises? He presents two broad interpretations—entailment in reality (nafs al-amr) and entailment according to knowledge (hasab al-ilm)—and then subdivides the second into the Ash'ari theory of habitual subsequence and the Mu'tazili theory of causal generation. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for appreciating how different Islamic theological schools ground the certainty of logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary interpretations
In the first line, the author contrasts 'entailment taken in reality' with 'entailment considered according to knowledge.' Locate these two phrases in the Arabic and note the key terms: (فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ) and (بِحَسَبِ الْعِلْمِ).
The first is about the objective connection, the second about our cognitive grasp.
2
Define 'entailment in reality'
The author explains: 'because the conclusion is a quality necessarily belonging to the syllogism in reality, since if it is true the conclusion is true, and if it is false the conclusion is false.' This means the connection is necessary and truth-preserving, independent of the mind.
3
Define 'entailment according to knowledge' as subsequence
The author says the more well-known interpretation takes entailment as subsequence (isti'qab): the conclusion occurs in the mind after realizing that it is subsumed under the premises. Find the phrase (الْإِنْدِرَاج) and note that it refers to subsumption, as Ibn Sina stated.
4
Unpack the two theological subtypes of subsequence
The subsequence is then said to be either 'in the manner of habit' (Ash'ari) or 'by generation' (Mu'tazili). For habit: God habitually creates knowledge of the conclusion after knowledge of the premises, without necessity on God's part. For generation: the premises act as a real cause producing the conclusion, like a key moving when the hand moves.
The Ash'ari view is occasionalist; the Mu'tazili view is causalist.
5
Assess the implications for logical certainty
Consider: on the Ash'ari view, is a syllogism still reliable? The author implies yes, because God's habit is consistent. On the Mu'tazili view, the mind itself generates the conclusion, so human reasoning has causal efficacy. Both preserve the practical reliability of logic, but ground it differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ إِنْ أُخِذَ اللُّزُومُ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ فَبِهَا) ونِعْمَ؛ لأن النتيجة لازمة كيفية للقياس في نفس الأمر ؛ إذ بصدقه تصدق وبكذبه تكذب، (وَإِنِ اعْتُبِرَ) اللزوم حصول العلم الية من ( بِحَسَبِ الْعِلْمِ، وَهُوَ الْأَشْهَرُ ، فَالْمُرَادُ الاسْتِعْقابُ)، أي حصول النتيجة عقيب حصول القياس، (بَعْدَ تَفَطَّنِ الإِنْدِرَاج، أي اندراج النتيجة في القياس، بالنتيجة المقدمتين بالاشتمال عليه أو على نقيضه ؛ كَمَا قَالَ) الشيخ الرئيس (ابْنُ سِينَا . وَذَلِكَ الِاسْتِعْقَابُ ] (۱) عَلَى سَبِيلِ الْعَادَةِ)، أي جري عادة الله تعالى [ بإيجاد العلم بالنتيجة عقيب حصول العلم بالقياس الصحيح، من غير وجوب منه تعالى. هذا مذهب الشيخ المفضل الإمام من أئمة أهل الحق أبي الحسن الأشعري - قدس سره - وهذا بناء على أن لا خالق إلا الله تعالى ابتداء باختياره وقدرته جلت أسماؤه ، لكن لا يلزم منه أن يكون من غير وجوب، بل الوجود من غير وجوب باطل، كما بين في موضعه . أو التَّوْلِيدِ)، وهو وجود فعل بتبعية فعل آخر صادر عن مختار باختيار من غير اختيار منه، كحركة المفتاح بحركة اليد الصادر عن المختار، إليه ذهب المعتزلة خذلهم الله تعالى قالوا النظر فعل العبد وصادر من اختياره،
Then, if entailment is taken in reality (nafs al-amr), then it is well and good, because the conclusion is a quality necessarily belonging to the syllogism in reality, since if it is true the conclusion is true, and if it is false the conclusion is false. And if entailment is considered according to knowledge, and this is the more well-known, then what is intended is subsequence (isti'qab), i.e., the obtaining of the conclusion after the obtaining of the syllogism, after realizing the subsumption (indiraj), meaning the subsumption of the conclusion under the syllogism, i.e., the two premises, either by containing it or its contradictory, as the Chief Master Ibn Sina said. And that subsequence is in the manner of habit (al-ada), i.e., the continuous habit of God, exalted, in bringing about knowledge of the conclusion after obtaining knowledge of the correct syllogism, without necessity on His part. This is the doctrine of the excellent Imam, one of the imams of the people of truth, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari — may God sanctify his secret — and this is based on the fact that there is no creator except God, may His names be exalted, from the beginning by His choice and power. However, it does not necessitate that it be without necessity; rather, existence without necessity is false, as established in its place. Or [entailment is] by generation (tawlid), which is the existence of an act following another act emanating from a free agent by choice without choice from it, like the movement of a key following the movement of a hand from the free agent. This is what the Mu'tazilites — may God forsake them — held; they said that reflection (nazar) is the act of the servant and emanates from his choice.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage delves into the nature of logical entailment (لزوم) between premises and conclusion in a syllogism. The author presents two interpretations. The first considers entailment in reality (نفس الأمر): the conclusion truly and necessarily follows from the premises such that when the premises are true, the conclusion is true, and when false, false. This is a necessary connection in the actual world. The second interpretation considers entailment according to knowledge (حسب العلم), which is more widespread. On this view, entailment is understood as subsequence (استعقاب): the conclusion occurs in the mind after the person has grasped the syllogism, specifically after realizing that the conclusion is subsumed under (or contained within) the premises — either directly or through its contradictory. The author attributes this notion of subsequence as habit (عادة) to the Ash'ari school: God habitually creates knowledge of the conclusion in the mind after knowledge of the correct syllogism, but not out of any necessity on God's part. God alone is the creator of all things, including knowledge, and He does so by His choice and power. However, the author notes that this does not mean existence can be without necessity; that would be false. The third view is that of the Mu'tazilites, who hold that entailment is by generation (توليد): the premises cause the conclusion in a real causal sense, similar to how the movement of a key is generated by the movement of the hand. They apply this to human reflection: the act of reflecting is the servant's own act, generated by his choice. The passage thus contrasts a realist, Ash'ari occasionalist, and Mu'tazili causalist view of logical consequence.

Key Terms
اللزوم
نفس الأمر
الاستعقاب
العادة
التوليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between taking entailment 'in reality' and 'according to knowledge'?
A) In reality, entailment is based on habit; in knowledge, it is based on necessity.
B) In reality, the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises; in knowledge, it is a habitual mental subsequence.
C) In reality, entailment is caused by God; in knowledge, it is caused by the syllogism itself.
D) There is no difference; both refer to the same thing.

Key Takeaway
Logical entailment can be viewed either as a real necessary connection (truth-preserving in the world) or as a cognitive subsequence (mind-dependent), with the latter further divided into Ash'ari habitual and Mu'tazili causal interpretations.

Warning against Inferring from a Void Syllogism
Author warns against a mistaken inference from the void syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error of confusing a valid syllogism's true conclusion with the truth of its premises in Islamic logical analyses.

Core Idea
A true conclusion does not guarantee true premises; false premises can yield a true conclusion.

In this passage, Bahr Ulum warns against a subtle mistake: some might think that because a syllogism about non-existent subjects (like the void) yields a true conclusion, affirmative propositions do not require the subject to exist. The author refutes this by pointing out that the truth of the conclusion does not guarantee the truth of the premises. A false premise can lead to a true conclusion, so we cannot deduce the nature of propositions from the conclusion's truth alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken inference
What did the student wrongly conclude about affirmative propositions from the void syllogism? The mistaken inference is that affirmative propositions do not require the existence of their subject.
This inference is based on the observation that the syllogism produced a true conclusion despite the subject 'void' being non-existent.
2
Understand the reasoning behind the mistake
How did they construct the syllogism to support their claim? They made the proposition about non-existent subjects the minor premise, and a negative relation the major premise, leading to a valid deduction.
The mistake is not in the syllogistic form but in interpreting the conclusion's truth as proof of a general principle.
3
Spot the author's refutation
Bahr Ulum argues that the utmost this syllogism shows is that a true conclusion can be produced, but it does not entail that the premises are true. A false premise can produce a true conclusion.
This is a classic refutation of the fallacy of affirming the consequent or confusing validity with soundness.
4
Apply the distinction
Use an Islamic science example to distinguish validity (correct form) from soundness (true premises). For instance, the syllogism 'Everything temporal is created; the world is eternal; therefore the world is created' is valid in form but unsound because the minor premise is false.
The distinction is crucial in Kalam critiques of philosophical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَقُولُ: وَلَكَ أَنْ تَسْتَدِلَّ مِنْ هَهُنَا عَلَى عَدَمِ اسْتِدْعَاءِ تِلْكَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْوُجُودَ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ؛ فَإِنَّ مَا مَوْضُوعُهُ مَعْدُومٌ إِنْ تَجْعَلْهَا صُغْرَى، وَتَجْعَلَ النِّسْبَةَ السَّلْبِيَّةَ مَوْضُوعَةً فِي الْكُبْرَى أَنْتَجَتْ أَلْبَتَّةَ، فَلَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُوجَبَةً سَالِبَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ لَزِمَ إِنْتَاجُ سَالِبَةِ الصُّغْرَى فَيَجِبُ كَوْنُهَا سَالِبَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ مَعَ عَدَمِ الْمَوْضُوعِ. فَتَدَبَّرْ؛ فَإِنَّ فِيهِ غَلَطًا فَاحِشًا؛ فَإِنَّ غَايَةَ مَا لَزِمَ إِنْتَاجُ الْمُوجَبَةِ السَّالِبَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ مَعَ عَدَمِ الْمَوْضُوعِ نَتِيجَةً صَادِقَةً، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ صِدْقُهَا؛ فَإِنَّ الْمُقَدِّمَةَ الْكَاذِبَةَ رُبَّمَا تَنْتِجُ نَتِيجَةً صَادِقَةً. هَذَا وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ، وَهُوَ الْعَاصِمُ.
I say: You may deduce from this that the affirmative does not require existence for the subject. For if its subject is non-existent, if you make it a minor premise, and make the negative relation the subject in the major premise, it produces absolutely. So if it were not an affirmative with a negative predicate, it would necessitate the production of a negative minor premise; thus it must be a negative predicate with non-existence of the subject. But ponder! For there is a glaring mistake: the utmost that is necessitated is the production of a true conclusion by the affirmative with a negative predicate despite the non-existence of the subject; and it does not entail its truth. For a false premise may produce a true conclusion. This, and Allah knows best, and He is the protector.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a critical remark by the author (Bahr Ulum) on the previous analysis of the void syllogism. Someone might mistakenly infer that since the syllogism produces a true conclusion even though the subject 'void' does not exist, an affirmative proposition does not require the existence of its subject. The author refutes this by pointing out that the truth of the conclusion does not guarantee the truth of the premises. A false premise can lead to a true conclusion. Therefore, the inference that the affirmative does not require subject existence is not valid. This is a common logical fallacy called 'affirming the consequent' or confusing validity with soundness.

Key Terms
مقدمة كاذبة
نتيجة صادقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the subject does exist

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conclusion alone does not guarantee the truth of its premises; a valid argument can have false premises and still yield a true conclusion.

Does this prove that the Qur'an is created? Explain using Bahr Ulum's point that a false premise can yield a true conclusion, so the conclusion does not verify the premise.
?
Find an example from usul al-fiqh where a ruling is derived from a weak premise chain (e.g., a weak hadith) but the ruling is nonetheless correct according to other evidence. Explain why the truth of the ruling does not validate the weak premise.

Definition of Burhan: The Demonstrative Syllogism
Author defines burhan and clarifies the need for valid figure conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia of burhan and explain why a syllogism with an invalid figure cannot be considered burhan even if its premises are certain.

Core Idea
Burhan is not just any syllogism; it requires both certain premises and valid form.

In this passage, the author defines burhan as a syllogism composed of certain premises. He then anticipates an objection: does a second-figure syllogism with two affirmative certainties also count as burhan? His answer reveals a crucial distinction: only syllogisms that satisfy the conditions of their figure are truly called qiyas, and therefore burhan. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating arguments in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of burhan
Find the statement: 'البرهان هو القياس اليقيني المقدمات'. The genus is 'qiyas' (syllogism) and the differentia is 'yaqini al-muqaddimat' (certain premises).
This is a hadd haqiqi.
2
Analyze the objection
The objector points to a second-figure syllogism with two affirmative certainties. Why might someone think this is burhan? Because the premises are certain, but the figure is invalid without a negative premise.
The author says such a syllogism is not a real syllogism; it is only called so loosely.
3
Distinguish real vs. tolerant usage
The author distinguishes between 'قياس حقيقة' (real syllogism) and 'يطلق عليها على التسامح' (called syllogism by tolerance). Apply this to fiqh: if a qiyas fails the 'illa condition, it is not a valid qiyas.
Formal validity is essential for burhan.
4
Relate to Islamic sciences
Think of a kalam argument that uses a second figure with two affirmatives. Why is it unsound? In the second figure, one premise must be negative. Without it, no conclusion follows.
This helps in evaluating theological proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الْأَوَّلُ: الْبُرْهانُ (١) ، وهو القياس اليقيني المقدمات. فإن قلت: هذا القياس البرهاني وما يصدق على الشكل الثاني المؤلف من موجبتين من اليقينيات، فالصواب أن يتعلق به من يقال: المنتج يقينا؟ قلت: الأشكال الغير المشتملة على شرائطها ليس قياسا حقيقة، وإنما يطلق عليها على التسامح. ثم لما كان في توهم البعض أن اليقينيات منحصرة في العقليات أزال بقوله: عقلية كانت أو نقلية؛ فإن النقل قد يشد النقل إذا كان خبر من يمتنع عليه الكذب، كخبر الله تعالى ورسوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وخبر أهل الإجماع. نعم النقل الصرف ليس كذلك إذ لا بد في القياس من المقدمتين والصورة، والنقل لا يفي بها. واليقين المستعمل في هذه الصناعة هو الاعتقاد الجازم بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض، خرج به الظن؛ المطابق للواقع، خرج به الجهل المركب؛ الثابت الضروري بحيث لا يمكن الزوال، قالوا: خرج به يقين المقلد، وفيه تأمل. وصناعة البرهان ملكة يُقْتَدَرُ بها على تأليف البرهان لتحصيل هذا اليقين.)
The first: demonstration, and it is the syllogism that yields certainty from premises. If you say: This demonstrative syllogism [applies] to what is true of the second figure composed of two affirmative premises from certainties, then the correct thing is that it should be said about it: it produces certainty? I say: The figures that do not include their conditions are not truly syllogisms; they are only called so by tolerance. Then, since some imagined that certainties are limited to intellectual matters, he removed that by saying: whether intellectual or transmitted; for transmission may strengthen reasoning if it is a report from one who cannot lie, like the report of Allah Almighty and His Messenger and his family and companions, and the report of the people of consensus. Yes, purely transmitted [premises] are not like that because the syllogism requires two premises and a form, and transmission alone does not fulfill that. And certainty used in this art is firm belief, such that it does not admit the opposite — by this opinion is excluded; corresponding to reality — by this compound ignorance is excluded; stable/necessary such that it cannot be removed — they said: by this the certainty of the blind follower is excluded, but there is contemplation about that. And the art of demonstration is a faculty by which one is able to compose a demonstration to attain this certainty.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage is an expanded definition of demonstration (البرهان). The author first states that burhan is a syllogism whose premises are certain. Then he addresses a potential objection: in the second figure, a syllogism with two affirmative premises from certainties is not valid because it lacks the conditions of the second figure (which requires one negative premise). The author replies that such invalid forms are not called syllogisms in reality, only loosely. Next, he clarifies that certain premises can be either intellectual (عقلية) or transmitted (نقلية), such as reports from Allah, the Prophet, or the consensus of scholars. However, purely transmitted premises (without intellectual grounding) do not satisfy the structure of a syllogism, which requires two premises and a logical form. Then the author defines certainty (اليقين) technically: it is firm belief that does not allow its opposite (excluding mere opinion), that corresponds to reality (excluding compound ignorance), and is stable/necessary such that it cannot be removed (excluding the blind follower's belief, though he notes there is consideration about that). Finally, he defines the art of demonstration as a faculty (ملكة) that enables one to compose demonstrations to attain such certainty. This passage clarifies both the scope and conditions of demonstrative reasoning.

Key Terms
البرهان
القياس اليقيني
النقل الصرف
ملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a second-figure syllogism with two affirmative certainties not a real burhan?
A) Because its premises are not certain.
B) Because it lacks the conditions of its figure.
C) Because it uses transmitted premises.
D) Because it does not produce certainty.

Key Takeaway
Burhan is a syllogism with certain premises that also meets the formal conditions of its figure; only such a syllogism yields certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Imagine an opponent argues using a second-figure syllogism: 'Some animals are not rational; All humans are rational; Therefore no human is an animal.' Is this a valid burhan? Explain using the author's distinction.

Conditions of Certainty (al-Yaqin)
Author defines certainty with three conditions and discusses their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three conditions of certainty and identify which type of belief each condition excludes.

Core Idea
Certainty is not just strong belief; it must be firm, correspond to reality, and be immune to removal.

After defining burhan, the author turns to its target: certainty. He gives a technical definition of اليقين that comprises three conditions. These conditions separate genuine certainty from mere opinion, compound ignorance, and even the belief of a blind follower. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating whether a conclusion truly qualifies as demonstrative knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three conditions
Find the phrase: 'الاعتقاد الجازم بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض، المطابق للواقع، الثابت الضروري بحيث لا يمكن الزوال'. Each condition excludes something: (1) firm belief excludes opinion (zann), (2) correspondence excludes compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), (3) stability excludes blind following (taqlid).
The third condition is subject to contemplation (ta'ammul) as the author notes.
2
Apply conditions to examples
Consider the belief that the world is created. It is firm, corresponds to reality, and for a mutakallim with proof, it is stable. But for a mere follower, is it stable? The author says there is doubt about excluding the follower's belief.
This shows that certainty requires intellectual grounding.
3
Connect to transmitted premises
The author says transmitted premises from an infallible source can be certain. How does this fit the third condition? If the transmission is backed by evidence, the belief can be stable. But if it is just blind acceptance (naql sirf), it may not be.
This links the two parts of the passage.
4
Evaluate the author's caveat
The author says 'وفيه تأمل' about excluding the blind follower. Why? Perhaps because a simple believer might have a stable belief through divine grace. Later scholars debated this.
This shows the complexity of defining certainty in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation of Two Skeptical Objections to Demonstration
Author refutes two arguments against demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute two skeptical objections to demonstration: infinite regress (al-tasalsul) and circular reasoning (al-dawr).

Core Idea
The two skeptical objections to demonstration fail because they ignore immediate certainties and commit logical fallacies.

In this passage, the author defends the validity of demonstration (burhan) against two skeptical attacks. The first objection claims that demonstration leads to an infinite regress of proofs. The second objection claims that premises can be proven by circular reasoning. The author shows why both objections are invalid, relying on the foundational principle that certain premises can be known without further proof and that circular reasoning is a form of begging the question (musadara).

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection: infinite regress
Find the claim that demonstration is impossible because every premise would itself need a demonstration, leading to an endless chain (tasalsul). Note the reasoning: 'if premises must be certain, they must themselves be demonstrated, ad infinitum.'
The author implies that some premises are immediately certain (badihiyyat) and need no proof, stopping the regress.
2
Understand why infinite regress is not a valid objection
Recognize that the skeptical argument assumes all certain premises require demonstration, but the author holds that first principles are known without demonstration. Thus the regress does not arise for sound demonstrations.
3
Identify the second objection: circular reasoning
Find the claim that the premises of a demonstration can be proven by using the original conclusion in a circle (dawr). The skeptic says: 'Prove the conclusion from premises, then prove those premises from the conclusion.'
4
Recognize the fallacy in circular reasoning
The author points out that this is a logical fallacy: begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlab). It also leads to a thing being prior to itself (taqaddum 'ala nafsih), which is impossible. The phrase 'the dependent of a dependent is dependent' (mawquf al-mawquf mawquf) shows the circular dependency makes both premises and conclusion unsupported.
5
Assess the author's refutation
Both objections are refuted by showing they either rely on a false assumption (that all premises need proof) or commit a logical fallacy. Thus demonstration remains a valid source of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه ظن قوم أن لا يقين إلا من البرهان، فمنهم من قال: لا يمكن تحصيل اليقين من البرهان، وما هو مستعمل في العلوم ليس برهانا، وإلا لزم التسلسل؛ فإن مقدماته يجب أن تكون يقينية؛ إذ لا يقين من غير اليقينيات، وهكذا يتسلسل. ومنهم من اعترف بالبرهان، وقال يستدل على مقدماته بالدور، بأن يحصل مطلوب من مقدمات، ويحصل تلك المقدمات بمقدمات أخر، ثم يرجع ويثبت تلك المقدمات بأصل المطلوب، فلا، ويلزمهم المصادرة على المطلوب الأول والتقدم على نفسه؛ لأن موقوف الموقوف موقوف.
Then know that some people thought that there is no certainty except from demonstration. Among them are those who said: certainty cannot be obtained from demonstration, and what is used in the sciences is not demonstration, otherwise an infinite regress would follow; because its premises must be certain, since there is no certainty from non-certainties, and so on ad infinitum. And among them are those who acknowledged demonstration, and said that its premises are proven by circular reasoning, such that a desired conclusion is obtained from premises, and those premises are obtained from other premises, then one returns and proves those premises by the original desired conclusion — that is not valid, and it necessitates begging the question (musadara) and priority over itself (taqaddum 'ala nafsih), because the thing on which a dependent thing depends is itself dependent.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of two skeptical positions regarding demonstration. The first position denies that certainty can be obtained through demonstration because it leads to infinite regress (التسلسل): if demonstrative premises must themselves be certain, then each premise requires its own demonstration, ad infinitum. The author implies that this regress is not a valid objection because certain premises can be immediately known (badihiyyat) without further demonstration, though the text does not elaborate. The second position acknowledges demonstration but claims its premises are proven by circular reasoning (الدور): one proves a conclusion from premises, then proves those premises using the original conclusion. The author rejects this because it is a form of begging the question (المصادرة على المطلوب), and it also involves the logical impossibility of a thing being prior to itself (التقدم على النفس). The phrase 'موقوف الموقوف موقوف' means that if A depends on B and B depends on A, then both are dependent on nothing, which is impossible. Thus, both objections to demonstration are invalid. This passage strengthens the case for demonstration as a valid source of certainty.

Key Terms
التسلسل
الدور
المصادرة على المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in the argument that uses circular reasoning (الدور) to prove the premises of demonstration?
A) It leads to infinite regress
B) It results in false certainty
C) It begs the question (المصادرة)
D) It relies on imagination

Key Takeaway
The two skeptical objections to demonstration—infinite regress and circular reasoning—are refuted by the recognition of immediate certainties and the identification of logical fallacies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Three Conditions of Certainty (Yaqin)
Definition of certainty and its components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions of certainty (yaqin) and test any belief claim against them.

Core Idea
Certainty requires firm belief, correspondence to reality, and unshakable stability.

The text opens by defining the central epistemic concept of the art: certainty (yaqin). The author gives a tripartite definition excluding conjecture, ignorance, and blind faith. Understanding this definition is crucial because it sets the standard for all demonstrative knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of yaqin
Find the sentence starting with 'وَالْيَقِينُ... هُوَ الِاعْتِقَادُ الْجَازِمُ...' in the Arabic passage. It is in parentheses.
2
Extract the three conditions
The text gives: 1) i'tiqad jazim (firm belief) excluding zann; 2) mutabiq li'l-waqi' (matching reality) excluding jahl murakkab; 3) thabit (stable) excluding the muqallid's certainty (with qualification).
3
Test each condition with an Islamic law example
For condition 1: A jurist who says a ruling is obligatory with doubt has no yaqin. Condition 2: A person who mistakenly thinks he knows the ruling suffers jahl murakkab. Condition 3: A layperson's taqlid may be firm but can be shaken; thus not thabit.
4
Consider the author's remark about the muqallid
The author comments 'fihi ta'ammul' – there is consideration. Some scholars argue that the muqallid's belief is not necessary (dharuri) and could be dislodged, so it does not meet the third condition fully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم
القياس من المقدمتين والصورة، والنقل لا يفي بها.
(وَالْيَقِينُ) المستعمل في هذه الصناعة (هُوَ الِاعْتِقَادُ الْجَازِمُ)، بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض، خرج به الظن (الْمُطَابِقُ للواقع، خرج به الجهل المركب، (الثَّابِتُ) الضروري بحيث لا يمكن الزوال، قالوا: خرج به يقين المقلد، وفيه تأمل.
ثم اعلم أنه ظن قوم أن لا يقين إلا من البرهان، فمنهم من قال: لا يمكن تحصيل اليقين من البرهان، وما هو مستعمل في العلوم ليس برهانا، وإلا لزم التسلسل؛ فإن مقدماته يجب أن تكون يقينية؛ إذ لا يقين من غير اليقينيات، وهكذا يتسلسل ومنهم من اعترف بالبرهان، وقال يستدل على مقدماته بالدور، بأن يحصل مطلوب من مقدمات، ويحصل تلك المقدمات بمقدمات أخر، ثم يرجع ويثبت تلك المقدمات بأصل المطلوب، فلا تسلسل، ويلزمهم المصادرة على المطلوب الأول والتقدم على نفسه؛ لأن موقوف الموقوف موقوف.
والصناعات الخمس
وهذا كله إنما نشأ من ظنهم الكاسد أن اليقين لا يحصل إلا من البرهان، بل ههنا مقدمات يقينية بنفسها، ينتهي إليها البرهان. نعم، العلم اليقيني بالنظريات لا يحصل إلا بالبرهان، وأشار المصنف إلى تلك المقدمات بقوله:
(وَأُصُولُهَا الْأَوْلِيَّات، وهي ما يجزم العقل فيها بمجرد الطَّرَفَيْن، نَظريَّا) كان الطرفان، (أو بديهيا وتتفاوت جلاء وخفاء) تصور الطرفين، وبداهة البديهى كعلم العلم منها، وهو الحق.
ظن قوم أن بداهة البديهي ضرورية، واختاره المصنف وشدد، حتى عدّ من الأوليات، واستدلوا عليه بأن الذهن إذا التفت إلى كيفية حصوله، فإن وجد حصوله بالنظر علم نظرية، وإن وجد حصوله بلا نظر علم بداهة، ولا يحتاج في ذلك إلى دليل. ورد بأنه يجوز أن يكون شيء حصل من غير نظر. وحصل الأشياء الأخر من نظر، ونسي المقدمات وتطاول الزمان، ونسي الحصول، وحينئذ يحتاج إلى الدليل.
وأيضا يجوز أن يكون البديهي خفيا، فأزيل الخفاء بالتنبيه على هيئة شكل من الأشكال، ثم اشتبه الحال بعد تطاول المدة أن هذه الهيئة للتنبيه أو القياس، فحينئذ يحتاج إلى الدليل. وقس عليه التصورات الضرورية.
وقال بعض أجلة المتأخرين : إن التصور النظري إنما يحصل بالكنه، وفيه تصور واحد متعلق بالحد أولا وبالذات وبالمحدود ثانيا وبالعرض، والحد.
Explanation of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. The syllogism consists of two premises and a form, and simple transmission does not suffice. And certainty used in this art is firm belief, such that it does not admit the opposite, by which conjecture is excluded; (it is) matching reality, by which compound ignorance is excluded; (it is) stable and necessary such that it cannot cease. They said: by this the certainty of the blind follower is excluded, but there is consideration. Then know that some people thought that there is no certainty except from demonstration. Some of them said: It is impossible to obtain certainty from demonstration, and what is used in the sciences is not a demonstration, otherwise an infinite regress would follow; for its premises must be certain, since there is no certainty from non-certainties, and so on ad infinitum. And among them, some admitted demonstration and said that one argues for its premises by circularity: that a desired conclusion is obtained from premises, and those premises are obtained from other premises, then one returns and establishes those premises from the original desired conclusion, so there is no regress. But they are guilty of begging the original question and of self-priority, because that on which something depends is dependent. The five arts. All this arose from their corrupt notion that certainty cannot be obtained except from demonstration. Rather, there are here premises certain in themselves, at which the demonstration terminates. Yes, certain knowledge of theoretical matters cannot be obtained except by demonstration, and the author pointed to those premises by his saying: (And their principles are the primary propositions, which are what the mind judges by merely the two endpoints, whether the two endpoints are theoretical or intuitive, and they vary in clarity and obscurity) regarding the conception of the two endpoints, and the primacy of the intuitive like the knowledge of knowledge itself, and that is correct. Some people thought that the primacy of the intuitive is necessary, and the author chose and emphasized this, even counting it among the primary propositions. They argued that the mind, when it attends to how it obtained it: if it finds it obtained by reflection, it knows it is theoretical; if it finds it obtained without reflection, it knows it is intuitive, and no proof is needed for that. But it is refuted by the possibility that something may be obtained without reflection, and other things are obtained by reflection, and the premises are forgotten and time passes, and one forgets the acquisition; then one needs a proof. Also, it is possible that the intuitive is obscure, and its obscurity is removed by prompting concerning a figure of a certain form, then after a long time the situation becomes confused about whether this figure is for prompting or for syllogism; then one needs a proof. And apply the same to necessary conceptions. And some eminent later scholars said: Theoretical conception is only obtained by the essence, and in it there is a single conception related to the definition first and essentially, and to the defined second and accidentally, and the definition...
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum is an epistemological discussion defining certainty (yaqin) and establishing the foundation of demonstration (burhan). The commentary opens by distinguishing certainty from conjecture (zann) and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) through three conditions: firm belief (i'tiqad jazim) that excludes doubt, correspondence to reality (mutabiq li'l-waqi') that excludes falsehood, and stability (thabit) that precludes the possibility of being shaken, which some argue excludes the certainty of a blind follower (muqallid). The author then reports two false positions on certainty: one denies that demonstration yields certainty due to infinite regress (tasalsul), arguing that premises must themselves be certain, leading to an endless chain; the other admits demonstration but tries to avoid regress by circular reasoning (dawr), which is refuted as begging the question (musadara). The author dismisses both as stemming from the corrupt assumption that certainty can only come from demonstration. Instead, he asserts that there are self-evident premises (muqaddimat yaqiniyya bi nafsiha) that terminate the demonstrative chain; these are the 'primary propositions' (awwaliyat), defined as judgments the mind makes solely from the two endpoints (tarafayn) of the proposition, whether those endpoints are theoretical or intuitive. The passage then delves into a dispute about whether the primacy (badaha) of such propositions is necessary or can be mistaken. Some argue that one can always introspect to determine if a belief came by reflection (nazar) or not, but this is refuted by the possibility of forgetting the source of knowledge. Another objection is that an intuitive proposition might be obscure and require prompting (tanbih) via a logical form, which could later be mistaken for a syllogism, thus needing proof. The passage ends with a remark by later scholars on theoretical conception (tasawwur nazari), stating that it only occurs through the essence (kunn) of the definition—a single conception relating primarily to the definition and secondarily to the defined. Throughout, the commentary follows the author's argumentative order, explaining each false view and its refutation, and then clarifying the true foundation of certainty in self-evident principles.

Key Terms
اليقين
الظن
الجهل المركب
المقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what three conditions define certainty (yaqin)?
A) Firm belief, correspondence to reality, stability
B) Firm belief, probability, stability
C) Correspondence to reality, necessity, probability
D) Firm belief, correspondence to reality, probability

Key Takeaway
Certainty (yaqin) in logic is defined by three essential conditions: firm belief, correspondence to reality, and unshakable stability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a statement like 'I am certain that riba is haram.' Check it against the three conditions: Is your belief firm beyond doubt? Does it match the Qur'anic ruling? Is it stable such that no new evidence would change it? Explain.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Refuting the Claim that Certainty Only Comes from Demonstration
Refutation of false views on demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the two false views on demonstration (infinite regress and circularity) using the author's arguments.

Core Idea
Not all certainty depends on demonstration; self-evident truths terminate the regress.

The author attacks a false assumption that yaqin can only be obtained through burhan. Two misguided attempts to justify this assumption—infinite regress and circular reasoning—are presented and dismantled. Understanding this refutation is key to recognizing the foundational role of primary propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false assumption
The author says: 'ظن قوم أن لا يقين إلا من البرهان' — some thought certainty only comes from demonstration. This is the target.
2
Analyze the first false view (infinite regress)
Group 1 said you cannot obtain certainty from demonstration because premises need to be certain, requiring further demonstration ad infinitum. So no demonstration yields certainty.
3
Analyze the second false view (circularity)
Group 2 accepted demonstration but tried to avoid regress by circular justification—premises are proved by other premises, eventually returning to the original conclusion. The author refutes this as begging the question (musadara) and requiring self-priority (taqaddum 'ala nafs).
4
Extract the author's solution
The author rejects the assumption entirely: there are premises that are certain in themselves (muqaddimat yaqiniyya bi nafsiha) at which demonstration terminates. These are the awwaliyat. So regress and circularity are avoided.

شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم
القياس من المقدمتين والصورة، والنقل لا يفي بها.
(وَالْيَقِينُ) المستعمل في هذه الصناعة (هُوَ الِاعْتِقَادُ الْجَازِمُ)، بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض، خرج به الظن (الْمُطَابِقُ للواقع، خرج به الجهل المركب، (الثَّابِتُ) الضروري بحيث لا يمكن الزوال، قالوا: خرج به يقين المقلد، وفيه تأمل.
ثم اعلم أنه ظن قوم أن لا يقين إلا من البرهان، فمنهم من قال: لا يمكن تحصيل اليقين من البرهان، وما هو مستعمل في العلوم ليس برهانا، وإلا لزم التسلسل؛ فإن مقدماته يجب أن تكون يقينية؛ إذ لا يقين من غير اليقينيات، وهكذا يتسلسل ومنهم من اعترف بالبرهان، وقال يستدل على مقدماته بالدور، بأن يحصل مطلوب من مقدمات، ويحصل تلك المقدمات بمقدمات أخر، ثم يرجع ويثبت تلك المقدمات بأصل المطلوب، فلا تسلسل، ويلزمهم المصادرة على المطلوب الأول والتقدم على نفسه؛ لأن موقوف الموقوف موقوف.
والصناعات الخمس
وهذا كله إنما نشأ من ظنهم الكاسد أن اليقين لا يحصل إلا من البرهان، بل ههنا مقدمات يقينية بنفسها، ينتهي إليها البرهان. نعم، العلم اليقيني بالنظريات لا يحصل إلا بالبرهان، وأشار المصنف إلى تلك المقدمات بقوله:

ظن قوم أن بداهة البديهي ضرورية، واختاره المصنف وشدد، حتى عدّ من الأوليات، واستدلوا عليه بأن الذهن إذا التفت إلى كيفية حصوله، فإن وجد حصوله بالنظر علم نظرية، وإن وجد حصوله بلا نظر علم بداهة، ولا يحتاج في ذلك إلى دليل. ورد بأنه يجوز أن يكون شيء حصل من غير نظر. وحصل الأشياء الأخر من نظر، ونسي المقدمات وتطاول الزمان، ونسي الحصول، وحينئذ يحتاج إلى الدليل.
وأيضا يجوز أن يكون البديهي خفيا، فأزيل الخفاء بالتنبيه على هيئة شكل من الأشكال، ثم اشتبه الحال بعد تطاول المدة أن هذه الهيئة للتنبيه أو القياس، فحينئذ يحتاج إلى الدليل. وقس عليه التصورات الضرورية.

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum is an epistemological discussion defining certainty (yaqin) and establishing the foundation of demonstration (burhan). The commentary opens by distinguishing certainty from conjecture (zann) and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) through three conditions: firm belief (i'tiqad jazim) that excludes doubt, correspondence to reality (mutabiq li'l-waqi') that excludes falsehood, and stability (thabit) that precludes the possibility of being shaken, which some argue excludes the certainty of a blind follower (muqallid). The passage then delves into a dispute about whether the primacy (badaha) of such propositions is necessary or can be mistaken. Another objection is that an intuitive proposition might be obscure and require prompting (tanbih) via a logical form, which could later be mistaken for a syllogism, thus needing proof. The passage ends with a remark by later scholars on theoretical conception (tasawwur nazari), stating that it only occurs through the essence (kunn) of the definition—a single conception relating primarily to the definition and secondarily to the defined. Throughout, the commentary follows the author's argumentative order, explaining each false view and its refutation, and then clarifying the true foundation of certainty in self-evident principles.

Key Takeaway
The false assumption that certainty only comes from demonstration leads to infinite regress or circularity; the remedy is self-evident primary propositions.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Primary Propositions (Awwaliyat): Self-Evident Foundations of Knowledge
Definition and dispute about primary propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define awwaliyat, explain the dispute about their intuitive nature, and identify when a proposition may need prompting rather than demonstration.

Core Idea
The mind can judge some propositions immediately upon understanding their terms.

The author introduces awwaliyat as the stopping points for demonstration. They are judgments made 'by merely the two endpoints' — i.e., merely by understanding the subject and predicate. But a dispute arises: is the intuitive character of these propositions self-evident or can it be mistaken? The author presents and refutes the claim that one can always introspect the source of belief.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of awwaliyat
The author defines awwaliyat as 'ما يجزم العقل فيها بمجرد الطرفين' — propositions the mind affirms by merely grasping the two terms (subject and predicate). Example: 'The whole is greater than the part.'
2
Understand the dispute about 'badaha' (self-evidence)
Some thinkers argued that the self-evidence of awwaliyat is necessary (dharuri): by introspection, one can know whether a belief came by reflection or not. The author chose this view and counted awwaliyat among primary propositions.
3
Analyze the refutation of the introspection view
The text presents two objections: (a) one might forget whether a belief was obtained without reflection, especially if time passes and premises are forgotten; (b) an intuitive proposition might be initially obscure and require prompting (tanbih) via a logical form, then later one may confuse prompting with syllogism. So introspection is not reliable.
4
Conclude: awwaliyat are self-evident, but recognizing them may require attention
The author affirms that awwaliyat exist as certain in themselves, but humans may need to be reminded of them. The mind judges them without demonstration, but not without thought.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate

Hissiyyat and Their Role as Premises in Demonstration
Author defends sense perception and explains how it yields universal premises for demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how particular sense perceptions can yield universal premises for demonstrative proof in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Sense perception gives necessary knowledge, and its particular judgments become universal premises through intellectual abstraction.

This passage defends sense perception as a source of knowledge and addresses a key objection: that particular sense perceptions cannot be premises in demonstration because they are transient. The author shows how the intellect, aided by sense, extracts universal judgments that are firm and lasting, making them fit for demonstrative proof. This discussion is crucial for understanding how empirical observations ground theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about sense knowledge
Find where the author declares that sense benefit is from necessary matters, and note his condemnation of those who deny it as deaf and blind.
The author considers sense knowledge as a necessity for any sane person.
2
Understand the objection against sensibles as premises
The objection says sensibles are particular and perish with the cessation of sense, so they cannot yield firm, lasting assent needed for demonstration.
3
See the first solution: abstraction of a universal
The intellect can take a universal matter common among sensibles (e.g., 'every fire is hot') and judge it universally. This judgment can be a premise.
This shows how sense provides material for a universal judgment.
4
See the second solution: intellectual form of particulars
Material particulars exist in the intellect as universal forms (through abstraction). The intellect uses them as a 'mirror' to know the particular, and the judgment is firm.
This applies even without direct sense input, e.g., divine knowledge.
5
Note the role of sense as a preparation
Sense judgment prepares the intellect; sometimes the intellect's judgment is not believed until sense judges first.
Sense is indirectly necessary for human intellectual judgments.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجزئيات (١)، (وَالْمُنْكِرُونَ لِإِفَادَتِهِ)، أي إفادة الحس العلم مطلقا (صُمٌّ (۱)، وَعُمْي (۲) ؛ فإنهم لما أنكروا علم البصر والسمع صاروا كأنهم لا يبصرون ولا يسمعون، فوجودهما كلا وجود والحاصل أن إفادة الحس العلم من كلاوجود الضروريات، ولا يليق بحال عاقل أن ينكره، لا سيما من له الحواس. ثم إن المحسوسات هل تقع مقدمة برهانية أم لا؟ قالوا: لا تقع؛ لأنها علوم جزئية زائلة بزوال الحس، فلا تفيد تصديقا جازما ثابتا ، فتأمل فيه . نعم، للعقل أن يأخذ أمرا كليا مشتركا في المحسوسات (۳) بمعونة الحس، ويحكم عليه حكما حكم الحس على الجزئيات المحسوسة جزئيتها، وغير ذلك(٤)، فهذا الحكم يقع مقدمة في البرهان ، فللحس مدخل ما. وههنا طريق آخر، هو : أنك قد علمت في بحث الكليات أن الجزئيات المادية كما تحصل صورها في الحس فتدركها كذلك قد يحصل في العقل المجرد ، إما من غير حصولها في الحواس، كما في علمه تعالى ـ جل مجده ـ أو بعد الحصول فيها، كما في أنفسنا، وبتلك ينكشف الجزئي كما هو، وإن كانت من حصولها في نفس غير آبية عن الشركة، لكن معلومها آب عنها، فالحكم الذي يحكم العقل بعد جعلها مرآة لملاحظتها على ذلك الجزئي تصدیق جازم ثابت، ويجوز أن يقع مسألة برهانية ومقدمة برهانية. فإن قلت: فما الدخل للحس؟ قلت ربما لا يُصدق بالحكم على الجزئي إلا بعد حكم الحس عليه ، فحكم الحس مُعِدَّ لحكم العقل . هذا والله أعلم ، والعلم عند علام الغيوب.
The particulars (1), and those who deny its benefit, i.e., the benefit of sense to knowledge absolutely, are deaf (1) and blind (2); for when they denied the knowledge of sight and hearing, they became as if they do not see nor hear, so their existence is like non-existence. The conclusion is that the benefit of sense to knowledge is from the necessary matters, and it is not befitting for any sane person to deny it, especially one who has senses. Then, do sensibles occur as a demonstrative premise or not? They said: They do not occur, because they are particular sciences that perish with the cessation of sense, so they do not yield firm, lasting assent. So reflect on that. Yes, the intellect can take a universal matter common among sensibles (3) with the help of sense, and judge it with a judgment like the judgment of sense on particular sensibles in their particularity, and other than that (4), so this judgment occurs as a premise in demonstration, so sense has some input. And here is another way: that you have known in the discussion of universals that the material particulars, as their forms occur in sense so that they perceive them, similarly they may occur in the immaterial intellect, either without occurring in the senses, as in His knowledge – exalted is His majesty – or after occurring in them, as in ourselves. And by those, the particular is disclosed as it is, even though from their occurrence in a soul not averse to sharing, but their object is averse to it. So the judgment that the intellect judges after making them a mirror for observing that particular is a firm, lasting assent, and it may occur as a demonstrative question and a demonstrative premise. If you say: What is the input of sense? I say: Perhaps the judgment about the particular is not believed except after the sense's judgment upon it. So the sense's judgment is a preparation for the intellect's judgment. This is what is known, and Allah knows best, and knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about sense perception (hissiyyat) and its role in demonstrative proof (burhan). The author begins by condemning those who deny that sense gives knowledge, calling them deaf and blind, because sense knowledge is necessary. Then he addresses a question: Can particular sense perceptions serve as premises in demonstration? Some say no, because they are particular and transient. The author agrees that the particular sense perception itself cannot be a premise, but the intellect can abstract a universal from multiple sense experiences, e.g., 'every fire is hot' is a universal judgment derived from sense. This universal judgment can be a premise. He then gives another way: material particulars exist in the mind as universal concepts (by abstraction), and the intellect can use them as mirrors to know particular things. The sense judgment prepares the intellect for its judgment. So sense has an indirect input.

مقدمة برهانية
تصديق جازم
