Two Conditions of Tawatur and Refutation of Objections
Author defines conditions for mutawatir and answers objection about rational reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a mutawatir report and explain how the objection about rational matters is resolved using the distinction between sensory and rational evidence.

Core Idea
Tawatur requires sense perception and equal numbers at every level; it cannot directly prove rational truths.

The concept of tawatur (mass transmission) is central to establishing certainty in Islamic knowledge. This passage clarifies two necessary conditions for a report to be considered mutawatir: it must be based on sense perception, and the number of narrators at each level must prevent collusion. The author also addresses a potential objection about using tawatur for rational matters, showing how the method actually works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage and locate the two conditions stated by the author. The first condition is that the report must end with sense perception (al-intiha' ila al-hiss). The second is that the number of narrators at each level must be such that the mind cannot conceive of them colluding on a lie (musawat al-taraf al-wasat).
These conditions ensure the certainty produced by tawatur.
2
Understand why purely rational matters are excluded
Note the author's statement 'no tawatur in rational matters'. Reflect on why rational truths cannot be established by tawatur directly. Tawatur relies on sensory reports; rational truths are known by intellect, not transmission.
The objection arises because scholars use tawatur to prove rational doctrines like the obligation of prayer.
3
Analyze the objection and its resolution
The objector points to doctrines (prayer obligation, Hour, etc.) that scholars prove by tawatur. The author responds: tawatur is not used directly on these rational matters, but to prove the existence of a sensory report (the Prophet's statement) that indicates them. The tawatur establishes the truth of the report, then the rational inference follows from the report.
This preserves the condition that tawatur must end with sense perception.
4
Examine the counterexamples
The author gives counterexamples: the Jewish claim of tawatur that Jesus was killed and that no prophet comes after Moses, and the Rawafid claim of tawatur about Ali's caliphate. These fail because they originated from a small group, not from a sufficient number at the first level.
This illustrates the second condition: the chain must have been well-established from the start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نَعَمْ، لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنْ شرطين، أحدهما الانتهاء إلى الحس، فلا تواتر في العقليات فإن قلت: قد استدل السلف والخلف بالتواتر على كون الصلاة فرضا، وأن الساعة حق، وعذاب القبر حق، والشفاعة حق، مع كونها عقليات صرفة؟ قلت: لا يستدل بالتواتر على هذه الأمور، بل يستدل به على وجود قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، الدال عليها دلالة قطعية، وهو مسموع محسوس، ويستدل به على تلك الأمور؛ لكون المخبر بها صادقا من غير ريب وارتياب. وثانيهما مساواة الطرف الوسط، بأن يكون في كل مرتبة مبلغ يحيل العقل تواطئهم على الكذب، لا كادعاء اليهود تواتر قتل عيسى عليه السلام وقولهم أنه لا نبي بعدي، ولا كادعاء الروافض تواتر نص تسليم الخلافة إلى أمير المؤمنين علي كرم الله وجهه ودعواه ذلك لكن لم يظفر؛ فإن هذه الأقوال قد وضعها في بدأ الأمر جماعة قليلة، ثم نقل عن الواضعين تواترا.
Yes, it is necessary for it (i.e., mutawatir) to have two conditions: one of them is that it ends with sense perception, so there is no tawatur in rational matters. For if you say: The early and later generations have used tawatur to prove that prayer is obligatory, that the Hour is true, that the punishment of the grave is true, and that intercession is true, while these are purely rational matters? I say: Tawatur is not used to prove these matters directly, rather it is used to prove the existence of the statement of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family and Companions, which indicates them with decisive indication, and that statement is audible and sensible, and then it is used to prove those matters because the one who reports them is truthful without any doubt. And the second condition is the equality of the extreme and the middle, meaning that in every level there is a number that prevents the mind from conceiving of them colluding on a lie, unlike the claim of the Jews of tawatur of the killing of Jesus, peace be upon him, and their statement that there is no prophet after me, and unlike the claim of the Rawafid of tawatur of the text of the caliphate to the Commander of the Faithful, Ali, may Allah honor his face, and their claim of that, but it did not succeed; for these statements were originated in the beginning by a small group, then they were narrated from the originators as mutawatir.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on the Sullam al-Ulum, explaining the conditions for a mutawatir report (khabar mutawatir). The author states that two conditions are necessary. First, the report must be based on sense perception (al-hiss), meaning the transmitted information must be about something observable, not purely rational. Hence, there is no tawatur in purely intellectual matters. An objection arises: scholars have used tawatur to prove rational matters like the obligation of prayer or the truth of the Hour. The answer distinguishes between using tawatur to establish that the Prophet (PBUH) said something (which is a sensory report) and then using that statement to derive the rational judgment. The second condition is that the number of narrators at each level (tabaqah) must be sufficient that the mind cannot imagine them colluding on a lie. The author gives counterexamples: the Jews' claim of tawatur that Jesus was killed and that there is no prophet after Moses, and the Rawafid's claim of tawatur about the appointment of Ali as caliph, because these originated from a small group and were later attributed to the original narrators as if they were mutawatir. The point is that the chain must have been well-established from the start.

Key Terms

الانتهاء إلى الحس
مساواة الطرف الوسط

Key Takeaway
A mutawatir report must be based on sense perception and have sufficient narrators at each level to preclude collusion, and it cannot directly prove purely rational truths.

The Epistemic Status of Mutawatir Reports
Author summarizes and critiques views on mutawatir reports and their epistemic status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three main positions on whether mutawatir reports produce necessary knowledge and identify the author's critique of the reflection-based view.

Core Idea
Mutawatir knowledge is immediate and necessary, not dependent on reflection.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum discusses a key epistemological question in Islamic logic: do mutawatir reports produce knowledge immediately or only after reflection? The author summarizes the consensus of philosophers and Ahl al-Haqq, notes al-Ghazali's unique classification, and sharply criticizes a minority view. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping how certain premises function in demonstrative arguments (burhan).

Guided Path
1
Identify the false example of tawatur
The author begins with a cautionary example: the Rafidite claim that there is a mutawatir text designating 'Ali as caliph. This example shows that what appears to be mutawatir may be fabricated by a small group and then transmitted widely.
This serves as a reminder that tawatur is not merely popular transmission but requires conditions that prevent collusion.
2
Outline the three views on mutawatir's epistemic status
The author presents three positions: (1) The philosophers and majority of Ahl al-Haqq hold that mutawatir yields necessary (daruri) knowledge without reflection. (2) Imam al-Ghazali classifies it as fitriyyat (innate propositions), which is a subtype of necessary knowledge. (3) A minority, including some Rafidites and a few from our school, claim it benefits only after reflection (nazar).
Al-Ghazali's view is noted as not far-fetched, indicating the author's openness to that classification.
3
Analyze the author's critique of the reflection view
The author dismisses the reflection view as false (bāṭil) by appealing to necessity: when a large group reports a report, the mind assents without any intellectual effort or deliberation. Denying this is obstinacy (mukābara).
This is a direct epistemological argument based on common experience of how testimony works.
4
Connect to demonstrative premises
The author ends by asking whether mutawatir can serve as a premise in a burhan (demonstrative argument), and refers to the earlier discussion of mahsūsāt (sense-perceptible premises). This implies that mutawatir is considered a type of sense-perceptible premise that yields certainty.
This links the epistemological discussion to the theory of demonstration in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه لا نبي بعدي، ولا كادعاء الروافض تواتر نص تسليم الخلافة إلى أمير المؤمنين على كرم الله وجهه ودعواه ذلك، لكن لم يظفر؛ فإن هذه الأقوال قد وضعها في بدء الأمر جماعة قليلة، ثم نقل عن الواضعين تواترا. ثم إن الفلاسفة وجمهور أهل الحق اتفقوا على أن المتواتر يوجب العلم بالضرورة، لكن الإمام حجة الإسلام منا زعم أنه من الفطريات، خلافا للجمهور، وليس قوله بعيدا. وذهب شرذمة قليلة من الروافض وبعض منا إلى أنه يفيد بالنظر، وهو قول باطل؛ فإن الضرورة حاكمة بأن تلك الجماعة إذا أخبرت بخبر يصدق العقل من غير إعمال فكر وروية، وإنكاره مكابرة. والكلام في أنها هل تقع مقدمة برهانية أم لا؟ يعرف بما ذكر في المحسوسات.
That there is no prophet after me, and not like the Rafidites' claim of tawatur of the text of handing over the caliphate to the Commander of the Faithful (may God honor his face) and his claim of that, but he did not succeed; for these sayings were put at the beginning of the matter by a small group, then it was transmitted from the originators by tawatur. Then the philosophers and the majority of the people of truth agreed that the mutawatir necessitates knowledge necessarily, but the Imam Hujjat al-Islam from us claimed that it is from the fitriyyat, contrary to the majority, and his saying is not far-fetched. And a small faction of the Rafidites and some from us went that it benefits by reflection, and it is a false saying; for necessity judges that when that group reports a report, the mind believes it without exercising thought and deliberation, and its denial is obstinacy. And the discussion of whether it can be a demonstrative premise is known from what was mentioned in the sensibles.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about mutawatir reports. It begins by giving an example of a false claim of tawatur (the Rafidite claim about the caliphate) to illustrate that some claims of tawatur are fabricated by a small group and then transmitted as if mutawatir. Then it states the consensus of philosophers and most Ahl al-Haqq that a true mutawatir report yields necessary knowledge (daruri). However, Imam al-Ghazali is noted to have considered mutawatir among fitriyyat (innate propositions), which is not unreasonable. A minority view, including some Rafidites and a few from among us, holds that mutawatir requires reflection (nazar) to produce knowledge. The author dismisses this as false, arguing that it is a matter of necessity: when a multitude reports something, the mind assents without any intellectual effort. Denying this is mere obstinacy. Finally, it asks whether mutawatir can serve as a premise in a demonstrative argument (burhan), referring back to the discussion of sense-perceptible premises (mahsusat). The author implies that the answer is known from that discussion, likely that mutawatir is a kind of sense-perceptible premise.

فطريات

Key Takeaway
Mutawatir reports produce necessary, immediate knowledge and can serve as demonstrative premises, contrary to the view that they require reflective reasoning.

Burhan limmi and burhan inni defined by causal role
Author defines two types of demonstration based on the middle term's causal role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between burhan limmi and burhan inni based on whether the middle term is a cause in reality or only in the mind, and identify which type a given demonstration belongs to.

Core Idea
In demonstration, the middle term must be a cause in the mind; burhan limmi adds causation in reality.

This passage from a classical logic text defines two fundamental types of demonstration: burhan limmi and burhan inni. The distinction hinges on whether the middle term is a cause of the judgment in reality or only in the mind. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the strength of proofs in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of demonstration
In the passage, the author defines burhan limmi and burhan inni. Locate these terms and note the defining condition for each.
2
Extract the causal condition for burhan limmi
For burhan limmi, the middle term must be a cause of the judgment in reality. That means it explains why the predicate (al-akbar) holds for the subject (al-asghar).
The cause in reality does not necessarily mean existential causation; it can be the cause of the attribution.
3
Extract the causal condition for burhan inni
For burhan inni, the middle term is a cause only in the mind. It proves that the predicate belongs, but does not reveal the real reason. The middle term may be an effect of the judgment or unrelated.
The author notes that in burhan inni the middle term is 'not a cause for it in reality, but only in the mind.'
4
Note the scope limitation
The author remarks that these definitions apply only to categorical syllogisms (al-qiyas al-iqtirani), not conditional ones. Reflect on why the causal condition might differ in conditional syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأوسط لا بد أن يكون علة للحكم في الذهن، فهو ذلك. فالبرهان اللمي إن كان علة للحكم في الواقع، أي لثبوت الأكبر للأصغر، سواء كان علة لوجود الأكبر في نفسه أو لا، فالبرهان المشتمل عليه يسمى برهانا مطلقا أيضا. وإن لم يكن علة له في الواقع، بل في الذهن فقط، كان الوسط معلولا للحكم في الواقع، أو ليس بينهما علاقة العلية. ثم هذان التعريفان للبرهان اللمي والإني لا يتناولان ما كان منه على هيئة القياس الاستثنائي، كما لا يخفى، فالأولى أن يقال: البرهان إن كان...
Then the middle term must be a cause of the judgment in the mind, so it is that. So the burhan limmi: if it is a cause of the judgment in reality, i.e., for the affirmation of the major term for the minor, whether it is a cause for the existence of the major in itself or not, then the demonstration containing it is called absolute demonstration as well. And if it is not a cause for it in reality, but only in the mind, then the middle term is an effect of the judgment in reality, or there is no causal relation between them. Then these two definitions of burhan limmi and burhan inni do not cover what is in the form of a conditional syllogism, as is not hidden, so it is better to say: the demonstration if it is...
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage defines the two main types of demonstration (burhan) in Aristotelian logic: burhan limmi (demonstration of the reason why) and burhan inni (demonstration of the fact). The key is the causal relationship of the middle term. The author states that in any demonstration, the middle term must be the cause (illa) of the judgment in the mind. In burhan limmi, the middle term is not only the cause of the judgment in the mind but also the cause of the judgment in reality (i.e., it explains why the subject has the predicate). In burhan inni, the middle term is not the cause in reality; it may be an effect or merely coincidental. The author then notes that these definitions only apply to categorical syllogisms, not conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), so a broader definition is needed. The passage ends with an incomplete suggestion for a better definition.

Key Terms
برهان لمي
برهان إني
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes burhan limmi from burhan inni?
A) Burhan limmi uses only categorical syllogisms; burhan inni uses conditionals.
B) In burhan limmi, the middle term is the cause in reality; in burhan inni, it is not.
C) Burhan limmi is for physics; burhan inni for metaphysics.
D) Burhan limmi proves existence; burhan inni proves definition.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between burhan limmi and burhan inni lies in whether the middle term is a cause of the judgment in reality or only in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following syllogism is burhan limmi or burhan inni, and explain why: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' (Answer: burhan limmi, because the middle term 'temporally originated' is the real cause of the world's need for a cause.)
?

Demonstration -- Burhan

5 min
Burhan Limmi and Burhan Inni: Defining Demonstrations by Causality
Author defines burhan limmi and burhan inni based on causality of the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between burhan limmi and burhan inni based on the causality of the middle term, and apply the criterion to identify the type of demonstration in a given kalam argument.

Core Idea
The middle term's causality in reality or mind alone determines whether a demonstration is limmi (why) or inni (that).

In this lesson, we explore the classification of demonstrations (burhan) based on the role of the middle term. The author distinguishes between two types: limmi, where the middle term is a cause for the judgment in external reality, and inni, where it is only a cause in the mind. This distinction is foundational in Islamic logic, especially in proving the existence and attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in a demonstration
In a syllogism, locate the term repeated in both premises; this is the awsat (middle term).
For example, in 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing has a composer,' the middle term is 'composed'.
2
Determine if the middle term is a cause for the judgment in reality
Ask: Does the middle term explain why the predicate applies to the subject in the external world? If yes, it is a cause in reality, and the demonstration is limmi.
3
If not, check if it is a cause in the mind only
If the middle term is only a cause for the mind's judgment (e.g., it is an effect or concomitant of the judgment), then it is inni.
4
Apply the criterion to the example: 'Every composed thing has a composer'
The middle term 'composed' is a real cause for 'has a composer' because composition requires a composer. Thus, it is limmi.
The author uses this as an example of limmi demonstration.
5
Apply the criterion to another example: 'Bodies are possible, and every possible thing has a cause'
The middle term 'possible' is a real cause for 'has a cause' because possibility entails a cause. This too is limmi.
The author confirms this as limmi, emphasizing the criterion is causality for the judgment, not for the major term's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْأَوْسَطُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ عِلَّةً لِلْحُكْمِ فِي الذِّهْنِ فَقَطْ، فَهُوَ ذَلِكَ الْبُرْهَانُ اللَّمِيُّ. وَإِنْ كَانَ عِلَّةً لِلْحُكْمِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، أَيْ لِثُبُوتِ الْأَكْبَرِ لِلْأَصْغَرِ، سَوَاءٌ كَانَ عِلَّةً لِوُجُودِ الْأَكْبَرِ فِي نَفْسِهِ أَوْ لَا، فَالْبُرْهَانُ الْمُشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهِ يُسَمَّى بُرْهَانًا مُطْلَقًا أَيْضًا. وَإِلَّا يَكُنْ عِلَّةً لَهُ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، بَلْ فِي الذِّهْنِ فَقَطْ، كَانَ الْأَوْسَطُ مَعْلُولًا لِلْحُكْمِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ أَوْ لَا، فَهُوَ الْبُرْهَانُ الْإِنِّيُّ. الْأَوَّلَى أَنْ يُقَالَ: الْبُرْهَانُ إِنْ كَانَ أَوْسَطُهُ سِوَى الِاسْتِثْنَائِيِّ عِلَّةً لِلْحُكْمِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ فَلَمِّيٌّ، وَإِلَّا فَإِنِّيٌّ فِيهِمَا. وَالِاسْتِدْلَالُ بِوُجُودِ الْمَعْلُولِ لِشَيْءٍ عَلَى أَنَّ لَهُ عِلَّةً مَّا، أَوْ عَلَّتَهُ وَاجِبَ الْوُجُودِ، كَقَوْلِنَا: كُلُّ جِسْمٍ مُؤَلَّفٌ، وَكُلُّ مُؤَلَّفٍ فَلَهُ مُؤَلِّفٌ، وَكَقَوْلِنَا: الْأَجْسَامُ مُمْكِنَةٌ، وَكُلُّ مُمْكِنٍ لَهُ سَبَبٌ وَاجِبُ الْوُجُودِ حَيٌّ عَلِيمٌ قَدِيرٌ مُرِيدٌ، إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ بُرْهَانٌ لَمِّيٌّ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ. فَإِنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي بُرْهَانِ اللَّمِّ عِلِّيَّةُ الْأَوْسَطِ وَمَا فِي حُكْمِهِ لِثُبُوتِ الْأَكْبَرِ لِلْأَصْغَرِ، لَا لِثُبُوتِهِ فِي نَفْسِهِ. وَبَيْنَهُمَا بَوْنٌ بَيِّنٌ. وَالْأَوَّلُ مُتَحَقِّقٌ فِيمَا نَحْنُ فِيهِ، فَإِنَّ ثُبُوتَ الْمُؤَلِّفِ لِلْمُؤَلَّفِ بِوَاسِطَةِ اللَّامِ مَعْلُولٌ لِلتَّأْلِيفِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ وُجُودُهُ فِي نَفْسِهِ مَعْلُولًا مِنْهُ.
Then the middle term must be a cause for the judgment in the mind only; that is the burhan limmi. And if it is a cause for the judgment in reality, that is, for the affirmation of the major term to the minor term, whether it is a cause for the existence of the major term in itself or not, then the demonstration that includes it is also called absolute demonstration. But if it is not a cause for it in reality, but only in the mind, then the middle term is an effect of the judgment in reality or not, so it is the burhan inni. It is better to say: The demonstration, if its middle term other than the exceptional [conditional] is a cause for the judgment in reality, it is limmi; otherwise it is inni in both. And the inference from the existence of an effect of something to the fact that it has some cause, or its cause is the Necessary Existence, like our saying: Every body is composed, and every composed thing has a composer; and like our saying: Bodies are possible, and every possible thing has a cause that is the Necessary Existence, living, knowing, powerful, willing, and so on, is a limmi demonstration, and that is the truth. For the criterion in burhan limmi is the causality of the middle term and what is in its status for the affirmation of the major to the minor, not for its own [existence] in itself. And between them is a clear difference. The first is realized in what we are discussing, for the affirmation of the composer to the composed by means of the [Arabic letter] lam is an effect of the composition, even if its existence in itself is not an effect of it.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage defines two types of demonstration (burhan): limmi (why) and inni (that). The author, commenting on al-Sullam, clarifies that the middle term (al-awsat) must be a cause for the judgment in the mind. If that same middle term is also a cause for the judgment in external reality—meaning it explains why the major term is affirmed of the minor—then the demonstration is called limmi, or absolute demonstration. If the middle term is not a cause in reality but only in the mind (i.e., it is an effect or concomitant of the judgment), then it is inni. The author refines the definition by specifying that the middle term must be a cause in reality for the judgment itself, not necessarily for the existence of the major term in itself. He illustrates with two examples: (1) 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing has a composer'—here the middle term 'composed' is a cause for the judgment 'has a composer' in reality, so it is limmi. (2) 'Bodies are possible, and every possible thing has a cause that is the Necessary Existence'—again, the middle term 'possible' is a real cause for the judgment 'has a cause'. The author emphasizes that the criterion for limmi is the causality of the middle term for the judgment's truth, not for the existence of the subject or predicate themselves. This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where proofs from causality are used to establish the existence and attributes of God.

Key Terms
البرهان اللمي
البرهان الإني
الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what distinguishes a burhan limmi from a burhan inni?
A) The presence of a conditional syllogism.
B) The middle term being a cause for the judgment in reality.
C) The major term being a cause for the middle term.
D) The absence of any middle term.

Key Takeaway
The key to distinguishing burhan limmi from burhan inni is whether the middle term is a cause for the judgment in external reality or only in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining Burhan Limmi and Avoiding Common Mistakes
Definition of Burhan Limmi and correction of an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining feature of Burhan Limmi and distinguish correct from incorrect examples by examining the causal role of the middle term.

Core Idea
Burhan Limmi requires the middle term to be the cause of the attribution, not of the major term's own existence.

In this passage, the author defines Burhan Limmi as a demonstration where the middle term is the cause of the judgment (the major term's attribution to the minor). He then corrects a commonly cited example, showing why it fails to meet this criterion. We will dissect the definition and learn to spot such errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of demonstration
Find where the author defines Burhan Limmi and Burhan Inni. Note the condition: if the middle term is a cause of the judgment, it is limmi; otherwise inni.
2
Extract the causal condition
The author says 'the consideration in limmi demonstration is the causality of the middle term for the affirmation of the major for the minor, not for its affirmation in itself.' This means the middle term must cause the predication, not the existence of the major term.
3
Compare the two examples
The author gives two pairs of premises: (1) 'Every body is composed; every composed thing has a composer' and (2) 'Bodies are possible; everything possible has a cause, the Necessary of Existence...' He calls the second one truly limmi. Compare why the first is problematic.
4
Analyze the critique
The author explains that in the first example, the predicate is 'has a composer' not 'composer'. The cause is the composer, but the predicate is not the cause; rather the predicate is an effect. Therefore the middle term is not the cause of the major's attribution.
5
Formulate the correct condition
Summarize: For Burhan Limmi, the middle term must be the cause of the major term's being attributed to the minor term. The major term itself must be the effect or the predicate that the middle term causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم الأوسط سوى الاستثنائي علة للحكم في الواقع فلمي، وإلا فإني فيهما. والاستدلال بوجود المعلول لشيء على أن له علة ما، أو علته، واجب الوجود كقولنا كل جسم مؤلف، وكل مؤلف فله مؤلف، وكقولنا الأجسام ممكنة، وكل ممكن له سبب واجب الوجود حي عليم قدير مريد إلى غير ذلك برهان لمي، وهو الحق، فإن المعتبر في برهان اللّام علية الأوسط وما في حكمه لثبوت الأكبر للأصغر، لا لثبوته في نفسه، وبينهما بون بين، والأول متحقق فيما نحن فيه؛ فإن ثبوت المؤلف للمؤلف بواسطة اللام معلول للتأليف، وإن لم يكن وجوده في نفسه معلولا منه، والأظهر ما قال الشيخ. وأما المثال الآخر، وهو أن كل جسم مؤلف، وكل مؤلف فله مؤلف، فالمحمول فيه أن له مؤلفا لا المؤلف، فليس المحمول علة؛ لأن العلة هو المؤلف، لا أن له مؤلفا، وليس المؤلف هو الحد الأكبر، بل أن له مؤلفا، فهذا هو محمول على الأوسط الذي هو المؤلف؛ فإنك تعلم أن المؤلف يوصف بأن له مؤلف، ولا يقال إن المؤلف مؤلف ثم ذو المؤلف أولا للمؤلف ثم للمؤلف من الهيولى والصورة، سواء كان مقوما للمؤلف منهما أو لازما تابعا له، وإذا لم يكن ذو المؤلف إلا للمؤلف فهو لما تحت المؤلف بسبب المؤلف، على ما عرفت فيما سبق، فيكون اليقين حاصلا لعلة، ويكون المؤلف علة لوجود الحكم وإن كان جزء من ذي المؤلف هو المؤلف علة للمؤلف، اهـ.
Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum: The middle term, other than the exceptional, is a cause of the judgment in reality, so it is a priori (limmi), otherwise it is a posteriori (inni). And inferring from the existence of an effect of something that it has some cause, or its cause, the Necessary of Existence, such as our statement 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing has a composer', and our statement 'Bodies are possible, and everything possible has a cause, the Necessary of Existence, living, knowing, powerful, willing', and so on, is a limmi demonstration, and that is correct. For the consideration in limmi demonstration is the causality of the middle term and what is in its ruling for the affirmation of the major for the minor, not for its affirmation in itself, and between them is a clear difference. The first is realized in what we are discussing, for the affirmation of the authored thing for the author through the 'lām' is an effect of the composition, even if its existence in itself is not an effect of it. The most apparent is what the Shaykh said. As for the other example, that every body is composed and every composed thing has a composer, the predicate in it is that it has a composer, not the composer, so the predicate is not a cause, because the cause is the composer, not that it has a composer. The composer is not the major term, rather 'having a composer' is the major term. This is predicated on the middle term, which is the composer, for you know that the composer is described as having a composer, and it is not said that the composer is composed. Then 'the one having a composer' is [predicated] first on the composer, then on the composer from matter and form, whether it constitutes the composer from both or is a necessary consequent of it. And if 'the one having a composer' is only for the composer, it is for what is under the composer because of the composer, as you have known previously. So certainty is obtained from a cause, and the composer is a cause for the existence of the judgment, even if part of the one having the composer is the composer, a cause for the composer. End.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage defines Burhan Limmi (a priori demonstration) and distinguishes it from Burhan Inni (a posteriori demonstration). The author begins by stating that when the middle term is a cause of the judgment (the attribution of the major to the minor), the demonstration is called 'limmi'; otherwise it is 'inni'. He then provides examples of limmi demonstrations: (1) 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing has a composer' – but he later critiques this example, explaining that the predicate 'has a composer' is not the cause (the composer is the cause), so this example is actually not a proper limmi demonstration because the major term is 'having a composer', not 'composer'. The correct limmi demonstration should have the middle term as the cause of the major term. The author then explains that the causality of the middle term is what matters for affirming the major of the minor, not the middle term's own existence. He supports the Shaykh's (Ibn Sina's) view. The passage then discusses the example 'every composed thing has a composer' and clarifies that the subject 'composed thing' is the cause of the predicate 'has a composer', but the predicate itself is not the cause. The discussion emphasizes the logical precision required in identifying the middle term and its causal role.

Key Terms
برهان لمي
برهان إني
علية الأوسط

C) It must be an effect of the minor term.
D) It must be known by sense perception.

Key Takeaway
Burhan Limmi is defined by the middle term causing the predication of the major to the minor, not the major's own existence.

Resolving the Doubt about Burhan Inni
Author presents a doubt that Burhan Inni is invalid and resolves it by distinguishing universal and partial knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the doubt against Burhan Inni and explain how the author resolves it by distinguishing universal and partial knowledge.

Core Idea
Burhan Inni is valid for partial knowledge; universal knowledge requires the cause.

Ibn Sina's epistemology seems to undermine a posteriori demonstration (Burhan Inni) by claiming certain knowledge of caused things comes only from the cause. But is that a fatal objection? In this passage, our author presents a doubt, critiques a partial answer, and then offers a resolution that saves Burhan Inni for non-universal knowledge. Let's trace the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt and its premises
First, locate the doubt: Ibn Sina's view that certain knowledge of a caused thing comes only from its cause. From this, the doubt concludes that Burhan Inni (effect-to-cause inference) is impossible. Also, things without cause are either self-evident or unknowable, so inni is useless.
2
Reconstruct the argument against Burhan Inni
The doubt can be formalized: (1) If knowledge of an effect requires knowing its cause, then inni is invalid. (2) Ibn Sina holds that certain knowledge requires knowing the cause. (3) Therefore, inni is invalid. Also, for uncaused things, inni cannot apply, so all three types of inni fail.
This is a hypothetical syllogism leading to the demolition of inni.
3
Examine the proposed answer and its refutation
Some respond that Ibn Sina's ruling is only for necessary (self-evident) propositions, so inni remains for others. But the author objects: Ibn Sina's evidence does not differentiate necessary from contingent, and the same reasoning would also invalidate reductio arguments in geometry and physics, which is unacceptable.
The refutation shows the answer is too narrow and has absurd consequences.
4
Grasp the resolution and its distinction
The author resolves: Ibn Sina meant universal, permanent, necessary knowledge must be from the cause or self-evident. But partial, changeable knowledge can be obtained through Burhan Inni. This preserves inni for empirical sciences and most theological arguments.
The key is distinguishing universal (kulli) from partial (juz'i) sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا شك، وهو أن الشيخ ذهب إلى أن العلم اليقيني بما له سبب لا يحصل إلا من جهة السبب، فما لم يعلم سببه لا يحصل علمه اليقيني، فيلزم منه بطلان البرهان الإني الذي يستدل فيه من المعلول على العلة، أو من أحد معلولي علة على الآخر، وذهب أيضا إلى أن ما ليس له سبب إما أن يكون بينا بنفسه، فلا يحتاج في إثباته إلى البرهان، أو ميئوسا عن تبيانه بوجه يقيني، فلا يمكن تحصيله عن برهان فلا يفيد الإني الذي لا يكون أوسط علة ولا معلولا، وهل هذا إلا هدم قصر برهان الإني؟ إذ قد بطل أقسامه الثلاثة. وأجاب البعض بأن هذين الحكمين من الشيخ منحصران في الضرورية لا غير، فحينئذ لا يهدم قصر برهان الإني إلا فيها ولعله لا شناعة في التزام ذلك. وفيه أن دليل الشيخ غير فارق بين الضرورية وغيرها، على أنه ينهدم قصر القياسات الخلفية التي تقام في الهندسة على القضايا الضرورية، بل على ما ليس له سبب، وكذا المقامة في الطبيعي والفلسفة عليها، كما لا يخفى على الناظر فيها. وحله: لعل مراده أن العلوم الكلية، وهي اليقين الدائم الضروري غير الزائل ما دام العالم يبقى، بل إلى الأبد، إما أن يكون بينا من جهة السبب أو بينا بنفسه، فالعلوم الجزئية التي يمكن عليها الزوال جاز أن تكون...
And here is a doubt: that the Shaykh held that certain knowledge of something that has a cause cannot be obtained except from the side of the cause. So as long as its cause is not known, its certain knowledge is not obtained. This necessitates the invalidity of the inni demonstration, in which one infers from the effect to the cause, or from one effect of a cause to another. He also held that that which has no cause is either self-evident, so it does not need a demonstration for its proof, or hopeless of being explained in a certain way, so its acquisition through demonstration is impossible. So the inni demonstration, whose middle term is neither cause nor effect, is useless. Is this not demolishing the edifice of inni demonstration? For its three divisions have become invalid. Some responded that these two rulings of the Shaykh are restricted to necessary [propositions] only. Then the edifice of inni demonstration is only demolished in them, and perhaps there is no shame in accepting that. But it is said that the Shaykh's evidence does not differentiate between necessary and non-necessary, and moreover the edifice of reduction ad absurdum arguments used in geometry on necessary propositions, even on what has no cause, would be demolished, as well as those used in physics and philosophy, as is not hidden from the examiner. And its resolution: Perhaps he meant that universal sciences, which are the permanent certain knowledge, the necessary that does not cease as long as the knower remains, rather forever, are either evident from the side of the cause or evident in themselves. As for partial sciences, which can be subject to change, it is possible that they...
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage presents a doubt (shakk) regarding the validity of Burhan Inni (a posteriori demonstration) based on the views of Ibn Sina (the Shaykh). The doubt is that if Ibn Sina says certain knowledge of a caused thing can only come from its cause, then inferring from effect to cause (inni) is impossible. Moreover, if something has no cause, it is either self-evident or unknowable, so inni demonstration is useless. This would invalidate all three types of inni demonstration. Some attempted to answer by restricting Ibn Sina's claim to necessary propositions only, but the author objects that Ibn Sina's argument does not distinguish necessary from contingent, and moreover it would also invalidate important arguments like reductio in geometry and physics. The author then gives the resolution: Ibn Sina meant that universal, permanent, necessary knowledge must be either from the cause or self-evident, but partial knowledge (which can change) can be obtained through inni demonstration. The text ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: inni demonstration is valid for partial, non-necessary knowledge.

Key Terms
شك
برهان إني
علوم كلية
علوم جزئية

Checkpoint
According to the resolution of the doubt, what type of knowledge can be obtained through Burhan Inni?
A) Universal necessary knowledge.
B) Partial knowledge that can change.
C) Self-evident knowledge.
D) Knowledge of the Necessary Existent.

Key Takeaway
Burhan Inni is a valid demonstration for partial, changeable knowledge, even if universal necessary knowledge requires a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Using the resolution from this passage, explain why this objection fails and how the huduth argument remains valid as a Burhan Inni.

Distinguishing Cause of Knowledge from Cause of Existence
Author critiques Ibn Sina's claim that knowledge of a necessary judgment requires knowledge of its real-world cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the cause of existence and the cause of knowledge in necessary judgments, and evaluate Ibn Sina's claim that knowledge of a necessary judgment requires knowledge of its real-world cause, using the commentator's critique.

Core Idea
Certainty does not depend on knowing the real cause; demonstration suffices.

The commentator tackles a deep epistemological issue: does certain knowledge of a necessary judgment require knowing the cause that makes the relation necessary in reality? Ibn Sina argued yes; the commentator argues no, by distinguishing the cause of existence from the cause of knowledge (demonstration). In this lesson, we will unpack this critical exchange and see how it refines our understanding of demonstrative certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's claim
Find the core claim: that knowing a necessary judgment from an aspect other than its necessary cause yields only possibility knowledge, not necessity. This is the position the commentator targets.
Look for the statement: 'If we know from other than the aspect by which the judgment between them became necessary, then we have known from an aspect other than the aspect by which it cannot be otherwise.'
2
Understand the commentator's distinction
Recognize that the commentator distinguishes between two modes of realization for a necessary judgment: one in reality (independent of the knower) and one in the mind (intellectual existence). Their causes can be different.
The cause of intellectual existence is demonstration (burhan), while the cause of existence in reality may be unknown.
3
Analyze the commentator's objection
See how he argues that if demonstration is present, the intellect necessarily knows the judgment as necessary, even without knowledge of the real cause. Thus Ibn Sina's inference that knowledge from a non-causal aspect yields only possibility is invalid.
The commentator says: 'It is possible that the cause of knowledge of a thing is other than the cause of existence of that thing.'
4
Evaluate the strength of the critique
Consider whether the commentator successfully shows that certain knowledge can be obtained without knowledge of the real cause, and where Ibn Sina's reasoning might still hold.
Reflect on the distinction between self-evident and non-self-evident judgments mentioned at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَعْلُومَةً بِالضَّرُورَةِ، أَوْ بِالْبُرْهَانِ غَيْرِ اللَّم، فلم ينهدم قصر برهان الإن مطلقا، فَتَأَمَّلْ؛ فإنه موضع تأمل. واعلم أن علينا أن ننقل كلام الشيخ، وننظر أنه هل يتم، أم لا، فنقول: قال: ثم إذا كان لحمل محمول ما على موضوع دائما، أو سلبه عنه دائما، أو لحمله وسلبه في وقت بعينه يكونان فيه بالضرورة علة بتلك العلة صارت النسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول تلك النسبة، وذلك الموضوع والمحمول ليس لهما لولا ذلك السبب تلك النسبة بالوجوب، بل بالإمكان. فإذا علمنا من غير الوجه الذي صار الحكم ما بينهما ضروريا على تلك النسبة فقد علمنا من جهة غير الجهة التي لا يمكن أن لا يكون بتلك الحال، وذلك هو أن يعلم بوجه غير وجه السبب الذي يوجبه؛ لأن كل نسبة للمحمول إلى الموضوع المذكورين يفرض واقعة لا من الجهة التي توجبها العلة، فهي واقعة من جهة إمكان لا وجوب، فيكون قد علم أنه كذا وكذا، ولم يعلم أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا وكذا. ثم أورد أمثلة ليتبين هذا وهذا العبد غفر الله له لا يفهمه حق الفهم، لا أنه يعترض على أمثال الشيخ الرئيس؛ فإن للحكم الضروري الواقعي تحققا في الواقع من غير اعتبار المعتبر، وتحققا في الذهن بصورته عند الشيخ، أو بشبحه المبدأ للانكشاف عند غيره من البعض، وإذا حصل هو في الذهن بصورته أو حالة أخرى تكون مبدأ للانكشاف، انكشف عند العقل مبتة على اختلاف الرائين، ولهذين النحوين من التحقق علة ألبتة، وليس مما يلزم أن تكون علتهما واحدة بل ربما تكون عللهما مختلفة، وعلة الوجود العقلي البرهان، وإذا جاز اختلاف العلة فليجز أن يحصل علة الوجود العقلي، فحينئذ يجب وجود الحكم الضروري في العقل ألبتة، وإن جهل علة الوجود الواقعي فحينئذ يحكم العقل قطعا بأن هذا الحكم ضروري، فيحكم أيضا أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا. والحاصل أنه يجوز أن يكون علة علم الشيء غير علة الوجود لذلك الشيء، فإذا وجد علة العلم بالحكم الضروري وجد العلم به ضرورة، من غير توقف على العلم بعلة وجوده في الواقع، فيحكم العقل بأن هذا الحكم ضروري في الواقع، ولا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا فقوله «فإذا علمنا من غير الوجه الذي صار الحكم ما بينهما ضروريا فقد علمنا من جهة غير الجهة التي لا يمكن إلخ مسلّم، لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الحيثية بحيث يو. تحقق علمه في العقل، فيحكم العقل به، وبأنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا وكذا، فلا يصح تفريع قوله فيكون قد علم أنه كذا وكذا، ولم يعلم أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا وكذا. هذا، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال ثم الشيخ قال في الفصل الثاني لهذا الفصل: ثم لسائل أن يسأل ويقول إنه إذا لم يكن بين الموضوع والمحمول سبب في نفس الوجود، فكيف يتبن النسبة بينهما ببيان؟ فنقول: إذا كان بينا بنفسه فلا يحتاج إلى بيان، ويثبت فيه اليقين من جهة أن نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع لذات الموضوع، فذات الموضوع يجب مواصلتها للمحمول، وقد علمت المواصلة ووجوبها من حيث وجب، فالعلم الحاصل يقيني غير زائل. وإن لم يكن بينا بنفسه فلا يمكن ألبتة أن يقع به علم يقيني غير زائل؛
Known necessarily, or by demonstration other than the why-proof. So the fortress of the demonstration of certainty is not demolished at all. (So reflect); for it is a place of reflection. Know that it is upon us to transmit the words of the Shaykh, and examine whether it is sound or not. So we say: He said: Then, if there is a cause for the predication of a predicate of a subject always, or its negation always, or for its predication and negation at a specific time in which they are necessarily the cause, by that cause the relation between subject and predicate becomes that relation. And that subject and predicate, without that cause, do not have that relation by necessity, but by possibility. So if we know from other than the aspect by which the judgment between them became necessary with respect to that relation, then we have known from an aspect other than the aspect by which it cannot be otherwise. That is, knowing by an aspect other than the aspect of the cause that necessitates it; because every relation of the predicate to the mentioned subjects is assumed to occur not from the aspect that the cause necessitates it, so it occurs from an aspect of possibility, not necessity. Thus one would have known that it is such and such, but not known that it cannot be other than such and such. Then he gave examples to clarify this. And this servant (may God forgive him) does not understand it properly, not that he objects to the likes of the Chief Master. For the real necessary judgment has a realization in reality independent of the consideration of the observer, and a realization in the mind by its form according to the Shaykh, or by its phantom that is the principle of disclosure according to others. And if it occurs in the mind by its form or another state that is the principle of disclosure, it is disclosed to the intellect categorically, despite the difference of opinions. And for these two modes of realization there is a cause absolutely, and it is not necessary that their cause be one, but perhaps their causes are different. And the cause of intellectual existence is demonstration. And if difference of cause is possible, then let it be possible that the cause of intellectual existence is obtained, then at that moment the existence of the necessary judgment in the intellect becomes necessary absolutely, even if the cause of real existence is unknown. Then the intellect judges decisively that this judgment is necessary, and also judges that it cannot be otherwise. The upshot is that it is possible that the cause of knowledge of a thing is other than the cause of existence of that thing. So if the cause of knowledge of the necessary judgment exists, knowledge of it exists necessarily, without being dependent on knowledge of the cause of its existence in reality. So the intellect judges that this judgment is necessary in reality, and cannot be otherwise. So his statement: 'If we know from other than the aspect by which the judgment between them became necessary, then we have known from an aspect other than the aspect by which it cannot... etc.' is granted, but why is it not permissible that that consideration be such that it results in knowledge of it in the intellect, so the intellect judges it and judges that it cannot be otherwise? Thus his deduction in saying: 'Then one would have known that it is such and such, but not known that it cannot be other than such and such' is not valid. This, and God knows best the reality of the situation. Then the Shaykh said in the second chapter of this chapter: Then if an asker asks and says: If there is no cause between subject and predicate in the very existence, how can the relation between them be established by a proof? We say: If it is self-evident, it does not need a proof, and certainty is established in it from the aspect that the relation of the predicate to the subject is due to the essence of the subject, so the essence of the subject necessitates its connection to the predicate, and the connection and its necessity have been known from the aspect from which it is necessary, so the obtained knowledge is certain and not subject to cessation. And if it is not self-evident, it is absolutely impossible that certain and permanent knowledge be obtained through it.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on a position attributed to Ibn Sina (the Shaykh) regarding the nature of necessary judgments and the cause of certainty. The commentator begins by acknowledging that the discussion is deep and requires reflection. He then quotes Ibn Sina's statement: if a subject-predicate relation is always necessary (or always negated, or necessary at a specific time) due to a cause, then that relation holds by necessity only through that cause. Without the cause, the relation is merely possible. Ibn Sina then claims that if one knows the relation from an aspect other than the necessary cause, one knows that it is the case but does not know that it cannot be otherwise—i.e., one lacks certainty. The commentator argues that this is not necessarily correct. He explains that a necessary judgment has two modes of realization: one in reality (independent of the knower) and one in the mind (as a form or principle of disclosure). These can have different causes. The cause of the mental realization (i.e., knowledge) can be a demonstration (burhan), which may be independent of the cause of the real relation. If the mental cause (demonstration) is present, the intellect necessarily obtains certain knowledge of the judgment, even if the real cause is unknown. The intellect then judges that the judgment is necessary in reality. The commentator concludes that Ibn Sina's reasoning that knowing from a non-causal aspect only yields possibility is invalid because the mental consideration (the demonstration) itself can produce certain knowledge. He then adds a second quote from Ibn Sina: if there is no real cause between subject and predicate, how can the relation be proven? The response: if the relation is self-evident, it does not need proof; certainty arises from the essence of the subject itself. If not self-evident, certainty cannot be attained. The commentator appears to accept this second part. The overall discussion refines the epistemology of necessary judgments, distinguishing between the cause of existence and the cause of knowledge, and affirming that certain knowledge can be attained through demonstration even when the real cause is unknown.

الحكم الضروري
البرهان
الوجود العقلي

B) They can be different; the cause of knowledge can be demonstration even if the cause of existence is unknown.

D) The cause of knowledge must be the same as the cause of existence for certainty.

Key Takeaway
The cause of knowledge (demonstration) can be distinct from the cause of existence, so certain knowledge of necessary judgments can be obtained through demonstration without knowing the real cause.

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
15 min
Why Induction Cannot Yield Certainty: A Critique and Defense of Syllogism
Author critiques the claim that induction yields certain knowledge and argues syllogism is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical argument against induction as a source of certain knowledge and evaluate the commentator's critique of that argument.

Core Idea
Induction fails because sense perception is particular; only syllogism with universal premises yields certainty.

This passage from a classical commentary on logic presents a rigorous argument that induction (istiqra') cannot produce certain and universal knowledge. The commentator then raises a critical objection against the assumption that knowledge of a caused thing must come from its cause. By working through this layered discussion, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate epistemological claims in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Locate the claim that induction is insufficient for certain knowledge. The author argues that if we assume no cause for a predication, the middle term cannot yield certainty.
Focus on the phrase 'لأنا إذا جعلنا المتوسط ما ليس بسبب'.
2
Follow the original argument's structure
The author examines two possibilities: either the relation of predicate to subject particulars is self-evident or it is caused. Trace how each leads to a problem—self-evident via sense fails, via intellect is impossible; caused leads back to requiring a cause, contradicting the assumption.
3
Identify the commentator's objection
The commentator objects that the whole argument assumes 'knowledge of what has a cause is only from the cause', which he claims to have refuted. He also rejects the claim that sense cannot give universal knowledge.
Look for 'مبني على أن العلم بذي السبب لا يكون إلا من جهة السبب'.
4
Assess the commentator's alternative
The commentator points out that a certain demonstration (burhan 'inn) can be formed without a causal middle term if both premises are universal and necessary. This shows that syllogism can yield certainty even without a causal relation.
See 'فينعقد برهان يقيني، ويكون البرهان برهان "إن" ليس برهان "لم"'.
5
Evaluate the overall dialectic
Consider which side has the stronger case. The original argument against induction is thorough, but the commentator exposes a hidden assumption. This reflects the living tradition of logical critique within Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنا إذا جعلنا المتوسط ما ليس بسبب لم يكن أن يطلب به هذا العلم اليقيني، وإن جعلناه ما هو سبب فقد وسطنا سببا، وهذا محال؛ إذ فرضنا أنه لا سبب. ويشبه أن يكون أمثال هذه بينة بنفسها كلها، أو يكون بيانها بالاستقراء، إلا أنه لا يخلو إذا تبين بالاستقراء من أحد أمرين، وذلك لأنه إما أن يكون وجود نسبة المحمول إلى جزئيات الموضوع بينا بنفسه بلا سبب؛ إذ إنما يتبين الاستقراء بهذا النوع، وإما أن يكون وجود نسبة المحمول إلى جزئيات الموضوع في نفسه بسبب، فإن كان بينا بنفسه في كل واحد منها، فإما أن يكون البيان بالحس، وذلك لا يوجب الدوام، ولا يرفع إمكان الزوال، فلا يكون من تلك المقدمات يقين. وإما أن يكون بالعقل، وهذا القسم غير جائز؛ لأن هذا المحمول لا يجوز أن يكون ذاتيا، بمعنى المقوم؛ فإنا سنبين بعد ذلك أن الذاتي بمعنى المقوم غير مطلوب في الحقيقة وجوده لما هو ذاتي له بين. وإما أن يكون عرضيا، ولا شك أن الأعراض اللازمة لكلي يقال على الجزئيات؛ إذ حمله على الكل فيكون هذا العرض لازما لشيء من المعاني الذاتية لجزئيات النوع؛ فإن العرض الذي هذه صفته هذا شأنه، وإذا كان كذلك كان حمله على كل جزئي لأجل معنى موجود له ولغيره من الجزئيات، فيكون ذلك - أي الذاتي - سببا عاما لوجود هذا العرض في الجزئيات، وفرضناه بلا سبب. وإذا علم من غير جهة ذلك السبب لم يكن ذلك بعلم ضروري ولا يقين، فضلا عن علم بين بنفسه. وأما إذا كان حال الموضوع عند جزئيات الموضوع غير بين بنفسه، بل يتبين ببيان، فذلك البيان إما أن يكون بيانا لا يوجب في كل منها اليقين الحقيقي الذي يقصده، فكيف يوقع ما ليس يقينا اليقين الحقيقي الكلي؟ وإما أن يكون بيانا بسبب، فيوجب اليقين الحقيقي في كل واحد منها، فيجب أن يتفق في السبب، كما قلنا، فيجب كون وجود السبب للمعنى الكلي أولا. وإذا كان السبب لا ينفع هذا المعنى الكلي فليس أيضا بنافع في الجزئي، وإذا نفع في الكلي فيكون المفيد والنافع هو القياس عند ذلك، لا الاستقراء. وإما أن يكون لا بسبب هناك ألبتة، فيكون إما أن يكون بينا بنفسه، وذلك مما قد أبطلت، وإما بالاستقراء وهذا مما يذهب بلا وقوف، فقد بان أن ما لا سبب لنسبة المحمول إما أن يتبين بنفسه، وإما أن لا يبين بيانا يقينيا بوجه قياسي. انتهى مع التلخيص.

ولا يخفى أن هذا مع طوله مبني على أن العلم بذي السبب لا يكون إلا من جهة السبب، وقد أبطلناه، وعلى أن الحس لا يفيد علما كليا، وقد عرفت طريقا به يعطي العلم الكلي، ومع ذلك يرد عليه عدم إفادة القياسات الخلفية اليقين الكلي، ولا يصدق باستحالة المستحيلات كاستحالة غير المتناهي، والجوهر الفرد والخلاء والمثلث الذي زواياه ثلث قوائم إلى غير ذلك، وقوله "لأنا إذا جعلنا المتوسط ما ليس بسبب إلخ" مجرد دعوى من غير برهان، فلا يقبله الخصم. والعجب من الشيخ قال مع قوله هذا في الفصل المتقدم، فإن كان الأكبر للأصغر لا بسبب، بل لذاته، لكنه ليس بين الوجود له، والأوسط كذلك للأصغر ثم الأكبر بين الوجود للأوسط، فينعقد برهان يقيني، ويكون البرهان برهان "إن" ليس برهان "لم"، وإنما كان يقينا لأن المقدمتين كليتان واجبتان.
Because if we make the middle term not a cause, then this certain knowledge cannot be sought by it. And if we make it a cause, then we have introduced a cause, and this is impossible since we assumed there is no cause. And it seems that such things are either self-evident in themselves, or their explanation is by induction, but it does not avoid, when explained by induction, one of two matters: either the existence of the relation of the predicate to the particulars of the subject is self-evident without a cause—for induction is only made clear by this type—or the existence of the relation of the predicate to the particulars of the subject is in itself due to a cause. If it is self-evident in each one of them, then the explanation is either by sense, and that does not entail permanence nor remove the possibility of change, so it does not yield certainty among those premises. Or it is by intellect, and this division is impermissible because this predicate cannot be essential (dhati) in the sense of constitutive (muqawwim); for we will show later that the essential in the sense of constitutive is not required in reality for its existence for what is essential to it is evident. Or it is accidental ('aradi), and there is no doubt that the necessary accidents of a universal are predicated of particulars; since its predication on the whole means this accident is necessary for some essential meanings of the particulars of the species. For an accident with this description, if it is so, then its predication on every particular is due to a meaning existing for it and for other particulars, so that meaning—i.e., the essential—is a general cause for the existence of this accident in the particulars, and we assumed it without a cause. And if it is known from other than that cause, it would not be necessary knowledge nor certainty, let alone self-evident knowledge.

And as for if the state of the subject with respect to the particulars of the subject is not self-evident, but is known by a proof, then that proof either does not yield in each of them the true certainty that is intended—so how can that which is not certain bring about true universal certainty?—or it is a proof by cause, and it yields true certainty in each one of them, so it must agree in the cause, as we said, so the existence of the cause for the universal meaning must be prior. And if the cause does not benefit this universal meaning, then it is also not beneficial for the particular; and if it benefits the universal, then the beneficial and useful is the syllogism then, not induction. Or there is no cause at all, then it is either self-evident—and that is something we have already refuted—or by induction, and this goes away without stopping. So it has become clear that that which has no cause for the predication of the predicate is either known by itself, or is not known by a certain proof in any syllogistic way. End with summary.

And it is not hidden that this, despite its length, is based on the premise that knowledge of that which has a cause is only from the cause, and we have refuted that, and that sense does not give universal knowledge, and you have known a way by which universal knowledge is given. Nevertheless, it is objected that the hypothetical syllogisms do not yield universal certainty, and they do not affirm the impossibility of impossibilities such as the impossibility of the infinite, the atom, the void, and a triangle whose angles are one and a half right angles, etc. And his statement 'Because if we make the middle term not a cause etc.' is a mere claim without proof, so the opponent does not accept it. And it is surprising from the Shaykh that he said along with this in the previous chapter: if the major term is for the minor term not by cause but by itself, yet its existence for it is not evident, and the middle term is likewise for the minor, then the major is evident in existence for the middle, then a certain demonstration is formed, and it is a demonstration 'inn' not demonstration 'limma', and it is certain only because both premises are universal and necessary.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the role of induction (istiqra') in producing certain knowledge. The commentator first presents an argument (likely from the original text of Sullam al-Ulum or a prior commentary) that induction cannot yield certainty, and then he critiques that argument. The structure is as follows:

1. The original argument begins by examining the middle term: if the middle term is not a cause, then it cannot produce certain knowledge; if it is a cause, then we have assumed there is no cause, leading to contradiction. So the argument proceeds to consider two cases for the relation between predicate and subject particulars: either it is self-evident without a cause, or it is due to a cause.

2. If the relation is self-evident in each particular, the explanation is either by sense or by intellect. Sense perception does not guarantee permanence or universality, so it cannot yield certainty. Intellect cannot make the predicate essential in the constitutive sense because that would be self-evident and not require demonstration. The remaining option is accidental (aradi). But accidental attributes that are necessary to a universal are predicated on particulars due to an underlying essential meaning that is a common cause. If we assume no cause, then knowledge of that accident cannot be necessary or certain.

3. If the relation is not self-evident but requires proof, the proof either does not yield true certainty in each particular (so it cannot produce universal certainty) or it does yield certainty through a cause. In the latter case, the cause must be universal and prior, making the syllogism (qiyas) the beneficial method, not induction. If there is no cause at all, the knowledge must be self-evident (which has been refuted) or by induction (which is unstable). Hence, anything without a cause for predication is either known by itself or not known with certainty by any syllogistic means.

4. The commentator then criticizes this entire argument. He points out that it assumes knowledge of something with a cause can only come from the cause (a premise he claims to have refuted) and that sense does not give universal knowledge (though he suggests there is a way it can). He also objects that hypothetical syllogisms (qiyasat khalfiya) do not yield universal certainty, and they fail to prove the impossibility of certain things like the infinite, the atom, the void, and a triangle with angles of one and a half right angles. The original claim that 'if we make the middle term not a cause...' is dismissed as a mere assertion without proof. Finally, the commentator notes a contradiction in the Shaykh's own earlier chapter: a demonstration of 'inn' (that something is) can be certain without a causal middle term, provided both premises are universal and necessary.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
القياس
الذاتي
العرضي
برهان إن

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, the original argument against induction is flawed because it assumes which premise?
A) Knowledge of something with a cause must come from the cause.
B) Induction is the only source of knowledge.
C) Syllogism cannot produce certainty.
D) All premises are universal and necessary.

Key Takeaway
Induction cannot yield universal certainty on its own; syllogistic demonstration, even without a causal middle term, can provide certain knowledge if premises are universal and necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a valid syllogism on an Islamic legal topic (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is forbidden') and then explain why induction alone cannot establish the universal premise 'every intoxicant is forbidden' with certainty. Refer to the commentator's critique.
